The Broken Refutation (Naqd Maksur): Definition and Ruling
Defining naqd maksur and its effect on composite 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-naqd al-maksūr and apply its ruling to invalidate a composite 'illah.

Core Idea
A broken refutation cancels some parts of a composite 'illah and then refutes the remainder, invalidating the 'illah.

This lesson introduces al-naqd al-maksūr, a type of refutation in qiyas. The Hanafi school accepts it as a valid refutation that invalidates a composite 'illah unless a preventer exists. The example of marriage to an unseen woman versus sale of an absent item illustrates how it works. Understanding this concept sharpens your ability to dismantle weak 'illal in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite 'illah
Break the opponent's claimed 'illah into its component parts. For example, in the sale of an absent item, the 'illah might be 'ignorance + being a sale'. Recognize that these parts combine to form a composite 'illah.
2
Distinguish suitable and incidental parts
Determine which parts contribute to the suitability (munāsabah) and which are incidental (ṭardī). In the example, 'ignorance' is suitable for invalidity, while 'being a sale' is incidental because other contracts like marriage also involve ignorance but are valid.
3
Cancel the incidental parts
Argue that the incidental parts do not contribute to the 'illah's suitability. Cancel them, leaving only the suitable part.
4
Refute the remaining part
Provide a counterexample that contradicts the remaining part. For 'ignorance', cite marriage to an unseen woman: it is valid despite ignorance, so ignorance alone cannot be the 'illah for invalidity.
5
Conclude invalidation of the 'illah
Since the remaining part is refuted, the original composite 'illah is invalid. The refutation is accepted unless a preventer appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض المكسور وهو نقض بعض العلة مع إلغاء الباقي من الأجزاء بأن تكون العلة المدعاة مركبة من أجزاء، فيبين كفاية البعض من الأجزاء في المناسبة، ويلغي الباقي في العلية ثم ينقض الجزء المناسب (فالمختار أنه وارد) على العلة، وتبطل به العلية إلا عند ظهور مانع (وعليه الأكثر، خلافاً لشرذمة) قليلة (ذاهبين إلى أن الوصف ولو كان (طردياً دافع للنقض (مثاله) قول الشافعي رضي الله عنه في بيع الغائب بيع مجهول الصفة فلا يصح كبيع عبد بلا تعيين فينقض الحنفي بتزوج من لم يرها فإنه تزوج صحيح مع وجود الجهالة (بناء على أن الجهالة مستقلة بالمناسبة) في إفساد العقد لإفضائه إلى المنازعة والبغضاء، فلو كان الوصف المدعي علة لكان من الجهالة فقط (وكونه مبيعاً) وصف (طردي) لا دخل له ثم في القياس المذكور شيء آخر قوي هو أن الجهالة إنما تفسد الرضا من المشتري فيكون موكولاً إلى رضاه، ويثبت الخيار لا أنه يفسد، وهذا بخلاف النكاح فإنه يصح مع الهزل أيضاً فلا يتوقف إلا على الرضا بالتكلم بالسبب وقد وجد فنفذ (لنا : العلة) ههنا أما (المجموع أو الباقي بعد الإلغاء والأوّل) باطل لإلغاء الملغي) من الأجزاء فتعين الباقي للعلة والباقي منقوض) فيقبل هذا النقض فافهم
And as for the broken refutation (naqd maksur): it is refuting part of the 'illah while canceling the rest of the parts, such that the claimed 'illah is composed of parts. Then it is shown that some parts are sufficient for suitability, and the rest are canceled from being part of the 'illah. Then the suitable part is refuted. The chosen position is that it is valid against the 'illah and the 'illah is invalidated by it, unless a preventer appears. The majority hold this, contrary to a small group who go to the extent that a description, even if it is incidental (ṭardī), prevents the refutation. Its example is al-Shafi'i's statement concerning the sale of an absent item: it is a sale of an unknown description and is invalid, like the sale of a slave without specification. The Hanafi refutes this by the marriage of a woman not seen: it is valid despite the presence of ignorance (jahālah). This is based on the idea that ignorance is independently suitable to invalidate a contract because it leads to dispute and rancor. Thus, if the claimed description were the 'illah, it would be from ignorance alone, and being a sold item is a incidental description (ṭardī) that has no bearing. Moreover, in the mentioned analogy, there is another strong point: ignorance only spoils the consent of the buyer, and thus it is left to his consent, and an option is established, not that it invalidates. This is contrary to marriage, for it is valid even with jest, so it only depends on consent through the spoken cause, and that has occurred, so it is executed. For us: the 'illah here is either the composite or the remainder after cancellation. The first is invalid because the canceled parts are canceled, so the remainder is determined as the 'illah, and the remainder is refuted. Therefore, this refutation is accepted. So understand.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines naqd maksur, a type of refutation where a composite 'illah is dismantled: some parts are shown to be sufficient and kept, others are canceled, and then the kept part is refuted. The majority hold that this invalidates the 'illah unless there is a preventer. The example contrasts al-Shafi'i's ruling on selling an unseen item (invalid due to ignorance) with the Hanafi ruling on marriage to an unseen woman (valid despite ignorance). The Hanafi argues that ignorance is not the 'illah for invalidity in both cases; rather, in sales, ignorance affects consent and gives an option, while in marriage, consent is through the spoken cause alone.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور (al-naqd al-maksūr): A refutation that cancels some parts of a composite 'illah and then refutes the remaining part.
العلة المركبة (al-ʿillah al-murakkabah): A composite 'illah consisting of multiple parts.
طردي (ṭardī): An incidental description that does not contribute to the suitability of the 'illah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on naqd maksur?
A) It does not invalidate the 'illah
B) It invalidates the 'illah unless there is a preventer
C) It is only accepted in sales
D) It is rejected entirely

Key Takeaway
Naqd maksur is a valid refutation of composite 'illah in Hanafi usul, invalidating the 'illah unless a preventer exists.

The Validity of Broken Refutation (Naqd Maqsūr) in Usul al-Fiqh
Explaining and defending the concept of broken refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a broken refutation (naqd maqsūr) and apply the Hanafi position on its validity in evaluating composite legal causes.

Core Idea
A broken refutation is valid: nullifying incidental parts leaves the core cause exposed to refutation.

When a legal cause is composed of multiple parts, critics sometimes attack only part of it, dismissing the rest as irrelevant. This is called a broken refutation. Does such a partial critique destroy the cause? The majority, including the Hanafi school, says yes—unless a preventing factor appears. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and illustrates it with the classic example of the sale of an unseen object versus marriage to an unseen woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify a composite cause
A composite cause has multiple parts, some of which may be suitable for the ruling and others merely incidental. The claimed cause for the invalidity of selling an unseen object is 'ignorance of description' and the fact that it is a 'sold item'. The latter is incidental.
The Hanafi argument treats the 'sold item' part as incidental (wasf tardī).
2
Perform a broken refutation
Show that only part of the cause is relevant (e.g., ignorance alone suffices to cause invalidity), then nullify the other part as having no bearing. Then refute the remaining part by showing a case where it exists but the ruling does not follow.
The Hanafi uses marriage to an unseen woman: ignorance exists but the contract is valid.
3
Evaluate the refutation's validity
According to the majority, the broken refutation is accepted; it destroys the claimed cause unless there is a preventer (e.g., a text blocking the qiyas). The small faction argues that the incidental part repels the refutation.
The text says: 'فالمختار أنه وارد على العلة، وتبطل به العلية إلا عند ظهور مانع'.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete dispute
In the sale of an unseen object, Shafi‘i claims invalidity by analogy to selling an unspecified slave. The Hanafi counter‐example of marriage (valid despite ignorance) shows that ignorance alone does not always invalidate; it only gives an option. Hence the claimed cause fails.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Shafi‘i declares sale invalid; Hanafi holds it valid with khiyār al-ru’yah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض المكسور وهو نقض بعض العلة مع إلغاء الباقي من الأجزاء بأن تكون العلة المدعاة مركبة من أجزاء، فيبين كفاية البعض من الأجزاء في المناسبة، ويلغي الباقي في العلية ثم ينقض الجزء المناسب (فالمختار أنه وارد) على العلة، وتبطل به العلية إلا عند ظهور مانع (وعليه الأكثر، خلافاً لشرذمة) قليلة (ذاهبين إلى أن الوصف ولو كان (طردياً دافع للنقض (مثاله) قول الشافعي رضي الله عنه في بيع الغائب بيع مجهول الصفة فلا يصح كبيع عبد بلا تعيين فينقض الحنفي بتزوج من لم يرها فإنه تزوج صحيح مع وجود الجهالة (بناء على أن الجهالة مستقلة بالمناسبة) في إفساد العقد لإفضائه إلى المنازعة والبغضاء، فلو كان الوصف المدعي علة لكان من الجهالة فقط (وكونه مبيعاً) وصف (طردي) لا دخل له ثم في القياس المذكور شيء آخر قوي هو أن الجهالة إنما تفسد الرضا من المشتري فيكون موكولاً إلى رضاه، ويثبت الخيار لا أنه يفسد، وهذا بخلاف النكاح فإنه يصح مع الهزل أيضاً فلا يتوقف إلا على الرضا بالتكلم بالسبب وقد وجد فنفذ (لنا : العلة) ههنا أما (المجموع أو الباقي بعد الإلغاء والأوّل) باطل لإلغاء الملغي) من الأجزاء فتعين الباقي للعلة والباقي منقوض) فيقبل هذا النقض فافهم
As for the broken refutation, it is the refutation of part of the cause while nullifying the remainder of the parts, such that the claimed cause is composed of parts, and it is shown that some of the parts are sufficient in suitability, and the remainder are nullified in causality, then the suitable part is refuted. The chosen view is that it is valid against the cause, and the causality is invalidated by it unless a preventer appears. This is the view of the majority, contrary to a small faction who hold that the description, even if it is merely incidental, repels the refutation. An example is the saying of al-Shafi‘i, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding the sale of an absent object: it is a sale of something unknown in description, so it is invalid, like selling a slave without specification. The Hanafi refutes this by the marriage of a woman one has not seen, for such a marriage is valid despite the presence of ignorance, based on the fact that ignorance is independently suitable in nullifying the contract because it leads to dispute and enmity. If the claimed description were the cause, it would be due to ignorance alone; and being a sold item is an incidental description with no bearing. Then in the aforementioned analogy there is another strong point: ignorance only spoils the consent of the buyer, so it is left to his consent, and the option is established, not that it spoils. This is contrary to marriage, for it is valid even with jest, so it depends only on consent to the utterance of the cause, and that has occurred, so it is executed. For us: the cause here is either the whole or the remainder after nullification. The first is invalid because the nullified parts are nullified, so the remainder is determined as the cause, and the remainder is refuted, so this refutation is accepted. So understand.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of 'broken refutation' (naqd maqsūr) in legal reasoning. It is a type of criticism leveled against a claimed composite cause (i.e., a cause consisting of multiple parts). The critic shows that only some of the parts are actually suitable for the ruling, and dismisses the others as irrelevant. Then he attacks the remaining part, arguing that it does not satisfy the conditions for a valid cause. The majority of scholars hold that such a refutation is valid and destroys the claimed cause, unless there is some preventing factor. A small minority argue that even if the incidental description (the dismissed part) is not suitable, it still protects the cause from refutation. The author provides the example of Imam al-Shafi‘i's argument regarding the sale of an unseen object: he claims it is invalid because it involves ignorance, analogous to selling an unspecified slave. The Hanafi critic responds by citing marriage to an unseen woman, which is valid despite ignorance. The Hanafi rebuttal is that ignorance is independently suitable to invalidate the contract (as it leads to disputes), but in this context the ignorance is left to the consent of the buyer and the option of rescission is given; it does not cause absolute invalidity. In marriage, the contract is valid even if there is some ignorance because it depends on the utterance of the proposal. The author concludes that the correct position is that the cause is either the entire set or the remaining part after nullification; the first is invalid because the nullified parts are indeed nullified, so the cause is the remaining part, which is refuted. Thus, broken refutation is accepted.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور (al-naqd al-maqsūr): A refutation that challenges part of a composite cause while nullifying the other parts, showing that only the challenged part is relevant.
الوصف الطردي (al-wasf al-ṭardī): An incidental description that does not contribute to the suitability of the cause for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the validity of broken refutation (naqd maqsūr)?
A) It is invalid because the incidental description repels the refutation.
B) It is valid, and the claimed cause is destroyed unless a preventer appears.
C) It is valid only if the refuted part is the only suitable part.
D) It is invalid in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Broken refutation is accepted in Hanafi usul: nullifying incidental parts leaves the core cause, which can be refuted, thereby invalidating the claimed cause.

The Condition of Inverse Entailment (In‘ikās) and Multiple Causes
Debate on inverse entailment and multiple causes in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on multiple independent causes and apply it to evaluate the condition of inverse entailment (in‘ikās) in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit multiple causes for a ruling, so inverse entailment is not a required condition.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: must a legal cause be such that the ruling is always absent when the cause is absent? That condition is called in‘ikās, and it is tied to the question of whether a ruling can have multiple independent causes. The Hanafi school, based on actual legal examples, allows multiple causes and thus rejects in‘ikās as a necessary condition. We will trace the arguments, objections, and responses that shape this debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of in‘ikās
Define in‘ikās: a cause is valid only if whenever the cause is absent, the ruling is also absent. This is based on the assumption that a ruling cannot have more than one cause.
This condition is defended by some usulis who argue that if multiple causes exist, the absence of one does not guarantee absence of the ruling.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its evidence
The Hanafi school permits multiple independent causes for a single ruling. They cite positive examples from fiqh: various impurities (urine, stool, etc.) each independently necessitate ritual impurity; apostasy and retaliation each independently cause killing. Thus, in‘ikās is not a necessary condition.
The majority of scholars (jumhūr) agree with the Hanafi view.
3
Analyze the objections and responses
Opponents argue that if one cause is absent, the ruling might still be present due to another cause, undermining legal reasoning. The Hanafi response distinguishes between aspects: the same act may have different legal aspects (ḥaqq Allāh vs. ḥaqq al-‘abd), leading to multiple rulings. For example, killing for apostasy is a right of Allah, while retaliation is a right of the victim’s family.
Al-Amidi restricts the dispute to the individual particular, not the type.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafis do not require in‘ikās when assessing the validity of an extracted cause. A cause can still be valid even if the ruling is found in its absence, as long as another cause is present. This affects how legal analogies (qiyas) are evaluated.
The dispute does not mean Hanafis reject the general principle that a ruling must have a cause; rather, they allow for multiple causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها) أي من شرائط العلة (الانعكاس) عند البعض (وهو انتفاؤه) أي الحكم ( عند انتفائها أي العلة وذلك مبني على منع التعليل بعلتين كل منهما (مستقل بالاقتضاء) للحكم إذ لا يكون الحكم بلا باعث) عليه (تفضلا) منه سبحانه علينا كما هو مذهبنا معشر أهل السنة القامعين للبدعة (أو وجوباً) عليه تعالى كما عند المعتزلة وإذا لم يكن بلا باعث فينفي عند انتفاء الباعث لعدم وجود باعث آخر (والحق عند الجمهور جوازه أي جواز (وبالعكس) أي ينتفي القتل بالردة ويبقى الآخر بالإسلام. قلنا) تعدد الأحكام لا تعدداً في الذات فإن الذات الواحدة ربما تضاف إلى شيئين فتختلف بالاعتبار فتختلف الأحكام ولو تعددت لتعددت بالإضافة إلى الأدلة إذ ليس ما به (الاختلاف ههنا (إلا) ذلك، واللازم باطل، لأن الإضافات لا توجب تعدداً في ذات المضاف) إليه، وللخصم أن لا يقنع عليه، بل يقول : تعدد الإضافة إلى العلة يجوز أن يستلزم تعدد المضاف وإن لم يستلزم الإضافات الأخر، بل هذا أول المسألة فتدبر قال شارح «المختصر » : يوجب هو لو أوجب الإضافة إلى العلل تعدداً في الذات لأمكن بقاء أحد الحدثين انتفاء الآخر فيمكن مع بقاء حدث البول مع انتفاء حدث الغائط ورد بأن التعدد لا يوجب إمكان البقاء لجواز كونهما متلازمين قال المصنف لو تعدد الذات لتعدد الوجود فتعدد الإعدام فكان يتصور عند العقل انتفاء أحدهما مع بقاء الآخر، وإن كان بينهما تلازم في الواقع، وهذا هو مراد شارح المختصر، وللسائل أن لا يقنع عليه ويقول : إن أراد تصور انتفاء أحدهما بقاء الآخر مع تصوراً مطابقاً فاللزوم ممنوع ، وإن أراد انفكاك أحد التصورين عن الآخر فبطلانه ممنوع (وما قيل القتل بالردة حق الله تعالى والقصاص حق العبد وهما متغايران (فأقول : مدفوع بأن ذلك) التغاير (معتبر في جانب العلة والحكم المعلول هو القتل (ولذلك كان الحكمة في أحدهما) القتل بالردة حفظ الدين وفي الآخر هو القصاص (حفظ) (النفس وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن القتل فعل قائم بالقاتل متعلق بالمقتول ولا شك أن القتل بالردة فعل الإمام أو ما يقول مقامه، والقتل بالقصاص فعل الولي أو ما يقوم مقامه والأوّل ،واجب والثاني مباح، فهما متغايران قطعاً، وأما ما على المقتول فإنما هو تسليم نفسه إلى الأولياء إن طلبوا قتله، فليس ههنا اتحاد أصلاً، ولعله هو الذي رامه هذا القائل (واعترض الآمدي بأن النزاع) إنما هو (في الواحد بالشخص والمخالف يمنعه في الصورة المذكورة بل المعلول في تلك الصورة واحد (بالنوع، أقول : المفروض) في الصورة المذكورة (التوارد) للعلل (معاً) كما إذا بال ورعف معاً (فلو كان هناك اتحاد بالنوع لا بالشخص) لكان بإزاء كل علة معلول شخص مغاير بالشخص لمعلول بإزاء أخرى، ولزم اجتماع (المثلين وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن هذا اللزوم إنما يتم لو كان لكل علة معلول، بل المؤثر ههنا القدر المشترك بين العلل في واحد شخصي، فليس ههنا مثلان فافهم
And among them, i.e., among the conditions of the cause, is inverse entailment according to some. It is the negation of the ruling upon the negation of the cause. This is based on preventing the explanation by two causes each of which is independent in necessitating the ruling, for a ruling cannot be without a cause—either as a favor from Him, exalted, upon us, as is our doctrine, the community of Ahl al-Sunnah who suppress innovation, or as an obligation upon Him, exalted, as according to the Mu‘tazilah. When it is not without a cause, then it is negated upon the negation of the cause due to the absence of another cause. The truth according to the majority is the permissibility of explanation by more than one cause; thus inverse entailment is not required. Hence, Imam Fakhr al-Islam counted reasoning by negation to prove the absence of the ruling among the corrupt methods. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani permits it in the case of a textual cause only, not an extracted one. And it is said the opposite: he permits multiple extracted causes but not textual ones. The Imam said: multiple causes are permitted intellectually but prevented legally. For us: if multiple causes were not permitted, intellectually or legally, they would not occur, but they have occurred. For urine, stool, prostatic fluid, and nosebleed each independently necessitates ritual impurity. Similarly, retaliation and apostasy each alone is a cause for killing, if it is said that the two killings are not the same thing, otherwise rulings would not occur, but that is not so; rather the rulings are multiple. Therefore, the killing for retaliation is negated by pardon, while the other, killing for apostasy, remains; and vice versa. We say: multiplicity of rulings does not entail multiplicity in essence; for the same essence may be attributed to two things and differ by consideration, so rulings differ. If there were multiplicity, it would be by multiplicity of attribution to evidences, for the difference here is nothing but that. The necessary consequence is false, because attributions do not cause multiplicity in the essence of the attributed thing. The opponent may not be satisfied with this, but says: multiplicity of attribution to the cause may entail multiplicity of the attributed thing, even if it does not entail other attributions; rather this is the beginning of the issue, so reflect. The commentator of Al-Mukhtasar said: It necessitates. If attribution to causes caused multiplicity in essence, then it would be possible for one of the two events to remain while the other is negated. So it would be possible for the event of urine to remain while the event of stool is negated. It was replied that multiplicity does not necessitate the possibility of remaining because they may be inevitably concomitant. The author said: If the essence were multiple, then existence would be multiple, and thus non-existences would be multiple. So it would be conceivable for the intellect that one of them is negated while the other remains, even if there is inevitable conjunction in reality. This is the meaning of the commentator of Al-Mukhtasar. The questioner may not be satisfied with this and say: If he intends a mental conception that conforms to reality, then the implication is denied; if he intends the separation of one conception from the other, then its invalidity is denied. And what is said that killing for apostasy is a right of Allah, and retaliation is a right of the slave, and they are different—I say: it is rebutted by the fact that this difference is considered on the side of the cause, and the ruling that is the caused is the killing itself. Therefore, the wisdom in one of them (killing for apostasy) is preservation of religion, and in the other (retaliation) is preservation of life. And it should not escape you that killing is an act performed by the killer and related to the killed. There is no doubt that killing for apostasy is the act of the Imam or his representative, and killing for retaliation is the act of the heir or his representative. The first is obligatory, the second permissible, so they are definitely different. As for what is upon the killed, it is merely surrendering himself to the heirs if they demand his killing; there is no unity at all here. Perhaps this is what the proponent aimed at. Al-Amidi objected that the dispute is only about the individual particular, and the opponent denies it in the mentioned example; rather the caused in that example is one in type. I say: the assumption in the mentioned example is the concurrent occurrence of the causes, as when one urinates and has a nosebleed simultaneously. If there were unity by type not by individual, then for each cause there would be an individual caused that is different in individuality from the caused of another cause, and this would necessitate the conjunction of two similars. And it should not escape you that this necessity is only completed if for each cause there is a caused; rather, the effective factor here is the common element among the causes in a single individual, so there are no two similars. So understand.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute concerning two connected issues: (1) the condition of 'inverse entailment' (in‘ikās)—whether a valid legal cause must be such that the ruling is absent when the cause is absent; and (2) the permissibility of having multiple independent causes for a single legal ruling. The author begins by noting that some scholars require inverse entailment, which is based on the assumption that a ruling cannot have two independent causes. The rationale is that a ruling must have a cause; if it could have two causes, then the absence of one cause would not necessarily lead to the absence of the ruling, since the other could still be present. The majority of scholars (the position defended here) permit multiple causes and thus do not require inverse entailment. The author then cites various authorities: Imam Fakhr al-Islam (a Hanafi figure) considered reasoning from negation to be invalid; al-Qadi al-Baqillani (an Ash‘ari theologian) permits multiple causes only if the cause is explicitly stated in the text (manṣūṣah), not if it is derived (mustanbaṭah); another opinion reverses this; and 'the Imam' (likely Abu Hanifa or another leading imam) says multiple causes are intellectually possible but legally prevented. The Hanafi argument is that multiple causes have actually occurred in Islamic law: for ritual purification, various discharges (urine, stool, prostatic fluid, nosebleed) each independently necessitate ritual impurity. The same applies to capital punishment: apostasy alone warrants death, and retaliation alone also warrants death, but they are different because one is a right of Allah and the other is a right of the individual. An opponent may argue that the killings are the same, but the author responds that the acts are different: the act of execution for apostasy is performed by the Imam, while retaliation is performed by the heir. The rulings are distinct in terms of obligation vs. permissibility, and the underlying wisdoms differ (preservation of religion vs. preservation of life). Finally, the author addresses al-Amidi's objection that the dispute is only about the individual particular (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ); if the causes occur simultaneously (e.g., both urination and nosebleed), the resulting impurity is one in type but not necessarily one in individual. The author argues that the effective factor is the common element (the thing that causes impurity) which is a single individual, so there is no multiplicity of similars.

Key Terms
الانعكاس (al-in‘ikās): The condition that a valid cause must be such that when the cause is absent, the ruling is also absent.
العلة المنصوصة (al-‘illah al-manṣūṣah): A cause explicitly stated in the primary text (Qur’an or Sunnah) as the reason for a ruling.
العلة المستنبطة (al-‘illah al-mustanbaṭah): A cause derived by juristic reasoning from the text, not explicitly mentioned.
الواحد بالشخص (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ): A particular individual instance, as opposed to a type (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi evidence for the permissibility of multiple independent causes for a single ruling?
A) The verse on retaliation
B) The hadith on intention
C) The fact that several impurities (urine, stool, etc.) each independently cause ritual impurity
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits multiple independent legal causes, thereby rejecting inverse entailment (in‘ikās) as a necessary condition for a valid cause.

Multiple Causes for a Single Ruling by Type
Continuing the debate on multiple causes with Ibn al-Humam's refinement and the opponents' objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn al-Humam's shift to the unity of type in the debate on multiple causes, state the Hanafi position, and evaluate the opponents' contradiction argument.

Core Idea
Multiple causes are possible for a single ruling type; ritual impurity proves it.

This passage refines the long-standing debate on whether a single legal ruling can have multiple causes. Ibn al-Humam redirects the discussion from the individual instance to the type (naw‘), using ritual impurity as a clear example. The Hanafi school permits multiple causes, and the passage presents the opponents' objection—that multiple causes lead to contradictions—alongside the Hanafi response that independence means sufficiency when alone, not exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine what exactly is being debated: is a single ruling by type (e.g., ritual impurity) allowed to have multiple causes?
The debate is not about a specific individual ruling but about the category.
2
Recognize Ibn al-Humam's refinement
Ibn al-Humam points out that the discussion should be about 'the single ruling by type' (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘), not the individual particular. This removes previous complications.
Example: ritual impurity is one in type but has multiple causes (urine, stool, etc.).
3
Analyze the opponents' argument
Opponents argue that if multiple causes exist, each must be independent, but they cannot be because another cause exists; also the ruling would be both established and not established, leading to contradiction.
This is a classic logical objection against multiple causation.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply defines 'independence' as sufficiency when considered alone. This property remains even when other causes are present. Thus, no contradiction arises.
Independence does not mean exclusivity; multiple causes can coexist without negating each other's efficacy.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
If multiple causes are allowed, inverse entailment (in‘ikās) is not required for a ruling by type. The ruling type can persist even if one cause is absent.
This affects the validity of many qiyas arguments that rely on inverse entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه قال الشيخ ابن الهمام الظاهر بعد كون الكلام في الواحد الشخصي من الشرع، وهذا يدل على أن الكلام في الحكم الواحد بالنوع هل تتعدد علته وحينئذ يتم الكلام من غير كلفة . فإن الحدث واحد بالنوع قطعاً، وقد تعدد موجباته وسقط التكلفات التي قد مرت لإثبات الوحدة الشخصية، ويؤيده أنهم جعلوا من فروعه الانعكاس، وهو أن ينتفي بانتفاء العلة ومن البين أنه إنما يلزم لو امتنع تعدد العلة للحكم المتعلق بالفعل الواحد بالنوع، وأما إذا امتنع في الواحد بالشخص دون الواحد بالنوع فحينئذ يجوز أن ينتفي الحكم الشخصي بانتفاء العلة ويبقى نوعه، فلا انعكاس فلا يصح التفرع فافهم واستدل لو امتنع تعدد العلل (امتنع تعدد الأدلة) على حكم واحد، لأنها معرفات مثلها وتمنع الملازمة لأن الأدلة الباعثة أخص) من مطلق الأدلة، فلا يلزم من امتناع التعدد في الأخص امتناعه في الأعم المتحقق في فرد آخر . المانعون (قالوا : أولاً : لو تعددت العلل لزم استقلال كل لأنه مفروض وعدمه لعلية غيره) عنه (و) لزم (الثبوت) بهما لأنهما مؤثران و (لا) بهما لإمكان الثبوت بدون كل منهما فلزم التناقض (قلنا) الملازمة ممنوعة و معنى الاستقلال والثبوت بها كونها بحيث إذا انفردت ثبت بها الحكم وهذه الحيثية ثابتة لها دائماً وليست منفية فلا يلزم عدم الاستقلال ولا عدم الثبوت بهذا المعنى (أقول : إنما اختير ههنا هذا المعنى لأنه مشترك بين المجوّزين مطلقاً) من القائلين بكون
Then know that Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: 'It is apparent after considering that the discussion is about the individual particular (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) from the perspective of the Law.' This indicates that the discussion is about the single ruling by type: can its cause be multiple? At that point, the discussion proceeds without difficulty. For ritual impurity is one by type undoubtedly, and its causes are multiple. Thus the complications mentioned earlier to prove individual unity are removed. Supporting this is that they made among its branches the issue of inverse entailment (in‘ikās), which is that the ruling is negated by negation of the cause. It is clear that this only follows if multiple causes for the ruling relating to a single act by type are impossible. But if it is impossible only for the individual particular, not for the type, then it is possible that the individual ruling is negated by negation of the cause while its type remains, so there is no inverse entailment, and the branching is invalid. So understand. And they argued: if multiple causes are impossible, then multiple evidences for a single ruling would be impossible, because they are like identifiers. But the entailment is denied, because causing evidences (al-adillah al-bā‘ithah) are more specific than absolute evidences, so impossibility of multiplicity in the specific does not entail impossibility in the general realized in another individual. The opponents said: First, if causes are multiple, then each must be independent, but it is not independent because of the causality of another, and there would be establishment by both because they are effective, but also not establishment because establishment without each is possible, so contradiction follows. We say: The entailment is denied. The meaning of independence and establishment by them is that when each is alone, the ruling is thereby established. This aspect is established for them always and is not negated. So neither lack of independence nor lack of establishment in this sense follows. I say: This meaning is chosen here because it is common among those who permit multiple causes absolutely, among those who say that...
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on multiple causes with two important contributions. First, Ibn al-Humam points out that the entire debate has been about the individual particular, but the proper focus should be on the unity of the type (naw‘). When we speak of ritual impurity, for instance, it is one in type, but its causes are multiple (urine, stool, etc.). This removes the need to argue about individual unity and clarifies that the branch of inverse entailment (in‘ikās) only applies if multiple causes for a single type-ruling are impossible. If multiple causes are impossible only for the individual instance, then the absence of one cause could still leave the type intact, so inverse entailment is not required. The author then presents an argument from the opponents of multiple causes: if multiple causes were possible, each would have to be independent, but they would not be independent because of the other cause; also, the ruling would be established by both and also not established, leading to a contradiction. The Hanafi response is that independence means that if each cause were alone, it would suffice; this property is always present and not negated by the existence of other causes. The author notes that this definition is adopted because it is common among those who absolutely permit multiple causes. The passage ends abruptly at the bottom of the page, with the text continuing elsewhere.

Key Terms
الواحد بالنوع (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘): A single legal classification or type of ruling, such as ritual impurity as a category.
الأدلة الباعثة (al-adillah al-bā‘ithah): Evidences that actually impel the ruling, as opposed to mere indicators.
الانعكاس (al-in‘ikās): Inverse entailment: the principle that if the cause is negated, the ruling is negated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The individual particular (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ)
B) The type (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘)
C) The text of the Qur’an
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Humam's refinement shifts the multiple-causes debate to the type (naw‘), allowing multiplicity and resolving the logical objections through a proper definition of independence.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
6 min
Dispute on Multiple Causes for a Single Ruling
Analyzing the dispute on multiplicity of causes for a single legal ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ and al-wāḥid bi al-nawʿ, apply the Hanafi position on multiplicity of causes to examples like hadath and killing, and evaluate the objections and responses regarding independence and concurrence of similars.

Core Idea
A single ruling in type can have multiple causes; logical contradictions are resolved by the common element producing one individual effect.

One of the subtle debates in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh is whether a single legal ruling (ḥukm) can have more than one cause (ʿilla). At stake is how we understand the relationship between causes and their effects when multiple causes seem to converge. This passage from 'Fawātiḥ al-Raḥamūt' presents the dispute with the opponents (al-Āmidī et al.), the Hanafi defense, and a deeper analysis by later scholars like Ibn al-Humām. By working through this lesson, you will learn the critical distinction between unitary rulings by 'individual' and by 'type', and how the Hanafi school navigates the logical pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
State the core issue: Can a single legal ruling (e.g., the ḥukm of impurity) have multiple causes (e.g., urine and feces)? Note that the discussion focuses on the 'motivating cause' (ʿilla bāʿitha) that actually brings the ruling into existence.
The text distinguishes between cause (ʿilla) and mere evidence (dalīl).
2
Distinguish the two types of unity
Define al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ (individual unit) and al-wāḥid bi al-nawʿ (type unit). The Hanafi position permits multiplicity for the type unit but debates the individual unit. Example: hadath is one type but has multiple causes.
The same event (ḥadath) can arise from urine, feces, sleep, etc.
3
Examine the Hanafi evidence
Recap the two concrete examples: (a) two impurities (urine and nosebleed) occurring together, and (b) killing for apostasy vs. retributive killing. These show that multiple causes can lead to a shared effect without requiring multiple independent effects.
In the killing example, the acts differ in agent, wisdom, and ḥukm, so they are not a single ruling with multiple causes.
4
Analyze the opponents' objections
List the two main objections: (1) the independence contradiction – each cause must be both independent and not independent; (2) concurrence of similars – multiple causes would produce multiple effects, leading to two identical rulings. Understand why these objections seem forceful.
The opponents (al-Āmidī and others) argue that multiplicity is impossible even for the type unit.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi responses and resolution
Study the Hanafi responses: the meaning of 'independence' is conditional ('when isolated, the ruling is established'), so no real contradiction. For the concurrence issue, the effective factor is the common element (al-qadr al-mushtarak) that produces one individual effect, not two similars. Finally, note that if the discussion is about the type unit, the difficulties fall away entirely.
The common element explanation is a key Hanafi move: each cause has efficacy but is impeded by the locus, so only the shared aspect produces the effect.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

«المختصر وتسميته بالاستقلال مجاز، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن غاية ما يلزم مما ذكر كون الثبوت مجازاً في الثبوت على تقدير الانفراد ولا يلزم منه كون الاستقلال مجازاً، فإن التجوز في التفسير لا يستلزم التجوز في المفسر ثم الحق المجازية قطعاً لأن التأثير ليس إلا ثبوت الوجود بها وقد توقف على الانفراد، فعند الاجتماع لا تأثيراً أصلاً لفقد شرطه، فلا استقلال، وعلى هذا قوي الاستدلال بحيث لا شبهة فيه فافهم (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : لو جاز) تعدّد العلل (لزم اجتماع (المثلين إذ لا بد لكل من معلول وقد يجاب بأنه على تقدير التعدّد واجتماعهما العلة المجموع فيكون بإزائه شخص واحد من الحكم. قال المصنف : جعل كل علة مستقلة عند الانفراد وجزء عند الاجتماع تحكم، ثم اعلم أن هذا الدليل بظاهره يدل على امتناع التعدّد للواحد بالنوع، بل هو الألصق به، فإن لزوم اجتماع المثلين فيه ظاهر، وهذا يرشدك أيضاً إلى أن الكلام في الواحد بالنوع، وحينئذ يمكن لنا أن نجيب بأن العلة حينئذ القدر المشترك لفرد منه، لا كل يوجب شخصاً مغاير لما يوجبه الآخر، نعم كان اقتضاء كل ذلك، لكن تخلف لمانع وهو عدم صلوح المحل فأوجب المشترك لعدم إباء المحل عن قبول أثره فافهم واستقم (وأجيب بأن ذلك) اللزوم (في العلل العقلية المفيدة للوجود لامتناع التخلف هناك، فيلزم وجود معلول كل فيلزم المثلان و (أما الأدلة المفيدة للعلم بالحكم (فلا) لعدم كونها عللاً بالحقيقة حتى توجب الوجود كذا) في المختصر أقول : لا يخفى أن الكلام) ههنا (في العلة الباعثة المفيدة لوجود الحكم في الخارج وإن كانت الإفادة بوضع الشارع (لا) في مطلق الدليل) الدال على الحكم والعلة الباعثة لا يتخلف عنها الحكم كالعقلية فتأمل على أن العلم أيضاً
If multiplicity of causes necessitated multiplicity in essence, then it would be possible for one of the two events to remain while the other is absent. Thus it would be possible for the event of urine to remain while the event of feces is absent. It was responded that multiplicity does not necessitate possibility of remaining because it is possible that they are concomitant. The author said: If the essence were multiple, then existence would be multiple, and thereby non-existence would be multiple; so it would be conceivable for the mind that one of them is absent while the other remains, even if there is actual concomitance between them. This is what the commentator of al-Mukhtasar meant. And the questioner may not be satisfied and say: If he means a conformable conception of the absence of one with the presence of the other, then the implication is denied; if he means the detachment of one conception from the other, then its falsehood is denied. And what is said: 'Killing for apostasy is a right of Allah, and retributive killing (qisas) is a right of the slave, and they are different' – I say: It is refuted because that difference is considered on the side of the cause, while the rule (hukm) and effect (ma'lul) is killing. Therefore the wisdom in one (killing for apostasy) is preservation of religion, and in the other (qisas) is preservation of life. And you are not unaware that killing is an act subsisting in the killer and relating to the killed. There is no doubt that killing for apostasy is an act of the Imam or his deputy, and killing for qisas is an act of the guardian (wali) or his deputy. The first is obligatory, the second is permissible, so they are definitely different. As for what is upon the killed, it is only surrendering himself to the guardians if they seek his killing. So there is no unity here at all. Perhaps this is what that speaker intended. Al-Amidi objected that the dispute is about the individual unit (al-wahid bi al-shakhs), and the opponent denies it in the mentioned image; rather the effect in that image is one in type. I say: The assumption in the mentioned image is the concurrence of the causes together, as when one urinates and has a nosebleed simultaneously. So if there were unity by type not by individual, then for each cause there would be a personal effect different in person from the effect of the other, and that would necessitate the concurrence of similars. And you are not unaware that this necessitation is only complete if each cause has an effect; but the effective factor here is the common element between the causes in one individual, so there are no two similars here. Understand. Then know that Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: 'Apparently the discussion is about the individual unit from the Shari'a, and this indicates that the discussion is about whether the single ruling in type can have multiple causes. In that case the discussion proceeds without difficulty. For the event (hadath) is one in type definitively, and its causes are multiple. The difficulties that occurred to prove individual unity fall away. This is supported by the fact that they made from its branches the 'in'ikās (reciprocal implication), which is that [the ruling] is negated by the negation of the cause. And it is clear that this only follows if multiplicity of cause for the ruling related to a single act in type is impossible. But if it is impossible for the individual unit but not for the type unit, then it is permissible that the personal ruling be negated by the negation of the cause while its type remains, so no 'in'ikās, so the branching is not valid. Understand.' And he argued: If multiplicity of causes is impossible, then multiplicity of evidences for a single ruling is impossible because they are like identifiers. But the implication is denied because the motivating evidences are more specific than absolute evidences, so impossibility of multiplicity in the more specific does not necessitate impossibility in the more general which is realized in another individual. The opponents said: 'First: If causes were multiple, each would be independent because it is assumed so, and not independent due to the causality of the other; and affirmation by both would be necessary because they are effectual, and not by both because affirmation without each is possible, thus contradiction follows.' We say: The implication is denied. The meaning of independence and affirmation by them is that they are such that when isolated, the ruling is established by them. This quality is always established for them and is not negated, so neither lack of independence nor lack of affirmation in this sense follows. I say: This meaning is chosen here because it is common between all those who permit [multiplicity] – those who hold that each influence is independently effective when combined, or the whole is a part. It is not as Taftazani imagined, that it is only for the one who holds partiality, as is not hidden. And it will come what is the verification. Then do not be unaware that this response is nothing, because if they were multiple, it would be possible for an individual effect to exist without each one, so it would not depend on any one, and no influence of any one would reach it, so it would not be established by any one, so the quality of being established by it – i.e., by its influence when isolated – would not be established. Rather, it would follow that it is not established by any one, and no one is independent. Then, since each is assumed as a cause, affirmation by its influence is necessary, so affirmation by it follows, and thus the two contradictories necessarily follow. Contemplate, for it is subtle. It is as if it is known and denied. Then this contradiction does not follow for the type unit, for it has multiple existences; with respect to some it depends on one, and with respect to another on another. If the discussion is about it, then the response is complete. He said in al-Hashiya: 'The arguer took independence to mean affirmation by it and not by another. This is of two types: actual affirmation and conditional affirmation. The first is real, the second metaphorical, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. This is because it is established that applying the description to a single instance is metaphorical. So the arguer conducted his speech on the real, and the respondent answered by clarifying the intended meaning. This speech apparently indicates that affirmation by each of the two causes when combined is a conditional, metaphorical affirmation, and that affirmation by each is only in the case of isolation. On this, isolation is a condition for the effect's being established by it. So when combined, it is not established by either one in reality, so no influence in reality, so no causality in reality. This is in reality a confession that multiplicity is impossible in reality.' Then he said: 'By what we have said, what is in Sharh al-Sharh is refuted: If the meaning of independence is this, then the statement of the commentator of al-Mukhtasar is not valid, and his calling it independence is metaphorical. And you are not unaware that the utmost that follows from what was mentioned is that affirmation is metaphorical in the case of conditional isolation, and it does not require that independence be metaphorical. For figurative interpretation does not require the interpreted term to be figurative. Then the correct view is that it is definitively metaphorical because influence is nothing but the establishment of existence by it, and that has been conditioned upon isolation. So when combined, there is no influence at all due to the absence of its condition, so no independence. On this, the argument becomes strong with no doubt. Understand.' And they said: 'Second: If multiplicity of causes were allowed, it would necessitate the concurrence of similars, because each must have an effect.' It may be answered that in the case of multiplicity and combination, the cause is the sum, so that it corresponds to one individual ruling. The author said: Considering each cause independent when isolated and a part when combined is arbitrary. Then know that this evidence apparently indicates the impossibility of multiplicity for the type unit; indeed it is more appropriate to it, for the necessity of concurrence of similars is apparent in it. This also guides you to the fact that the discussion is about the type unit. In that case, we can answer that the cause then is the common element for an individual of it, not each one producing a different individual from what the other produces. Yes, each had that efficacy, but it was impeded by a preventer, namely the unsuitability of the locus, so the common element produced what it did because the locus did not refuse to accept its effect. Understand and be upright. And it was answered that that necessity is in intellectual causes that give existence, because non-deferral there is impossible, so the effect of each would exist and two similars would concur. As for evidences that give knowledge of the ruling, they do not, because they are not causes in reality so as to necessitate existence. Thus in al-Mukhtasar. I say: It is not hidden that the discussion here is about the motivating cause that produces the existence of the ruling externally, even if the production is by the Legislator's placement, not about absolute evidence indicating the ruling. And the motivating cause is not deferred from the ruling like the intellectual, so contemplate. Moreover, knowledge also...
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a complex usul al-fiqh dispute on whether a single legal ruling (hukm) can have multiple causes (علل). The text begins by considering a hypothetical: if causes were multiple, then one could remain while the other is absent — but the response is that they might be concomitant. The commentator (مصنف) explains that if essence were multiple, existence would be multiple, making it conceivable for one to be absent while the other remains, even if in reality they are concomitant. The opponent may deny this implication. The example of killing for apostasy (حق الله) and retributive killing (حق العبد) is raised: both are killing but differ in cause, wisdom, and ruling (obligatory vs. permissible). So they are not identical. Al-Amidi objects that the dispute is about the individual unit (واحد بالشخص), not type. But the text argues that even if causes concur (like urine and nosebleed), the effective factor is the common element producing a single individual effect, avoiding 'concurrence of similars'. Ibn al-Humam suggests that the discussion is actually about the type unit (واحد بالنوع), making it easier: the event (حدث) is one in type with multiple causes. The branch of 'in'ikās (that the ruling is negated by the cause's negation) only applies if multiplicity is impossible for the individual; if only type is considered, then the personal ruling may be negated while the type remains. The opponents raise two arguments: (1) multiplicity would require each cause to be both independent and not independent, leading to contradiction. The response redefines independence as 'when isolated, the ruling is established' — this quality is always present, so no contradiction. (2) multiplicity would require multiple effects leading to 'concurrence of similars'. The answer is that when causes combine, the sum is the cause for one individual effect. The text critiques these responses, noting that if the discussion is about the type unit, the contradiction does not arise because existences are multiple. The commentator in al-Hashiya clarifies that independence is metaphorical when combined, as the condition of isolation is required for actual influence. The text concludes that the dispute is really about the motivating cause (علة باعثة) that produces the ruling externally, not mere evidence. The overall emphasis is on defending the possibility of multiple causes for a single type of ruling while recognizing the logical difficulties for individual unity.

Key Terms
العلة (al-‘illa): In this passage, cause refers to the motivating cause (علة باعثة) that produces the existence of the ruling externally, not merely evidence.
الواحد بالشخص (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ): The individual unit – a single specific instance of a ruling, not a class.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘): The type unit – a class or kind of ruling that can have multiple instances.
الاستقلال (al-istiqlāl): Independence, meaning that the cause alone is sufficient to establish the ruling when isolated.
القدر المشترك (al-qadr al-mushtarak): The common element shared between multiple causes that produces a single individual effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the example of killing for apostasy (قَتْلُ الرِّدَّة) and retributive killing (قِصَاص) NOT constitute a case of a single ruling having multiple causes?
A) Because they are both the same type of killing.
B) Because the causes and wisdoms differ, and the acts are performed by different agents.
C) Because the ruling on killing is always one.
D) Because the حكم is the killing itself, not the cause.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits multiple causes for a single ruling in type, resolving logical objections through the concepts of conditional independence and the common element.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
The Independence of Causes: Distinguishing One-by-Type from One-by-Person
Refuting arguments against multiple causes using logical and typological distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the one-by-type (al-wahid bi al-naw') and the one-by-person (al-wahid bi al-shakhs) and apply this distinction to resolve the apparent contradiction of multiple causes.

Core Idea
Multiple causes can operate on different instances of a ruling type, but not on a single specific individual.

In Hanafi usul, the possibility of multiple independent causes for a single ruling is a contentious issue. The critics argue that if two causes are independent, they would produce contradictory effects. This lesson unpacks the logical argument and introduces the critical distinction between the ruling as a type and as a specific individual, showing how this resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first argument against multiple causes
Read the beginning of the source passage where the opponent argues that if there are multiple independent causes, the effect could exist without each, leading to a contradiction. Understand the logical structure: each cause must be sufficient alone, but if both are present, the effect would be overdetermined.
The key is the concept of independence (istiqlal).
2
Analyze the Hanafi response: distinction between real and estimated establishment
The responder clarifies that 'independence' means establishment by it alone, but this is only real when the cause is isolated. When combined, the establishment is metaphorical/estimated (taqdiri). This avoids the contradiction because the effect is actually produced by the combination, not each individually.
The text from al-Hashiya explains that independence is metaphorical when combined.
3
Understand the one-by-type vs one-by-person distinction
The passage states that the contradiction does not occur for the one-by-type (al-wahid bi al-naw'), because a type has multiple instances. Each cause can affect a different instance. But for one specific individual (al-wahid bi al-shakhs), the combination is the cause.
This distinction is crucial to understanding the Hanafi position.
4
Apply the distinction to the second argument (meeting of likes)
The second argument is that multiple causes would require two identical effects (mithlayn) to coexist. The Hanafi answer is that when causes target the same individual, the cause is the totality, so only one effect results. When they target different individuals of the same type, the effects are different individuals, not identical in the problematic sense.
The text argues that the meeting of likes is only problematic in rational causes, not in legal causes.

الأصل الرابع/ القياس كل مؤثر مستقلاً عند الاجتماع أو المجموع والواحد جزء (لا كما توهم التفتازاني من بالقائل بالجزئية) فقط كما لا يخفى (وسيجيء مما هو (التحقيق ثم لا يذهب عليك أن هذا الجواب ليس بشيء فلا يصح في غير المنصوصة (وعورض بالعكس) يعني بأن الحكم بالجزئية دون العلية تحكم فلا يحكم بها أيضاً أقول في العلية إلغاء الآخر من وجه، فإنه لو انفرد واحد منها لا دخل للآخر أصلاً ( وليس كذلك الجزئية) فإنه لو انفرد احتاج إلى آخر في التأثير فالجزئية تترجح فتأمل إنه دقيق ولك أن تعارض بأن في الجزئية إلغاء الاستقلال والتأثير عن كل وإنما التأثير والاستقلال للمجموع ففي العلية كل مؤثر، وليس لأحد الإلغاءين ترجيح فعاد التحكم على أن التعدد مرجوح لأنه قليل وخلاف الأصل، فالجزئية راجحة (والجواب) عن استدلال القاضي أن (الاستقلال لكل (يستنبط بالعقل بأن يكون بينهما عموم من وجه فيوجد كل من العلل بدون الآخر مع الحكم فيعلم أن ليس العلة المجموع، بل كل مستقل، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن هذا لا يستقيم في الواحد بالشخص، فإن الحكم الواحد بالشخص لا يوجد في محلين مع واحد فقط، فالإجابة بهذا الجواب ترشدك إلى الكلام في الواحد بالنوع اللهم إلا أن يقرر أن كلا من العلتين لما وجدت مع نوع الحكم بدون الآخر فكل مؤثر في النوع ، فإذا وجدتا في محل فيجب تأثيرهما في النوع فيه والنوع في محل واحد لا يوجد إلا في شخص واحد، فلزم تعدّد العلل لمعلول واحد شخصي بالدليل فلا ينكر، فلا تحكم فتأمل فيه . ثم إنه قد يقرر الجواب بأنه إذا وجد كل بدون الآخر مع وجود الحكم فكل مستقل انفراداً، وعند الاجتماع العلة المجموع ورده المصنف بأن هذا تسليم أن
The fourth principle: Qiyas: every effective [cause] is independent when combined, or the totality [is the cause] and the individual is a part (not as Taftazani imagined from the one who says by partiality) only, as is not hidden. (And will come what is the verification). Then do not be unaware that this answer is nothing, because if they were multiple, it would be possible for the person of the effect to exist without each, so it would not depend on one, and the effect of one would not reach it, so it would not be established by one, so the mode of establishment would not be established by it, i.e., by its effect when alone; rather, it would necessitate that it is not established by each, and none is independent. Then, if each is assumed as a cause, then establishment is necessary by the effect of each, so establishment by it is necessary, so the two contradictories necessarily follow. So ponder, for it is subtle; it is as if he knows and denies. Then, this contradiction does not necessitate in the one-by-type, for it has existences; by virtue of some existences it depends on one, and by virtue of another on another. If the discussion is about it, then the answer is complete. He said in al-Hashiya: The proponent took independence to mean establishment by it and not by others, and this is in two ways: actual establishment and estimated establishment. The first is real, the second metaphorical, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. This is because it is established that applying a description to individuals is metaphorical. So the proponent conducted his speech on reality, and the responder responded by clarifying the intended meaning. This speech, by its apparent meaning, indicates that the establishment by each of the two causes at the time of combination is an estimated, metaphorical establishment, and that establishment by each is only during separation. On this, separation is a condition for the effect's establishment by it, so at the time of combination, it is not established by any one of them in reality, so no effect in reality, so no causality in reality. This is, in reality, an acknowledgment of the impossibility of multiplicity in reality. Then he said: By what we said, what is in Sharh al-Sharh is repelled: if the meaning of independence is this, then the statement of the commentator of al-Mukhtasar that it is called independence metaphorically is not valid. You are not unaware that the utmost that follows from what is mentioned is that the establishment is metaphorical in the establishment on the condition of separation, and it does not necessitate that independence is metaphorical, for metaphor in interpretation does not necessitate metaphor in the interpreted. Then he determined the metaphor definitively, because effect is nothing but the establishment of existence by it, and it is conditioned on separation, so at the time of combination there is no effect at all due to the absence of its condition, so no independence. On this, the proof is strong such that there is no doubt in it, so understand. (And) they said: (Second: If multiple causes were permissible, then the meeting of two like things would be necessary, for each must have an effect. It may be answered that on the presumption of multiplicity and their combination, the cause is the totality, so in front of it is one person of the ruling. The author said: Making each cause independent when separate and a part when together is arbitrary. Then know that this proof, in its apparent meaning, indicates the impossibility of multiplicity for the one-by-type, indeed it is more appropriate for it, for the necessity of meeting of two like things is evident therein. And this also guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type. Then we can answer that the cause then is the common element of an individual of it, not each necessitating a person different from what the other necessitates. Yes, each had that requirement, but it was unfulfilled due to a preventer, which is the unsuitability of the locus, so the common element necessitated due to the locus not refraining from accepting its effect. So understand and be steadfast. (And it was answered that) that necessity (is in rational causes that bring about existence, because non-deficiency is impossible there, so the effect of each must exist, so the two like things are necessary. As for proofs that bring about knowledge of the ruling, they do not, because they are not actually causes that necessitate existence). Thus in al-Mukhtasar. I say: It is not hidden that the discussion) here (is about the motivating cause that brings about the existence of the ruling in the external world, even if the bringing about is by the legislator's establishment, not) about an absolute proof (that indicates the ruling. And the motivating cause is not deficient regarding the ruling like the rational cause, so ponder. Moreover, knowledge also exists in the external world, for it is with us, the theologians, an external attribute subsisting in the world, so the meeting of two like things applies there. Even if it is accepted that knowledge does not exist in the external world but in the mind as the philosophers hold, or a relational matter as appears from some words of Imam al-Razi, then there is no dispute about existence in itself even if it is not called existence in the external world, so the meeting of two like things applies in the mind, so reflect. And the correct (answer is that the assumed) here (is the coming together upon the one-by-person) based on the discussion being about it, (so each causes what the other causes, not its like, such that the meeting of two like things is necessary. You may say: It is necessary for the existence of the effect to depend on the existence of the cause, and it is effective in it. If one cause is sufficient, it has effected the existence of the effect, so it does not depend on another, and another has been assumed also sufficient, so there must be another existence for it so that this other cause can effect it, and this existence comes from it. The multiplicity of existences necessitates multiplicity of persons, so the two like things necessarily follow. So ponder it. (And) they said: (Third: They argued regarding the cause of the prohibition of riba: Is it measurement) along with genus, as is our school, (or nutrition) as is the Shafi'i school, (or staple food) as is Malik's view, (by preponderance of one over the other. That is a branch of the validity of each and a necessary consequence of the negation of multiplicity, otherwise each would be a cause and there would be no need for preponderance. This also guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type, because riba is a type under which many individuals fall. (It was answered that they engaged in nullification, for the proponent of each denies the other), not for preponderance (such that the validity of each is necessary. Even if it is accepted that they engaged in preponderance, it is due to consensus on the unity of the cause here, and it does not necessitate unity in every ruling) Al-Qadi said: (If) the independence of each of the causes is explicitly stated, (it must be accepted due to the obligation to follow the text. And what is not explicitly stated, we rule (by partiality, not by independence), for ruling by causality (multiplicity independently) without partiality is arbitrary, so it is not valid in non-textual cases. (It was objected by the opposite), meaning that ruling by partiality without causality is also arbitrary, so it should not be ruled. I say: In causality there is an annulment of the other in one respect, for if one of them is alone, the other has no role at all. (This is not the case with partiality), for if it is alone, it needs another to effect, so partiality is preponderant. Ponder, it is subtle. You may object that in partiality there is annulment of independence and effect from each, and effect and independence are only for the totality, while in causality each is effective, and neither annulment has preponderance, so arbitrariness returns. Moreover, multiplicity is preponderant because it is rare and contrary to the principle, so partiality is preponderant. (And the answer) to al-Qadi's argument is that (the independence of each is deduced by reason, such that between them is a general from a perspective, so each of the causes is found without the other along with the ruling, so one knows that the cause is not the totality but each independent. And you are not unaware that this does not hold for the one-by-person, for the one ruling by person does not occur in two loci with only one. So answering with this answer guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type. Unless it is determined that each of the two causes, when found with the type of ruling without the other, is effective in the type, so when they coincide in one locus, their effect in the type in that locus is necessary, and the type in one locus does not exist except as one person, so the multiplicity of causes for one individual effect follows by evidence, so it cannot be denied, so it is not arbitrary. So ponder on it. Then, it may be determined that the answer is: when each is found without the other along with the existence of the ruling, then each is independent when separate, and when together the cause is the totality. The author rejected this, saying: This is an admission that...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a dense usul dispute about the permissibility of multiple independent causes (ta'addud al-'ilal) for a single ruling. The commentator (likely from Fawatih al-Rahmut) is defending the Hanafi position that when multiple potential causes are present, the cause is the combination of all, not each individually. The text presents and refutes three arguments against multiple causes. The first argument is that if multiple causes were independent, the effect could exist without each, leading to contradictions. The responder clarifies that independence means establishment by it alone, and this independence is only real when the cause is alone; when combined, establishment is only estimated. The second argument is that multiple causes would necessitate the meeting of two like things (al-mithlayn). The Hanafi response distinguishes between the one-by-type (al-wahid bi al-naw') and the one-by-person (al-wahid bi al-shakhs): for a type of ruling, multiple causes can apply to different individuals, but for one specific individual, only one cause operates. The third argument uses the example of the cause of riba prohibition (measurement vs nutrition vs staple) to show that since scholars preponderate one cause over others, this presupposes that only one cause can be correct, implying the impossibility of multiple causes. The answer is that these scholars are not really preponderating but nullifying each other's claims, and the consensus on a single cause for riba does not apply universally. The text also discusses al-Qadi's objection that if a text explicitly states the independence of a cause, it must be accepted, but the Hanafi response is that independence is deduced by reason and applies only to the type, not the individual. The commentator concludes by noting the author's rejection of the compromise that each is independent when separate and the totality when together. Throughout, the text stresses that the debate is about causes for a type of ruling, not a specific individual instance. The Hanafi position is that for a single specific ruling, only one cause is effective, but multiple causes can exist for different instances of the same type.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause that necessitates the ruling in qiyas, here discussed in terms of independence and multiplicity.
تعدد العلل (ta'addud al-'ilal): The possibility of multiple independent causes for a single ruling.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wahid bi al-naw'): One in terms of type or species, meaning a category of rulings with multiple instances.
اجتماع المثلين (ijtima' al-mithlayn): The meeting or coexistence of two identical (like) effects, considered impossible in rational and legal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when two potential causes are present together for a single specific ruling (one-by-person), what is considered the cause?
A) Each cause is independent and effective separately.
B) The cause is the combination (al-majmu') of both.
C) Only the strongest cause applies, the other is null.
D) Both causes are valid but only one produces the effect.

Key Takeaway
Multiple causes can coexist for a type of ruling but for a specific individual, the cause is the combination; understanding this distinction resolves the logical objections.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
The Riba Debate: Nullification vs Preponderance in Determining the 'Illa
Refuting the argument that the riba cause debate proves multiplicity impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that scholars' preponderance of a single cause for riba proves multiplicity is impossible, and explain the Hanafi counter-argument of nullification.

Core Idea
Disagreement over the cause of riba does not prove multiplicity impossible; it reflects each school nullifying the other's cause.

The third argument against multiple causes is based on the famous dispute over the effective cause ('illa) for riba: is it measurement, nutrition, or staple food? Critics say that if multiple causes were possible, scholars would not need to pick one. This lesson examines the Hanafi rebuttal that these scholars are engaged in nullification (ibtaal), not preponderance (tarjih), and that the consensus on riba does not apply universally.

Guided Path
1
State the three proposed causes for riba
Identify that the Hanafis say measurement (kayl) with genus; Shafi'is say nutrition (tu'm); Malikis say staple food (iqtitat). Each school claims its cause is the only valid one.
This is a classic ikhtilaf.
2
Understand the argument from preponderance
The opponent argues that if multiple causes were possible, each school would not need to preponderate one over the other; they could all be correct. The need to choose shows that only one cause can be correct, hence multiple causes are impossible.
This argument assumes that preponderance is the only reason for disagreement.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal: nullification (ibtaal)
The Hanafi response is that each school is not merely preponderating but actually nullifying the other's cause. Because each cause is mutually exclusive—if measurement is the cause, nutrition cannot also be the cause for that specific instance—the schools are denying the validity of the other's claim, not just weighing them.
The text says: 'they engaged in nullification, for the proponent of each denies the other'.
4
Evaluate the role of consensus (ijma)
Even if preponderance is granted, the text says it is due to consensus on a single cause for riba specifically, not a general principle. This does not rule out multiplicity in other cases.
The madhhab_dispute_map in the analytical toolbox provides this.
