الحكم فيعلم أن ليس العلة المجموع، بل كل مستقل، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن هذا لا يستقيم في الواحد بالشخص، فإن الحكم الواحد بالشخص لا يوجد في محلين مع واحد فقط، فالإجابة بهذا الجواب ترشدك إلى الكلام في الواحد بالنوع اللهم إلا أن يقرر أن كلا من العلتين لما وجدت مع نوع الحكم بدون الآخر فكل مؤثر في النوع ، فإذا وجدتا في محل فيجب تأثيرهما في النوع فيه والنوع في محل واحد لا يوجد إلا في شخص واحد، فلزم تعدّد العلل لمعلول واحد شخصي بالدليل فلا ينكر، فلا تحكم فتأمل فيه . ثم إنه قد يقرر الجواب بأنه إذا وجد كل بدون الآخر مع وجود الحكم فكل مستقل انفراداً، وعند الاجتماع العلة المجموع ورده المصنف بأن هذا تسليم أن
The fourth principle: Qiyas: every effective [cause] is independent when combined, or the totality [is the cause] and the individual is a part (not as Taftazani imagined from the one who says by partiality) only, as is not hidden. (And will come what is the verification). Then do not be unaware that this answer is nothing, because if they were multiple, it would be possible for the person of the effect to exist without each, so it would not depend on one, and the effect of one would not reach it, so it would not be established by one, so the mode of establishment would not be established by it, i.e., by its effect when alone; rather, it would necessitate that it is not established by each, and none is independent. Then, if each is assumed as a cause, then establishment is necessary by the effect of each, so establishment by it is necessary, so the two contradictories necessarily follow. So ponder, for it is subtle; it is as if he knows and denies. Then, this contradiction does not necessitate in the one-by-type, for it has existences; by virtue of some existences it depends on one, and by virtue of another on another. If the discussion is about it, then the answer is complete. He said in al-Hashiya: The proponent took independence to mean establishment by it and not by others, and this is in two ways: actual establishment and estimated establishment. The first is real, the second metaphorical, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. This is because it is established that applying a description to individuals is metaphorical. So the proponent conducted his speech on reality, and the responder responded by clarifying the intended meaning. This speech, by its apparent meaning, indicates that the establishment by each of the two causes at the time of combination is an estimated, metaphorical establishment, and that establishment by each is only during separation. On this, separation is a condition for the effect's establishment by it, so at the time of combination, it is not established by any one of them in reality, so no effect in reality, so no causality in reality. This is, in reality, an acknowledgment of the impossibility of multiplicity in reality. Then he said: By what we said, what is in Sharh al-Sharh is repelled: if the meaning of independence is this, then the statement of the commentator of al-Mukhtasar that it is called independence metaphorically is not valid. You are not unaware that the utmost that follows from what is mentioned is that the establishment is metaphorical in the establishment on the condition of separation, and it does not necessitate that independence is metaphorical, for metaphor in interpretation does not necessitate metaphor in the interpreted. Then he determined the metaphor definitively, because effect is nothing but the establishment of existence by it, and it is conditioned on separation, so at the time of combination there is no effect at all due to the absence of its condition, so no independence. On this, the proof is strong such that there is no doubt in it, so understand. (And) they said: (Second: If multiple causes were permissible, then the meeting of two like things would be necessary, for each must have an effect. It may be answered that on the presumption of multiplicity and their combination, the cause is the totality, so in front of it is one person of the ruling. The author said: Making each cause independent when separate and a part when together is arbitrary. Then know that this proof, in its apparent meaning, indicates the impossibility of multiplicity for the one-by-type, indeed it is more appropriate for it, for the necessity of meeting of two like things is evident therein. And this also guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type. Then we can answer that the cause then is the common element of an individual of it, not each necessitating a person different from what the other necessitates. Yes, each had that requirement, but it was unfulfilled due to a preventer, which is the unsuitability of the locus, so the common element necessitated due to the locus not refraining from accepting its effect. So understand and be steadfast. (And it was answered that) that necessity (is in rational causes that bring about existence, because non-deficiency is impossible there, so the effect of each must exist, so the two like things are necessary. As for proofs that bring about knowledge of the ruling, they do not, because they are not actually causes that necessitate existence). Thus in al-Mukhtasar. I say: It is not hidden that the discussion) here (is about the motivating cause that brings about the existence of the ruling in the external world, even if the bringing about is by the legislator's establishment, not) about an absolute proof (that indicates the ruling. And the motivating cause is not deficient regarding the ruling like the rational cause, so ponder. Moreover, knowledge also exists in the external world, for it is with us, the theologians, an external attribute subsisting in the world, so the meeting of two like things applies there. Even if it is accepted that knowledge does not exist in the external world but in the mind as the philosophers hold, or a relational matter as appears from some words of Imam al-Razi, then there is no dispute about existence in itself even if it is not called existence in the external world, so the meeting of two like things applies in the mind, so reflect. And the correct (answer is that the assumed) here (is the coming together upon the one-by-person) based on the discussion being about it, (so each causes what the other causes, not its like, such that the meeting of two like things is necessary. You may say: It is necessary for the existence of the effect to depend on the existence of the cause, and it is effective in it. If one cause is sufficient, it has effected the existence of the effect, so it does not depend on another, and another has been assumed also sufficient, so there must be another existence for it so that this other cause can effect it, and this existence comes from it. The multiplicity of existences necessitates multiplicity of persons, so the two like things necessarily follow. So ponder it. (And) they said: (Third: They argued regarding the cause of the prohibition of riba: Is it measurement) along with genus, as is our school, (or nutrition) as is the Shafi'i school, (or staple food) as is Malik's view, (by preponderance of one over the other. That is a branch of the validity of each and a necessary consequence of the negation of multiplicity, otherwise each would be a cause and there would be no need for preponderance. This also guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type, because riba is a type under which many individuals fall. (It was answered that they engaged in nullification, for the proponent of each denies the other), not for preponderance (such that the validity of each is necessary. Even if it is accepted that they engaged in preponderance, it is due to consensus on the unity of the cause here, and it does not necessitate unity in every ruling) Al-Qadi said: (If) the independence of each of the causes is explicitly stated, (it must be accepted due to the obligation to follow the text. And what is not explicitly stated, we rule (by partiality, not by independence), for ruling by causality (multiplicity independently) without partiality is arbitrary, so it is not valid in non-textual cases. (It was objected by the opposite), meaning that ruling by partiality without causality is also arbitrary, so it should not be ruled. I say: In causality there is an annulment of the other in one respect, for if one of them is alone, the other has no role at all. (This is not the case with partiality), for if it is alone, it needs another to effect, so partiality is preponderant. Ponder, it is subtle. You may object that in partiality there is annulment of independence and effect from each, and effect and independence are only for the totality, while in causality each is effective, and neither annulment has preponderance, so arbitrariness returns. Moreover, multiplicity is preponderant because it is rare and contrary to the principle, so partiality is preponderant. (And the answer) to al-Qadi's argument is that (the independence of each is deduced by reason, such that between them is a general from a perspective, so each of the causes is found without the other along with the ruling, so one knows that the cause is not the totality but each independent. And you are not unaware that this does not hold for the one-by-person, for the one ruling by person does not occur in two loci with only one. So answering with this answer guides you to the discussion being about the one-by-type. Unless it is determined that each of the two causes, when found with the type of ruling without the other, is effective in the type, so when they coincide in one locus, their effect in the type in that locus is necessary, and the type in one locus does not exist except as one person, so the multiplicity of causes for one individual effect follows by evidence, so it cannot be denied, so it is not arbitrary. So ponder on it. Then, it may be determined that the answer is: when each is found without the other along with the existence of the ruling, then each is independent when separate, and when together the cause is the totality. The author rejected this, saying: This is an admission that...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a dense usul dispute about the permissibility of multiple independent causes (ta'addud al-'ilal) for a single ruling. The commentator (likely from Fawatih al-Rahmut) is defending the Hanafi position that when multiple potential causes are present, the cause is the combination of all, not each individually. The text presents and refutes three arguments against multiple causes. The first argument is that if multiple causes were independent, the effect could exist without each, leading to contradictions. The responder clarifies that independence means establishment by it alone, and this independence is only real when the cause is alone; when combined, establishment is only estimated. The second argument is that multiple causes would necessitate the meeting of two like things (al-mithlayn). The Hanafi response distinguishes between the one-by-type (al-wahid bi al-naw') and the one-by-person (al-wahid bi al-shakhs): for a type of ruling, multiple causes can apply to different individuals, but for one specific individual, only one cause operates. The third argument uses the example of the cause of riba prohibition (measurement vs nutrition vs staple) to show that since scholars preponderate one cause over others, this presupposes that only one cause can be correct, implying the impossibility of multiple causes. The answer is that these scholars are not really preponderating but nullifying each other's claims, and the consensus on a single cause for riba does not apply universally. The text also discusses al-Qadi's objection that if a text explicitly states the independence of a cause, it must be accepted, but the Hanafi response is that independence is deduced by reason and applies only to the type, not the individual. The commentator concludes by noting the author's rejection of the compromise that each is independent when separate and the totality when together. Throughout, the text stresses that the debate is about causes for a type of ruling, not a specific individual instance. The Hanafi position is that for a single specific ruling, only one cause is effective, but multiple causes can exist for different instances of the same type.

Key Terms
الإبطال (al-ibtaal): Nullification: denying the validity of another claim or cause.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Preponderance: giving weight to one evidence over another.
الجزئية (al-juz'iyya): Partiality: treating each cause as a part of the totality rather than independent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the schools disagree on the cause of riba?
A) They are preponderating one cause over another based on stronger evidence.
B) They are nullifying each other's cause, each denying the other's validity.
C) They only disagree due to differing ijma on the matter.
D) They agree on the cause but differ on its application.

Key Takeaway
The riba dispute does not prove that multiple causes are impossible; it reflects each school nullifying the other's cause, and consensus on riba is specific to that case.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Convergence of Independent Causes on One Effect
Debate on multiple independent causes producing the same legal ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on tawārud of independent causes to analyze a scenario involving multiple causes and explain why the effect remains one.

Core Idea
Multiple independent causes can converge on one effect without contradiction; the effect is one, not duplicated.

In this lesson, we explore a fundamental debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can two fully independent causes produce the same single ruling? The passage defends the Hanafi view that they can, using the example of urination and nosebleed both causing ritual impurity. You will learn the arguments for and against, and the key distinction between the cause being independent and the effect being singular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Identify the exact point of disagreement: whether two or more independent causes can converge on a single effect, and if this leads to 'meeting of two similars' or caprice.
The Hanafi school affirms tawārud; some mutakallimun deny it.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position is that convergence is valid. The proof is the urination/nosebleed example: each independently causes impurity, but the result is a single impurity, not two.
The example is found in the source: 'بال ورعف معاً بال فصار محدثاً ورعف فصار محدثاً'.
3
Understand the opponent's argument
Opponents argue that unity of effect requires unity of cause; otherwise, there is a meeting of two similars (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn) or capricious choice (tahakkum).
They claim that if each cause is fully efficacious, they would produce two rulings, which is impossible.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response denies that the ruling is duplicated. The effect is one, and each cause is independently sufficient. The 'fa' (then) is valid for each: if the cause exists, the ruling exists. No contradiction arises because the ruling is singular.
The passage also addresses the 'one not specifically' view and refutes it.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Test the principle: consider simultaneous vomiting and bleeding (both cause impurity). According to Hanafi view, the person becomes impure once, and each cause independently establishes that ruling.
This demonstrates the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

The subject of dispute is verification. If the coming together is not of independent causes in this sense, but in the first sense, then the coming together of deficient causes occurs in this single individual. Because what is established individually is a different individual from what is established by the combination. Thus each is not independent in this sense; rather the combination is independent, and each cause is deficient. So there is no coming together of independent causes, and no dispute as mentioned. From here, because the subject of dispute is the coming together of that whose efficacy does not depend on another, the opponents have necessitated the meeting of two similars, unlike when each is independent individually and part of the independent at combination, there is no basis for meeting of two similars. And then, when it is realized that the subject of dispute is the coming together of that which is complete in efficacy, the objection is repelled: if he means independence for each individually, it is not beneficial for you because partiality at combination does not contradict independence individually; and if he means at combination, then the very subject of dispute is contradiction, not impossibility. The repelling is that independence is in efficacy, and that meaning is constantly established for it, both in combination and individually. Reflect. And you may object in another way: that conceded is efficacy of establishing the type of ruling when there is no obstructer, constant for each, but not always. This does not necessitate establishing efficacy of the specific ruling for each in combination. Reflect. Then I say: Perhaps there is prevention of caprice on the assumption of unity, as is the last branch of the second. Caprice would only be if the unity was identical, but the unity is not identical. It is permissible that the real cause is one of them not specifically. The answer: speech here is when there is no common factor between them that is the real cause, as in the absence of two parts. For the nature of absence of the part from the cause, with any designation, is the real cause. Then if by the specified unspecified is meant the ambiguous one, i.e., one of the specified ambiguous exists by the existence of each, then its non-determination follows, but the known is determined. The mind is averse to allowing it, so a specific designated one must be intended, whichever it is, and therein is caprice. Reflect. This answer is nothing, for assuming the absence of a common factor is an impossible assumption. And at least one of them not specifically. And aversion from causality of the ambiguous for the determined is only for the real effective cause, not for the effecter whose effect is by legal consideration. Indeed, the mind does not reject that the one wise judge rules a type of ruling and considers the effect of multiple attributes on this type, and the effect of one not specifically on an individual. As for considering the effect of each on an individual, it is impossible because it leads to contradiction as you know. Understand. The proponents of partiality said: If each is a cause, then meeting of two similars occurs as mentioned; or one, then caprice is necessary. Both are false, so partiality is determined. I say in response: We do not accept the meeting of two similars, for two causes come together on one, not two things. In meaning, each is a branching of one on each, so the effect is one with no duality at all. Its criterion is the correctness of the 'fa' by saying: the cause was found, so the ruling was found. It is valid here for each with respect to it independently, as is valid in the case of urination and nosebleed together: he urinated and became impure, and nosebleed and became impure. But you may say: The insertion of the meaning of 'fa' here is only due to each containing the one not specifically, which is the true branch not specifically. How not? Specifically as mentioned. The fourth principle: analogical reasoning. Branching on something contradicts existence without it. Understand. For this reason, because the one is branched on each, if one is absent, there is no need for another benefit in its existence at its absence, as when an apostate who killed another unjustly becomes Muslim, so the meeting of two similars is not necessary, nor is benefit by the combination necessary. You already knew the necessary meeting of two similars, so remember. The truth in response is to deny caprice on the assumption of unity. No, said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar in response: It is established by combination in the sense of its establishment for each independently, as in scriptural evidences. Each is independent in establishing it, so even if the other is absent, its absence does not harm. The difference between this and what you claimed is apparent. But it was replied that there is no difference in the harm of absence of one and its non-existence, because there is no dispute about independence individually. If one is absent, the other remains alone and effective. Rather, the difference is that causality of all in this view reverts to the individual whole, while in what the proponent claims, it reverts to the collective whole. The author said in his response in the gloss: If the cause is the combination, then with the absence of one cause, the combination which is the complete cause is absent, and the effect is possible. The necessity acquired from the complete cause disappears with its disappearance, so its absence harms its remaining. If it remains, it remains by another cause. Unlike if each is an independent cause, then possibility does not occur by the absence of one of them, because it is necessary by another independent cause, so its absence does not harm. So it does not need another cause or benefit. To this he pointed by his saying: there is no need for another benefit. This is solid speech, but it remains that acquiring one obligation from two is invalid, for it is axiomatic that the obligatory by another is only obligated by its obligator, so the other is superfluous. With multiplicity, acquisition of obligation is not valid, so it remains on possibility, and does not exist at all. So coming together of independent causes on a single individual is not valid. Understand. The proponents of the one not specifically said: Partiality for each is invalid due to independence (i.e., sufficiency of each for benefit) and independence for each here is invalid due to combination contradicting independence. The specific is caprice, so the one not specifically is determined. The answer is apparent: it is to deny contradiction between combination and independence, because it is complete in efficacy present in each. But you well know that completeness in efficacy necessitates existence by it and superfluity of the other in efficacy. So if at combination each cause is independent, they are superfluous, so combination contradicts independence. Thus the truth has become clear by the most sound proof. Understand.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate in usul al-fiqh about whether multiple independent causes can converge on a single legal effect, and the implications for the unity of the cause. The author begins by defining the subject of dispute: the convergence of causes that are fully efficacious (tam al-iqtida). He distinguishes this from deficient causes and clarifies that the dispute only concerns independent causes. The opponents argue that if each cause is independent, their convergence would lead to 'meeting of two similars' (ijtima' al-mithlayn) or capriciousness. The author provides several responses, including the idea that the effect is one and the causes each produce the same ruling, so there is no duality. He uses the example of urination and nosebleed both causing impurity to illustrate that multiple independent causes can produce the same effect. The passage also discusses the alternative view of 'the one not specifically' and refutes it. The author concludes that the truth is to deny the contradiction between combination and independence, but then raises the issue that if each cause is independent, the other is superfluous, which contradicts the concept of independence. This is a sophisticated analysis of causality in Islamic legal theory.

Key Terms
توارد (tawārud): Convergence of two or more causes on a single effect.
علة (ʿilla): A cause that necessitates its effect, whether independently or as part of a combination.
استقلال (istiqlāl): Independence of a cause in producing its effect without needing a partner.
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn): The meeting of two similar rulings from different causes, which is considered invalid by opponents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position discussed, what happens when a person both urinates and has a nosebleed simultaneously?
A) The person becomes impure twice due to two causes.
B) The person becomes impure once, and each cause is independently sufficient.
C) The person does not become impure because the causes cancel each other.
D) The impurity is determined by the first cause alone.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds that multiple independent causes can converge on a single ruling without duplication or contradiction.

Can a Single Cause Justify Two Rulings?
Debate on whether one 'illah can produce two ahkam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul distinction between 'illah as indicator (amara) and motive (ba'ith) to evaluate whether a single legal cause can produce two rulings.

Core Idea
A single 'illah can justify two rulings if it has two distinct maslahas, or as a mere indicator.

This lesson explores a fundamental usul dispute: can one 'illah serve as the basis for two separate ahkam? The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'illah as a mere indicator (amara) and 'illah as a motive (ba'ith), allowing the latter only when multiple maslahas are involved. We will examine the arguments and the reasoning behind the preferred Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'illah
From the passage, distinguish between 'illah as amara (indicator) and 'illah as ba'ith (motive). Note which is agreed upon and which is disputed.
The passage states that amara is agreed, ba'ith is the subject of debate.
2
State the Hanafi position for each meaning
For amara, it is permitted by consensus. For ba'ith, the preferred view (mukhtar) permits it. Provide the examples given: sunset for amara; theft and qadhf for ba'ith.
The Hanafi school does not accept restitution for theft, but the example still serves as a theoretical illustration.
3
Summarize the opponents' arguments
The opponents argue from two angles: the philosophical principle that one cause produces one effect, and the logical redundancy (tahsil al-hasil) if one ruling already achieves the maslaha.
These arguments are typical of Mutakallimun usul methodology.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response counters that the one-cause-one-effect principle applies only to true intellectual unity, not to conventional shar'i causes. Regarding redundancy, if the 'illah has two distinct maslahas, two rulings are needed to achieve both.
This rebuttal relies on the multiplicity of maslahas in the 'illah.
5
Determine the practical implication (thamarah al-khilaf)
The main practical consequence is in deriving rulings from a single cause. For example, qadhf serves as the 'illah for both the hadd penalty and the rejection of testimony in Hanafi fiqh.
Other schools may require separate evidence or additional conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العكس وهو تعليل حكمين بعلة واحدة فبمعنى الإمارة (اتفاق أي متفق على جوازه بمعنى كونها إمارة مجردة لحكمين (كالغروب) إمارة لجواز الفطر ووجوب (المغرب) و (أما العلة أي تعليل حكمين بعلة (بمعنى الباعث فالمختار جوازه وقيل : لا لنا بعد في مناسبة وصف لحكمين) واعتبار الشارع إياه فيهما كيف وقد وقع كالسرقة للقطع زجراً وللتغريم جبراً لما فات، وهذا لا يصلح إلزاماً لعدم الإجماع فإن الحنفية لا يغرمون بل هو ملتحق في حق التغريم بما لا قيمة له ولو بدل بالردة لكان أولى والقذف) علة (للحد) أي الجلد وعدم قبول الشهادة) بالنص إما دائماً كما هو مذهبنا ويقتضيه ظاهر النص أو إلى التوبة، المنكرون :(قالوا : أولاً : الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلاّ الواحد) فلا يكون وصف واحد علة لحكمين (قلنا) لو سلم بطلان صدور الواحد من الكثير ولم يقم عليه دليل فإنما ذلك في الواحد الحقيقي) من جميع الجهات (وههنا جهات مختلفة، وأيضاً ذلك في العلل الحقيقية العقلية لا الوضعية الشرعية كما لا يخفى (و) قالوا : ثانياً : تجويز تعليل حكمين بعلة (فيه تحصيل الحاصل لحصول المصلحة بأحدهما أي بأحد الحكمين، فلو شرع حكم آخر لتحصيلها يلزم تحصيل الحاصل (قلنا : ذلك اللزوم (إذا لم يحصل للوصف مصلحتان وكان كل من الحكمين وشرعه مستقلاً في التحصيل) أي في تحصيل المصلحة، وأما إذا كان له مصلحتان لا يكفي الحكم الواحد لتحصيلهما لا بد من شرع حكم آخر فافهم
As for the opposite—that is, using one cause to justify two rulings—it is agreed that it is permissible when the cause serves as an indicator (amara), i.e., a mere sign for two rulings, like sunset being a sign for the permissibility of breaking the fast and the obligation of Maghrib prayer. As for the cause in the sense of a motive (ba'ith), the preferred view is its permissibility, while some say no. Our evidence: the suitability of a description for two rulings and the Lawgiver's consideration of it for both—as has occurred, such as theft being a cause for amputation as a deterrent and for restitution as compensation for what is lost. However, this is not binding due to lack of consensus, for the Hanafis do not impose restitution; rather, it is likened in terms of restitution to something of no value, and replacement by apostasy would be more appropriate. Qadhf (slander) is a cause for the hadd (flogging) and for rejection of testimony, by text (nass), either permanently—as is our madhhab and as the apparent meaning of the text requires—or until repentance. The deniers said: First, one thing cannot produce more than one thing, so a single description cannot be a cause for two rulings. We reply: Even if the impossibility of multiple effects from one cause were granted, no proof has been established for it; that only applies to a true unity from all respects, whereas here aspects are different. Also, that is for true intellectual causes, not conventional legal (shar'i) causes, as is obvious. They said second: Allowing one cause for two rulings involves tahsil al-hasil (obtaining what is already obtained), because the maslaha is achieved by one of the two rulings; if another ruling is legislated for it, it would be redundant. We reply: That follows only if the description does not have two maslahas and each ruling alone suffices for achieving the maslaha. But if the description has two maslahas such that one ruling is insufficient to achieve both, then another ruling is necessary—so understand.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding whether a single legal cause ('illah) can serve as the basis for two distinct rulings (hukm). The author distinguishes two meanings of 'illah: as a mere indicator (amara) and as a motive (ba'ith). As an indicator, it is unanimously permissible, exemplified by sunset indicating both permissibility of iftar and obligation of Maghrib. As a motive, the preferred Hanafi view (al-mukhtar) permits it, citing examples from the Shariah: theft (sariqa) is the cause for the hadd penalty (amputation) as a deterrent and, according to some, for financial restitution as compensation, though the Hanafi school does not require restitution. Another example is qadhf (false accusation of adultery), which is the cause for both the hadd of flogging and the permanent rejection of testimony (in the Hanafi view). The opponents argue based on the philosophical principle that one thing cannot give rise to two effects, and that it would involve tahsil al-hasil (redundancy). The Hanafi response counters that this principle applies only to true intellectual unity, not to the multiple aspects in conventional legal causes, and that if the description has two distinct maslahas (benefits), then two rulings are needed. The author concludes with 'fafham' (so understand), emphasizing the point.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illah): The legal cause or effective description in qiyas that serves as the basis for a ruling.
الإمارة (al-imarah): A mere sign or indicator that points to a ruling without being the motive behind it.
الباعث (al-ba'ith): The motive or underlying reason that prompts the Lawgiver to enact a ruling.
تحصيل الحاصل (tahsil al-hasil): Obtaining what is already obtained; logical redundancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi preferred view, can a single 'illah (in the sense of motive) justify two different rulings?
A) No, because one cause can only produce one effect.
B) Yes, but only if the 'illah is considered as a mere indicator.
C) Yes, if the description has two distinct maslahas that require separate rulings.
D) No, because it would always lead to redundancy (tahsil al-hasil).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a single 'illah to justify two rulings, either as an agreed indicator or as a preferred motive when multiple maslahas are present.

Condition: The Cause Cannot Be Subsequent to the Ruling
Stating and defending a condition of causation in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the condition that the 'illah must precede or be simultaneous with the hukm al-asl, and evaluate examples where this condition is violated.

Core Idea
A cause cannot be later than its ruling — otherwise the ruling exists without a motive, which is absurd.

In Hanafi usul, the 'illah (effective cause) in qiyas must be prior or at least simultaneous to the ruling it grounds. If the 'illah is subsequent, the ruling would have been legislated without a cause, breaking the causal link. This lesson explores this condition through critical examples: the case of insanity and guardianship, and the repulsiveness (istiqdhār) of pig saliva. We will also examine the rational proofs that uphold this condition and a nuanced objection regarding multiple causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Recognize that the 'illah cannot be later than the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). If it is subsequent, the causal relationship is invalid.
This condition applies to all forms of qiyas, not only the examples given.
2
Analyze the First Example: Insanity and Guardianship
Examine the flawed example of using the guardian's insanity to remove guardianship over a minor. The 'illah (guardian's insanity) is not a suitable cause because it is unrelated to the minor's status and is temporally subsequent.
The commentator's example is criticized for misplacing the 'illah.
3
Analyze the Second Example: Pig Saliva and Repulsiveness
Consider the example of declaring pig saliva impure due to repulsiveness (istiqdhār). The repulsiveness in the legal sense is established only after the ruling of impurity, making it subsequent and thus invalid as a cause.
Ibn al-Humam's refutation notes that simultaneity would suffice, but here even simultaneity is absent.
4
Grasp the Rational Proofs
Understand the two arguments: (1) the khulf argument—if the cause were later, the ruling would be legislated without a motive, which is absurd; (2) the impossibility of two causes—if the cause is later, the ruling exists before its motive, impossible unless two causes are allowed.
The second argument relies on the Hanafi position that a single ruling cannot have two independent causes simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (أن لا تتأخر عن حكم الأصل كتعليل ولاية الأب في الأموال على الصغير الذي عرض له الجنون بالجنون فيلحق به المجنون في حق النكاح للاشتراك في الجنون ومثل شارح المختصر بتعليل سلب الولاية عن الصغير بالجنون العارض للولي ولا يخفى ما فيه إذ لا معنى لعلية جنون الولي لعدم كون الصغير ولياً ولا مناسبة أصلاً فقيل أنه من وضع الظاهر موضع المضمر والمعنى سلب الولاية عن الصغير العارض له جنون بجنونه العارض (وقيل) تعبيره بالولي لا يخلو عن كدر (بل) المعنى أن يعلل سلب الولاية عن الصغير المجنون بالجنون الذي هو عارض في الولي البالغ المقيس على الصغير المجنون في سلب الولاية (أقول : أنه أبعد عن الفهم (عكس المراد لأنه (المطلوب ههنا (العروض في الأصل) في صدد تمثيله بمثال (ولم يذكر لا في الفرع) أي ليس المطلوب العروض في الفرع (وقد ذكر ) فالمطلوب غير مذكور وما هو مذكور غير المطلوب بل المعنى أن يعلل سلب ولاية الولي عن الصغير أي ليس ولياً عليه أصلاً بالجنون العارض (له يعني سلب ولاية أحد على الصغير بالجنون العارض ،له ولا يخفى أن التوجيه الأول أظهر الكل (وقد يمثل بتعليل نجاسة لعاب الخنزير بالاستقذار) أي عده قذراً (فيقاس عليه العرق وهو) أي الاستقذار (متأخر عنها) لأن عده قذراً بعد العلم بنجاسته (ورد) الشيخ ابن الهمام بأنه أي التأخر (غير لازم للجواز المقارنة بينهما (أقول : الاستقذار طبعاً متقدم على نجاسة اللعاب، لكنه لم يظهر عليته (و) الاستقذار (شرعاً متأخر عن النجاسة (ولو رتبة لأن الطاهر لا يستقذر) شرعاً، فليس ههنا استقذار مقارن (فافهم لنا : لو تأخرت العلة عن شرع الحكم لم يكن شرعه لها أي لم تكن مشروعية الحكم لأجل العلة، فلم تبق العلة علة هذا خلف (واستدل) على المختار أيضاً (لو تأخرت العلة عن الحكم (ثبت) الحكم ( بلا باعث لوجوده قبل الباعث بالفرض (أقول) هذا الاستدلال مبني على امتناع التعليل بعلتين وإلا فالملازمة ممنوعة لجواز أن يكون الحكم معللاً بباعثين يوجد بأحدهما ثم يوجد الباعث الآخر فافهم .
And among the conditions of the cause is that it not be subsequent to the ruling of the original case. For example, using the guardianship of the father over the minor's property, when the minor becomes insane, by the insanity itself, so the insane person is analogized to the minor in matters of marriage due to sharing insanity. And the commentator of al-Mukhtasar gave an example: using the removal of guardianship over the minor by the insanity occurring to the guardian. But what is in it is not hidden, for there is no meaning to the insanity of the guardian being a cause since the minor is not a guardian, and there is no suitability at all. So it was said that this is placing the apparent in place of the implicit, and the meaning is: removal of guardianship over the minor who has insanity by his own insanity. And it was said: his expression 'guardian' is not free of turbidity. Rather, the meaning is that the removal of guardianship over the insane minor is caused by the insanity that is present in the adult guardian who is analogized to the insane minor in removal of guardianship. I say: That is farther from comprehension; it reverses the intended meaning because what is required here is that the occurrence be in the original case, and he did not mention it in the branch—so the required is not mentioned, and what is mentioned is not the required. Rather, the meaning is that the removal of the guardian's authority over the minor—i.e., that he has no guardianship at all—is caused by the insanity occurring to him. And it is not hidden that the first interpretation is the most apparent. And one may give the example of declaring the saliva of a pig impure by [the cause of] being repulsive (istiqdhār), i.e., counting it as filth; then sweat is analogized to it. And istiqdhār is subsequent to it because counting it as filth occurs after knowledge of its impurity. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam refuted by saying: this being subsequent is not necessary; simultaneity is sufficient. I say: Istiqdhār by nature is prior to the impurity of the saliva, but its being a cause is not apparent. And istiqdhār legally (shar'an) is subsequent to impurity, even in rank, because the pure is not considered repulsive legally. So there is no simultaneous istiqdhār here—understand. Our proof: if the cause were subsequent to the legislation of the ruling, the legislation would not be for the sake of the cause, so the cause would not remain a cause—this is absurd (khulf). And it was also argued for the preferred view: if the cause were subsequent to the ruling, the ruling would be established without a motive for its existence before the motive by assumption. I say: This argument is based on the impossibility of using two causes; otherwise the correlation is denied, for it is possible that the ruling is caused by two motives and exists by one, then the other motive comes—so understand.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of talīl (causation in qiyas) that the 'illah (cause) must not be subsequent to the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). The author provides examples to clarify. The first example: using the insanity of a minor who becomes insane as a cause for the father's guardianship over property is problematic because the insanity occurs after the minor's state changes. The commentator of al-Mukhtasar gave an example of removing guardianship over the minor due to the guardian's insanity, but that is criticized because the guardian's insanity is not a suitable cause for the minor's status. After discussing interpretations, the author presents another example: declaring pig's saliva impure due to it being regarded as repulsive (istiqdhār); but istiqdhār legally is subsequent to the knowledge of impurity, so it cannot be the cause. Ibn al-Humam refuted that simultaneity is sufficient. The author notes that naturally, animals are regarded as repulsive prior to knowledge of impurity, but that is not the cause. Legally, repulsiveness is subsequent to impurity because the pure is not legally repulsive. Therefore, the condition is that the cause must be prior (or at least simultaneous) to the ruling. The proof: if the cause were later, the ruling would have been legislated without its motive, leading to absurdity (khulf). Another argument based on the impossibility of two causes: if the cause is later, the ruling would exist without a motive before the cause, which is impossible unless one allows for two causes. The author notes that this argument assumes the impossibility of two causes; if two causes are possible, then one cause could precede and another succeed.

Key Terms
تأخر العلة عن الحكم (ta'akhkhur al-'illah 'an al-hukm): The cause being posterior to the ruling, which invalidates the causal relationship.
الاستقذار (al-istiqdhār): The quality of being repulsive or filthy, considered as a possible cause for impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is declaring pig's saliva impure due to istiqdhār (being repulsive) invalid as a cause according to the condition discussed?
A) Because istiqdhār is not a suitable legal description.
B) Because istiqdhār legally is subsequent to the knowledge of impurity.
C) Because pig's saliva is not impure in the Hanafi school.
D) Because the cause must be something else like intoxication.

Key Takeaway
The 'illah must be prior or simultaneous to the ruling of the original case; otherwise, the causal link is invalid and the qiyas fails.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Two Conditions for a Valid Cause in Qiyas
Stating two additional conditions for 'illah in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposed cause ('illah) violates the conditions of not invalidating its original or contradicting a text/consensus, and explain why with examples.

Core Idea
A valid 'illah must not make the original ruling meaningless or oppose a clear text.

In Hanafi usul, a cause ('illah) used for qiyas must satisfy several conditions. Two crucial ones are that the cause must not invalidate the ruling of the original case (asl) and must not contradict a clear text (nass) or consensus (ijma). This lesson explains these conditions through classical examples, such as the Shafi'i attempt to allow immediate salam based on avoiding uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed 'illah and its original case
State the 'illah and the ruling of the asl (original case). For example, in the salam contract, the asl requires a fixed term, and the Shafi'i 'illah is 'avoiding uncertainty'.
2
Check if the 'illah logically invalidates the asl's ruling
If the 'illah applies to the far' (branch) but applying it would make the ruling of the asl irrelevant or meaningless, then the condition is violated. For salam, if the 'illah is avoiding uncertainty, then immediate salam also avoids uncertainty, so the requirement of a fixed term becomes pointless.
This is the condition 'al-'awda 'ala al-asl bi al-ibtal'.
3
Check if the 'illah contradicts a text or consensus
Examine whether the 'illah conflicts with an explicit Quranic verse, authentic hadith, or agreed-upon consensus. The salam example also contradicts the explicit hadith 'to a known term'.
The condition is 'mukhalafat al-nass wa al-ijma'.
4
Apply to the Shafi'i salam example
Trace the Shafi'i argument: the 'illah of avoiding uncertainty is used to allow immediate salam. However, this 'illah would also require that the original ruling (deferred salam with a term) be unnecessary, thus invalidating the asl. Additionally, it contradicts the explicit text requiring a term. So the 'illah fails both conditions.
5
Apply to the fasting example
Consider the 'illah of 'deterrence' (zajr) for expiation fasting. If applied only to the rich king, it contradicts the general text that does not restrict the ruling to the wealthy. Thus it violates the condition of not contradicting nass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (أن لا يعود على أصله (بالإبطال أي لا يكون التعليل مبطلاً لحكم أصله (كتعليل الشافعية نص السلم وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنِ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُّسَمًّى فَاكْتُبُوه الله [البقرة: ۲۸۲] قال ابن عباس : نزلت في السلم وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «من أسلم فليسلم في كيل معلوم ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم (يخرج إحضار السلعة) فيجوز السلم الحال كالمؤجل المبطل للأجل المنصوص) في السلم (و) منها (أن لا يخالف نصاً ولا إجماعاً، كإيجاب الصوم على الملك المرفه خاصة في الكفارة) بتعليل الزجر، فإنه مبطل
And among the conditions of the cause is that it not invalidate its original—i.e., the ta'lil should not nullify the ruling of its original case. For example, the Shafi'is' ta'lil of the verse on salam (advance payment): the verse says, 'O you who believe, when you contract a debt for a fixed term, write it down' (2:282). Ibn 'Abbas said: it was revealed about salam. And the Prophet said: 'Whoever gives salam, let him give it in a known measure and weight and known term.' [That ta'lil] excludes the bringing of the commodity, thereby allowing immediate salam like deferred salam, which invalidates the stipulated term in salam. And among them is that it not contradict a text or consensus, such as making fasting obligatory specifically on the rich king in expiation, by ta'lil of deterrence, for that is invalidating.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage states two more conditions for a valid 'illah. First: the cause must not undermine the ruling of the original case (asl). The example given is the Shafi'i argument that the verse on writing debts (2:282) and the hadith on salam require a known term, so they infer that the salam contract's causa is the future delivery, thus allowing immediate salam as well since the purpose is to avoid uncertainty. But that would negate the explicit condition of a fixed term in the text, thus invalidating the original ruling. This condition ensures the analogy does not render the original hukm meaningless. Second: the cause must not contradict explicit textual evidence (nass) or consensus (ijma). The example: if someone claims that fasting is obligatory only on a luxury-rich king in expiation, using the cause of deterrence (zajr), this would violate the text which applies the ruling generally. Such an 'illah is invalid because it contradicts nass or ijma.

Key Terms
العودة على الأصل بالإبطال (al-'awda 'ala al-asl bi al-ibtal): The cause nullifying the very ruling it was meant to justify, making the analogy self-defeating.
مخالفة النص والإجماع (mukhalafat al-nass wa al-ijma): The cause opposing an explicit text or established consensus, rendering it invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the Shafi'i example of allowing immediate salam based on the cause of avoiding uncertainty considered invalid according to the condition?
A) Because immediate salam is not a valid contract.
B) Because the cause (avoiding uncertainty) would also apply to deferred salam, thus negating the requirement of a fixed term.
C) Because the Shafi'i school does not accept qiyas.
D) Because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A valid 'illah must neither negate the ruling of its original nor contradict a clear text or consensus.

Permitting Two Rulings from One 'Illah: Refuting Tahsil al-Hasil
Defending the permissibility of two rulings from one 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that one 'illah can justify multiple rulings when it contains multiple benefits, and identify the condition that avoids the fallacy of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
One 'illah can justify multiple rulings if it has distinct benefits; otherwise, redundancy invalidates the second.

In usul al-fiqh, qiyas relies on an 'illah to extend a ruling from an original case to a new one. A classical objection argues that if one 'illah produces two rulings, the second is redundant because the benefit is already achieved by the first (tahsil al-hasil). The Hanafi response, presented in this passage, clarifies that this objection only applies when the 'illah contains a single benefit. If it contains two distinct benefits, each ruling serves a separate purpose, and no redundancy occurs.

Guided Path
1
State the Objection
Identify the claim of the opponents: using one 'illah for two rulings leads to tahsil al-hasil because one ruling already obtains the benefit.
The benefit (maslahah) is the purpose of the ruling.
2
Extract the Hanafi Condition
Locate the key phrase 'إذا لم يحصل للوصف مصلحتان' (if the attribute does not have two benefits). This sets the condition under which the objection applies.
If the attribute has two benefits, the objection does not apply.
3
Analyze the Logical Structure
Understand the conditional reasoning: the objection holds only when the 'illah has one benefit; if it has two, each ruling targets a distinct benefit, so no redundancy.
This is a rational argument (dalil 'aqli).
4
Apply to a Concrete Example
Consider the Hanafi use of debt (dayn) as an 'illah for both the prohibition of selling debt for debt (bay' al-kali' bi al-kali') and the permissibility of istihsan in salam, where each ruling addresses a different maslahah.
This exemplifies the principle in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً ذلك في العلل الحقيقية العقلية لا الوضعية الشرعية كما لا يخفى (و) قالوا : ثانياً : تجويز تعليل حكمين بعلة (فيه تحصيل الحاصل لحصول المصلحة بأحدهما أي بأحد الحكمين، فلو شرع حكم آخر لتحصيلها يلزم تحصيل الحاصل (قلنا : ذلك اللزوم (إذا لم يحصل للوصف مصلحتان وكان كل من الحكمين وشرعه مستقلاً في التحصيل) أي في تحصيل المصلحة، وأما إذا كان له مصلحتان لا يكفي الحكم الواحد لتحصيلهما لا بد من شرع حكم آخر فافهم
And also that is in the real intellectual causes, not the legal conventional ones, as is not hidden. And they said: Secondly: Permitting the causation of two rulings by one cause entails tahsil al-hasil because the benefit is attained by one of them, i.e., one of the two rulings. So if another ruling is legislated to attain it, tahsil al-hasil follows. We said: That entailment [applies] if the attribute does not have two benefits and each of the two rulings and its legislation is independent in attainment, i.e., in attaining the benefit. But if it has two benefits, one ruling is insufficient to attain both; another ruling must be legislated, so understand.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the permissibility of using one 'illah for two rulings. The objection claims that if one ruling already achieves the benefit, legislating another is redundant (tahsil al-hasil). The Hanafi response distinguishes: if the attribute has two distinct benefits, each ruling targets a different benefit, so no redundancy occurs. For example, in Hanafi fiqh, the 'illah of debt is used to justify both the prohibition of selling debt for debt and the permissibility of istihsan in salam, each serving a separate maslahah.

Key Terms
علة (illah): The attribute that is the cause for a legal ruling in qiyas.
تحصيل الحاصل (tahsil al-hasil): The logical fallacy of achieving what is already achieved, used as an objection against multiple rulings from one 'illah.
مصلحة (maslahah): Benefit or purpose intended by the Lawgiver in legislating rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, when does the objection of tahsil al-hasil apply to the use of one 'illah for two rulings?
A) When the attribute has two benefits, but one ruling suffices.
B) When the attribute has only one benefit, so two rulings are redundant.
C) When the rulings are contradictory.
D) When the 'illah is not real but conventional.

Key Takeaway
One 'illah can justify multiple rulings without redundancy if it encompasses multiple distinct benefits (masalih).

The 'Illah Must Not Be Delayed from the Original Ruling
Stating a condition of the 'illah and debating its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that the 'illah must be contemporaneous with the original ruling, identify the rational proof (dalil aqli) for it, and distinguish between natural and legal istiqdhar in the debate.

Core Idea
The 'illah must be present at the time of the original ruling; otherwise the ruling would lack a cause.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, qiyas requires the 'illah (effective cause) to satisfy several conditions. One crucial condition is that the 'illah must not be delayed (mutakhkhir) from the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains why this condition is necessary, using examples from family law and purity law, and addresses a debate about whether natural disgust (istiqdhar) can serve as an 'illah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Read the opening statement: 'From the conditions of the illah is that it is not delayed from the ruling of the origin.' Note that this is a strict temporal requirement in Hanafi usul.
2
Examine the First Example
The text gives an example of using insanity as an 'illah to remove guardianship. Follow the reasoning: the insanity must be present in the original case (the minor who is insane) to be a valid cause for removing guardianship. The commentator criticizes an earlier formulation that mistakenly used the guardian's insanity.
This illustrates how the 'illah must be inherent to the asl.
3
Analyze the Second Example
The example of pig's saliva (najasah) and istiqdhar (considering something impure) is more complex. Natural istiqdhar precedes the legal ruling, but legal istiqdhar is derived from the ruling. The author argues that legal istiqdhar is delayed and thus cannot serve as the 'illah for the same ruling.
This shows the difficulty of applying the condition to natural vs. legal attributes.
4
Understand the Rational Proof
The author provides a rational argument: if the 'illah were delayed, the ruling would exist without a motivating cause (ba'ith) at the time of its establishment. This is logically untenable because every ruling must have a cause for its legislation.
This dalil aqli is the foundation of the condition.
5
Evaluate the Debate
The passage includes a rebuttal by Ibn al-Humann (the commentator) and the author's own response. Note the distinction between contemporaneity (muqarana) and precedence. The Hanafi position insists on contemporaneity, not mere precedence.
This nuance is critical for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (أن لا تتأخر عن حكم الأصل كتعليل ولاية الأب في الأموال على الصغير الذي عرض له الجنون بالجنون فيلحق به المجنون في حق النكاح للاشتراك في الجنون ومثل شارح المختصر بتعليل سلب الولاية عن الصغير بالجنون العارض للولي ولا يخفى ما فيه إذ لا معنى لعلية جنون الولي لعدم كون الصغير ولياً ولا مناسبة أصلاً فقيل أنه من وضع الظاهر موضع المضمر والمعنى سلب الولاية عن الصغير العارض له جنون بجنونه العارض (وقيل) تعبيره بالولي لا يخلو عن كدر (بل) المعنى أن يعلل سلب الولاية عن الصغير المجنون بالجنون الذي هو عارض في الولي البالغ المقيس على الصغير المجنون في سلب الولاية (أقول : أنه أبعد عن الفهم (عكس المراد لأنه (المطلوب ههنا (العروض في الأصل) في صدد تمثيله بمثال (ولم يذكر لا في الفرع) أي ليس المطلوب العروض في الفرع (وقد ذكر ) فالمطلوب غير مذكور وما هو مذكور غير المطلوب بل المعنى أن يعلل سلب ولاية الولي عن الصغير أي ليس ولياً عليه أصلاً بالجنون العارض (له يعني سلب ولاية أحد على الصغير بالجنون العارض ،له ولا يخفى أن التوجيه الأول أظهر الكل (وقد يمثل بتعليل نجاسة لعاب الخنزير بالاستقذار) أي عده قذراً (فيقاس عليه العرق وهو) أي الاستقذار (متأخر عنها) لأن عده قذراً بعد العلم بنجاسته (ورد) الشيخ ابن الهمام بأنه أي التأخر (غير لازم للجواز المقارنة بينهما (أقول : الاستقذار طبعاً متقدم على نجاسة اللعاب، لكنه لم يظهر عليته (و) الاستقذار (شرعاً متأخر عن النجاسة (ولو رتبة لأن الطاهر لا يستقذر) شرعاً، فليس ههنا استقذار مقارن (فافهم لنا : لو تأخرت العلة عن شرع الحكم لم يكن شرعه لها أي لم تكن مشروعية الحكم لأجل العلة، فلم تبق العلة علة هذا خلف (واستدل) على المختار أيضاً (لو تأخرت العلة عن الحكم (ثبت) الحكم ( بلا باعث لوجوده قبل الباعث بالفرض (أقول) هذا الاستدلال مبني على امتناع التعليل بعلتين وإلا فالملازمة ممنوعة لجواز أن يكون الحكم معللاً بباعثين يوجد بأحدهما ثم يوجد الباعث الآخر فافهم .
(And from them) i.e. from the conditions of the 'illah (is that it is not delayed from the ruling of the origin, like the causation of the father's guardianship over the minor's wealth who has been afflicted with insanity by insanity, so that the insane person is joined to him in the right of marriage due to sharing insanity. And al-Sharih al-Mukhtasar gave an example: causation of removing guardianship from the minor by the insanity that befalls the guardian. And it is not hidden what is in it, for there is no meaning to the 'illah of the guardian's insanity because the minor is not a guardian and there is no suitability at all. So it was said that it is a substitution of the explicit for the implicit, and the meaning is: removal of guardianship from the minor who has been afflicted with insanity by his insanity. And it was said: his expression 'guardian' is not free from turbidity. Rather, the meaning is that the removal of guardianship from the insane minor is caused by the insanity that is present in the adult guardian, analogized to the insane minor in the removal of guardianship. (I say: that is further from understanding, contrary to the intended meaning, because what is sought here is the occurrence in the origin, in the context of giving an example, and he did not mention it in the branch. So what is sought is not mentioned, and what is mentioned is not what is sought. Rather, the meaning is that the removal of the guardian's guardianship over the minor, i.e., that he is not a guardian over him at all, is caused by the insanity that befalls him, i.e., the removal of guardianship of someone over the minor by the insanity that befalls him. And it is not hidden that the first interpretation is the most apparent of all. And it may be exemplified by causation of the najasah of a pig's saliva by istiqdhar, i.e., considering it impure, so that sweat is analogized to it, and istiqdhar is delayed from it because considering it impure comes after knowledge of its najasah. And Shaykh Ibn al-Humann refuted that the delay is not necessary, because contemporaneity is possible between them. I say: natural istiqdhar precedes the najasah of saliva, but its being a cause is not apparent, and legal istiqdhar is delayed from najasah even in rank, because the pure is not considered impurely legally, so there is no contemporaneous istiqdhar here. So understand. For us: if the 'illah is delayed from the legislation of the ruling, then its legislation is not because of the 'illah, so the 'illah does not remain a cause; this is a contradiction. And it is also argued for the chosen view: if the 'illah is delayed from the ruling, the ruling is established without a motivator for its existence, because the motivator exists by assumption before [the ruling]. I say: This argument is based on the impossibility of causation by two causes; otherwise the entailment is denied because it is possible that the ruling is caused by two motivators, existing by one of them, then the other motivator exists later, so understand.)
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that the 'illah must not be temporally delayed from the ruling of the original case. The author provides several examples, including the use of insanity as an 'illah for removing guardianship, and the example of pig's saliva and sweat. The key point: if the 'illah occurs after the ruling, then the ruling would exist without a cause at its inception, which is untenable. The Hanafi position insists on contemporaneity, but allows for the 'illah to be a motivating factor only if it is present at the time. The case of pig's saliva illustrates the difficulty: is the legal impurity (najasah) the cause, or is the natural disgust? The Hanafi scholar Ibn al-Humann argues that the 'illah must be concurrent, but natural irritation may precede. Ultimately, the ruling must have a cause present at its genesis.

Key Terms
علة (illah): The effective cause or ratio legis in qiyas, which must be present at the time of the original ruling.
استقذار (istiqdhar): Considering something impure or disgusting; used as an example of a potential 'illah.
باعث (ba'ith): Motivating cause or rationale that prompts the legislation of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require that the 'illah not be delayed from the ruling of the origin?
A) Because the 'illah must be known before the ruling.
B) Because otherwise the ruling would exist without a motivating cause at its inception.
C) Because the 'illah must be textual, not rational.
D) Because delay negates suitability.

Key Takeaway
The 'illah must be contemporaneous with the original ruling; otherwise, it cannot be the legal cause of that ruling.

Condition of the 'Illah: It Must Not Invalidate Its Origin (Ibtal al-Asl)
Condition of 'illah: must not invalidate its own origin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an 'illah violates the condition of ibtal al-asl and apply the Hanafi criterion using the salam example.

Core Idea
An 'illah that invalidates the ruling of its own original text is itself invalid.

This lesson examines a key condition of the 'illah in Hanafi usul: the 'illah must not nullify the ruling of its own source (asl). We will see how this rule applies using the example of salam (advance payment for delayed delivery) and why the Shafi'i derivation for immediate salam is rejected by the Hanafis.

Guided Path
1
1. Understand the Condition
The 'illah derived for qiyas must not contradict or nullify the ruling of the original text (asl). If it does, the 'illah is invalid regardless of its logical appeal.
This condition preserves the authority of the primary texts (nusus).
2
2. Apply to the Salam Example
Examine the Shafi'i 'illah: they use the verse on writing debts and the hadith on salam to allow immediate delivery of the commodity. But the original ruling requires a known term (ajal). By allowing immediate salam, the 'illah invalidates that condition, violating ibtal al-asl.
The Hanafi critique is that the 'illah does not respect the original textual limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (أن لا يعود على أصله (بالإبطال أي لا يكون التعليل مبطلاً لحكم أصله (كتعليل الشافعية نص السلم وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنِ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُّسَمًّى فَاكْتُبُوهُ﴾ [البقرة: ۲۸۲] قال ابن عباس : نزلت في السلم وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «من أسلم فليسلم في كيل معلوم ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم (يخرج إحضار السلعة) فيجوز السلم الحال كالمؤجل المبطل للأجل المنصوص) في السلم
(And from them) i.e. from the conditions of the 'illah (is that it does not return upon its origin by invalidation), i.e. the causation should not invalidate the ruling of its origin. Like the Shafi'i causation of the text of salam, which is His statement: "O you who believe, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down" [al-Baqarah 2:282]. Ibn Abbas said: It was revealed about salam. And his saying, peace be upon him: "Whoever gives salam, let him give salam in a known measure, known weight, to a known term" (removes the requirement of bringing the commodity) so that immediate salam becomes permissible, like deferred [salam], invalidating the specified term in salam.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents the condition that the 'illah derived for qiyas must not invalidate the very ruling of the original text. The example given is from the Shafi'i school: they use the verse about writing debts and the hadith on salam as the 'illah for the permissibility of immediate salam (where the commodity is not yet present). But the original ruling (nass) specifies that salam must have a known term, implying deferred delivery. By using this 'illah to allow immediate salam, they invalidate the condition of a deferred term in the original ruling. The Hanafi position rejects any 'illah that would overturn the original legal requirement.

Key Terms
إبطال الأصل (ibtal al-asl): The condition that the 'illah must not nullify the ruling of the original case (asl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it relies on a weak hadith.
D) Because it uses a verse about writing debts incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
The 'illah must not invalidate the ruling of its original case; otherwise it is rejected in Hanafi usul.

The Condition of No Conflicting Attribute for the Derived 'Illah
Condition for derived 'illah: no conflicting attribute suitable as 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a derived 'illah is considered independent and when a composite 'illah is required, applying the Hanafi condition of no conflicting attribute.

Core Idea
A derived 'illah must be free of a conflicting attribute; if one exists, the 'illah becomes composite unless both are independently proven.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the derived 'illah must be carefully scrutinized. One critical condition is that it must not have a conflicting attribute (wasf mu'arid) that also qualifies as an 'illah in the same original case. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains how to handle such conflicts and the implications for the independence of the 'illah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
Identify that the derived 'illah must be free of any conflicting attribute that is also suitable to serve as an 'illah in the original case (asl).
This condition applies specifically to derived 'illahs, not textual ones.
2
Identify the exception when a conflict exists
If a conflicting attribute is present, the 'illah does not remain independent; instead, a composite 'illah (al-majmu' al-murakkab) combining both attributes may be used.
The composite 'illah is permitted, not required.
3
Recognize the further condition for independence
If each attribute has its own independent proof (dalil), then the rule of no independence may not apply; the ruling depends on whether multiple 'illahs are permissible.
This is a point of ikhtilaf; the Hanafi school generally permits multiple 'illahs.
4
Consider the ikhtilaf on multiple 'illahs
The passage indicates that the permissibility of multiple 'illahs is disputed. In the Hanafi school, multiple 'illahs are accepted under certain conditions, while other schools may reject them.
This dispute affects whether the original 'illah can remain independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (للمستنبطة) خاصة أن لا يكون لها) وصف (معارض صالح لمداخلته في العلية في الأصل وإلا أي إذا كان معارض (جاز التعليل بالمجموع المركب منها ومن المعارض فلم يبق مستقلاً (إلا أن يكون كل مستقلاً) بدلیله فحينئذ عدم الاشتراط والاشتراط موقوف على الخلاف في جواز تعدد العلل
(And from them) i.e. from the conditions of the 'illah (especially for the derived 'illah) is that it does not have a conflicting attribute suitable for interference in 'illah in the origin. Otherwise, i.e., if there is a conflicting attribute, then causation by the composite of both is permitted, so it does not remain independent, unless each is independent by its own proof. Then in that case, the absence of condition and the condition depend on the difference of opinion about the permissibility of multiple 'illahs.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses a condition specific to derived 'illahs (as opposed to textual ones). If there is another attribute in the original case that is equally suitable as an 'illah, then the 'illah cannot be considered independent unless each attribute is independently established. If both are suitable, the 'illah may be a composite of both attributes. However, if each attribute can independently serve as an 'illah, then the permissibility of having multiple 'illahs for the same ruling is a matter of scholarly dispute. The Hanafi school allows multiple 'illahs in some cases.

Key Terms
وصف معارض (wasf mu'arid): A conflicting attribute that also qualifies as an 'illah, challenging the independence of the original 'illah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an 'illah has a conflicting attribute suitable for 'illah in the origin, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The 'illah is completely invalid.
B) A composite 'illah combining both attributes may be used.
C) The conflicting attribute is ignored.
D) The original 'illah is strengthened.

Key Takeaway
The derived 'illah must be free of a conflicting attribute; if one exists, it becomes composite unless both are independently proven, in which case multiple 'illahs are permissible.

The Condition of the 'Illah's Proof Not Covering the Branch
Usul rule on the condition of 'illah proof and branch ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition regarding the 'illah's proof covering the branch, explain the Hanafi position rejecting it, and apply the reasoning to a new usul scenario.

Core Idea
Multiple proofs strengthen, not invalidate, qiyas: the 'illah's proof may already cover the branch.

In Hanafi usul, a condition was proposed that the proof of the 'illah in qiyas must not already directly cover the branch ruling (even by its generality). If it does, qiyas would seem redundant. However, the chosen view (mukhtar) rejects this condition. This lesson unpacks that reasoning, focusing on the epistemic benefit of multiple proofs and the role of qiyas in establishing 'illah.

Guided Path
1
State the proposed condition
Identify the condition that some usulis argue: the proof of the 'illah should not encompass the branch ruling directly, even by its generality.
This is the starting point of the discussion.
2
Understand the objection to qiyas
If the direct proof already covers the branch, then qiyas seems unnecessary and the causation ('illah) is lost. The conditioners argue that one should rely on the direct proof instead.
This is the argument for requiring the condition.
3
Identify the Hanafi chosen view
The Hanafi school does not require this condition. The passage states 'wal-mukhtar 'adamuh' (the chosen view is its absence).
This is the core Hanafi position.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof for rejecting the condition
The existence of another proof does not negate qiyas because multiple paths of knowledge are beneficial. Qiyas serves to establish the 'illah, not just the ruling. The objection of redundancy is not valid.
The phrase 'thubut al-far' bi kulli min al-qiyas wa hadha al-dalil' is key.
5
Address the conditioners' counter-argument
The conditioners claim that returning to the direct proof is a return from qiyas. The Hanafi response: the return is not from qiyas but to establish the 'illah, and the length of the argument is not harmful.
This step shows how the Hanafi school defends its position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة أن لا يكون دليلها متناولاً حكم الفرع ولو بعمومه) إذ حينئذ يكفي هذا الدليل لإثبات الفرع وضاع التعليل إلا عند النزاع في دخول الفرع (فيه) أو في كون عمومه حجة ونحوهما فإنه حينئذ يجوز إثبات الفرع بالعلة المثبتة بهذا الدليل لكون وجودها فيه أظهر (والمختار عدمه) أي عدم الاشتراط بهذا الشرط (لنا) المسلك ثابت ولا ينافي سلوكه وجود مسلك آخر، لأن تعيين الطريق ليس بواجب على المناظر، ولا يخيل المانع إلا انتفاء الفائدة مع التطويل وليس بمتحقق إذ تعدد طرق المعرفة من الفوائد فليس بتطويل بلا فائدة الشارطون (قالوا) في الرجوع إلى الدليل المتناول لحكم الفرع (رجوع عن القياس إليه فلا الرجوع (ممنوع، لأن (الثبوت أي ثبوت حكم الفرع ( بكل) من القياس، وهذا الدليل والرجوع في القياس إليه لإثبات العلية لا لإثبات الحكم غاية ما في الباب أن هذه المسافة أطول من مسافة الإثبات من الدليل ولا ضير فيه
(And from them) i.e. from the conditions of the 'illah is that its proof does not encompass the ruling of the branch even by its generality. For then this proof suffices for establishing the branch and the causation is lost, except in case of dispute about the entry of the branch into it or about the authority of its generality and the like. Then it may be permitted to establish the branch by the 'illah established by this proof because its existence in it is more apparent. And the chosen view is its absence, i.e., not requiring this condition. For us: the path is established and the existence of another path does not contradict it, because specifying the route is not obligatory for the disputant. And the obstacle is not imagined except the loss of benefit with prolongation, and that is not realized because multiple paths of knowledge are among the benefits, so it is not prolongation without benefit. The conditioners said: In returning to the proof that encompasses the branch ruling, it is a return from qiyas to it, so it is not a return. It is prevented, because the establishment of the branch ruling is by both the qiyas and this proof, and the return in qiyas to it is to establish the 'illah, not to establish the ruling. The upshot is that this distance is longer than the distance of establishment from the proof, and there is no harm in that.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that the 'illah's proof should not already cover the branch ruling through its general applicability. If it does, then qiyas becomes redundant. However, the chosen view (mukhtar) in the Hanafi school is that this condition is not required. The reason: multiple proofs are beneficial, and having both a direct proof and an 'illah is fine. The objection that one should then rely directly on the proof is rejected because qiyas establishes the 'illah, not the ruling. The length of the argument is not harmful. Thus, the Hanafi school allows qiyas even if a direct proof exists for the branch.

Key Terms
دليل متناول (dalīl mutanāwil): A proof that encompasses the branch ruling directly (by its generality).
شرط العلة (shart al-'illah): A condition that the 'illah must satisfy for qiyas to be valid.
الشارطون (al-shāriṭūn): The scholars who require this condition (the conditioners).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The condition is required to avoid redundancy.
B) The condition is not required; qiyas is still valid even if a direct proof covers the branch.
C) The condition is required only when the direct proof is general.
D) The condition is not required but qiyas is discouraged.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require that the 'illah's proof be exclusive of the branch ruling; multiple proofs are beneficial and qiyas establishes the 'illah, not just the ruling.
