Can a Legal Ruling Serve as an 'Illah? The Hanafi Position
Usul rule: permissibility of using hukm shar'i as 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on using a hukm shar'i as an 'illah, explain the Hanafi argument via the mudabbar example, and critically evaluate the hadith proof used.

Core Idea
A legal ruling can be an 'illah: the mudabbar's inalienability is derived from the umm walad's analogy.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul question: can a hukm shar'i itself serve as the effective cause ('illah) for another ruling? The Hanafi school answers in the affirmative, using the case of the mudabbar as a prime example. This illustrates how the madhhab extends rulings based on existing legal categories, not just natural attributes.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
Identify the three views: permissibility (mukhtar), conditional (benefit only), and absolute prohibition.
2
Examine the Hanafi example
Understand the mudabbar case: a slave whose emancipation is attached to death, and how its ruling (not sold) is derived from umm walad.
The 'illah is the legal status itself.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The Hanafis cite a hadith about Hajj and debt. Note the textual issue: the hadith is actually about a different woman. Consider the methodological impact.
Even if the hadith citation is flawed, the principle is supported by the mudabbar analogy.
4
Apply the principle
Given a new case, test whether a legal ruling can be used as 'illah, justifying your reasoning.
Focus on the type of 'illah (jaliyyah, khafiyyah, etc.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(مسألة) : اختلف في كون الحكم الشرعي علة لحكم شرعي آخر (المختار جواز كونها) أي العلة حكماً شرعياً، كقول الحنفية في المدير : مملوك تعلق عتقه بمطلق الموت) وهذا حكم شرعي ثبت بإيجابه فلا يباع كأم الولد وهذا أيضاً حكم شرعي معلل بالأول عندنا (وقيل) إنما يجوز كونها حكماً إن كان التعليل لجلب مصلحة وإن كان لدفع مفسدة فلا تكون حكماً شرعياً (وقيل : لا يجوز مطلقاً) سواء كان لجلب مصلحة أو دفع مفسدة (لنا ما عن (الخثعمية أنها ،قالت يا رسول الله أن أبي أدركه الحج وهو شيخ كبير لا يستمسك على راحلته، أفيجزئ أن أحج عنه ؟ فقال صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «أرأيت لو كان على أبيك دين دین فقضيته أما كان يقبل منك؟ قالت نعم قال : فدين الله أحق)» كذا في بعض كتب الأصول والذي يظهر بمراجعة كتب الحديث أنه لم يقل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أرأيت الخ: في حديث الخثعمية، بل فيه إجازة الحج عن أبيها، بل إنما كان هذا القول في جواب امرأة أخرى أمها وقالت أمي ضعيفة لا تستطيع أن تستمسك على الراحلة، أفيجزىء عنها أن أحج فقال : أرأيت الخ . . رواه الشيخان وفي حديث آخر أن رجلاً قال : إن أبي مات ولم سألته عن حج
(Issue): There is disagreement about whether a legal ruling can be an 'illah for another legal ruling. The chosen view is the permissibility of its being, i.e., the 'illah being a legal ruling, like the Hanafi statement about the mudabbar: a slave whose emancipation is attached to absolute death. And this is a legal ruling established by his obligation, so he is not sold, like umm walad. And this is also a legal ruling caused by the first according to us. And it was said: It is only permissible for it to be a ruling if the causation is for bringing benefit, but if it is for repelling harm, then it cannot be a legal ruling. And it was said: It is not permissible absolutely, whether for bringing benefit or repelling harm. Our proof: what is related from al-Khath'amiyyah that she said: O Messenger of Allah, my father has reached Hajj while he is an old man who cannot stay steady on his mount. Is it sufficient for me to perform Hajj on his behalf? He said: “Do you see that if your father had a debt and you paid it, would it be accepted from you?” She said: Yes. He said: “The debt of Allah is more worthy.”” Thus in some books of usul, but what appears upon reviewing hadith books is that the Messenger of Allah did not say “Do you see…” etc. in the hadith of al-Khath'amiyyah, but rather it includes permission for Hajj on behalf of her father. Rather, this statement was in response to another woman whose mother was ill. She said: My mother is weak, unable to stay steady on the mount. Is it sufficient for me to perform Hajj on her behalf? He said: “Do you see…” etc. It was narrated by al-Shaykhan. And in another hadith, a man said: My father died and I did not ask him about Hajj…
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) can serve as the 'illah for another legal ruling. The Hanafi school (mukhtar) permits this, illustrated by the case of the mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is contingent on the master's death): this ruling (that he cannot be sold) is caused by the ruling of umm walad (a slave mother of her master's child, who cannot be sold). Thus, one ruling (the legal status of mudabbar) is the 'illah for another ruling (prohibition of sale). Other views restrict this to cases of bringing benefit only, or prohibit it entirely. The Hanafi proof is from a hadith about Hajj on behalf of another, where the obligation of Hajj is analogized to debt (a legal ruling). However, the author notes a hadith accuracy issue: the specific hadith used is actually about a different woman. Nevertheless, the principle stands. This shows the Hanafi approach to using established legal rulings as grounds for extending rulings in qiyas.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (hukm shar'i): A legal ruling from the Sacred Law, which can itself be used as an 'illah for another ruling.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is suspended upon the master's death; a legal status that serves as an 'illah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, always, as in the case of mudabbar and umm walad.
C) Only if the causation is for repelling harm.
D) Only if the ruling is from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits using a hukm shar'i as an 'illah, as exemplified by the analogy between mudabbar and umm walad.

The Mustanbata 'Illa Must Not Add to the Text: A Hanafi Usul Condition
Lays out a condition for qiyas and contrasts Hanafi and Shafi'i views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that the mustanbata 'illa must not add to the text, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding takhsis and taqyid.

Core Idea
A derived cause cannot add to the text — doing so would be abrogation, which qiyas cannot perform.

In this passage, the author lays out a critical condition for the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) in the Hanafi school: the effective cause derived by the mujtahid (al-illa al-mustanbata) must not necessitate any addition to the text being analogized. The reason is that adding to the text would constitute abrogation (naskh) or alteration, and qiyas, being subordinate to the text, cannot effect such a change. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi absolute prohibition with the Shafi'i position that additions are not abrogation and that qiyas can specify (takhsis) or restrict (taqyid) the text. The author notes a potential contradiction in the Shafi'i stance, as prohibiting addition entirely would seemingly forbid even the permissible takhsis and taqyid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its scope
Read the passage and locate the condition: the mustanbata 'illa must not necessitate any addition to the text, whether the addition is restricted (muqayyad) or contradictory (mukhalif). Note that this applies absolutely.
The phrase 'مطلقاً' indicates the condition is unconditional.
2
Understand the Hanafi rationale
Find the phrase 'لأنه نسخ وتغيير' (because it is abrogation and change). Explain why the Hanafi school considers any addition as tantamount to naskh. Qiyas is inferior to the text (هو دون النص) and therefore cannot override or add to it.
Naskh in Hanafi usul can only be effected by Quran, sunna, or ijma, not by qiyas.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The passage states that for the Shafi'is, 'لا توجب زيادة منافية' – they do not forbid addition per se. They allow additions as long as they are not contradictory? Actually, the text says 'فإن مطلق الزيادة ليست معتبرة عندهم' meaning they do not consider absolute addition as invalid. They permit takhsis of the general and taqyid of the absolute through qiyas.
The Shafi'i school holds that qiyas can specify the general (takhsis al-amm) and restrict the absolute (taqyid al-mutlaq), which are forms of addition.
4
Analyze the alleged contradiction
The author argues that prohibiting addition absolutely (the Hanafi view) would also forbid the permissible takhsis and taqyid, as pointed out by Ibn al-Hajib. Yet the Hanafi school does allow takhsis and taqyid by qiyas. This seems contradictory unless the Hanafi position is that only addition that is 'naskh-like' (changing the ruling) is prohibited, not mere specification or restriction.
The text says 'تناقض ظاهر' – apparent contradiction. How do you resolve it? The Hanafi response distinguishes between addition that abrogates (naskh) and addition that merely clarifies (bayan).
5
Derive the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: A Shafi'i mujtahid may derive a cause that adds a new condition or detail to a text-based ruling, whereas a Hanafi will reject such an addition as an invalid attempt at naskh. Consider the example from the text: qiyas on manumission (الإعتاق) – a Shafi'i might extend a ruling to a case that was not originally covered, adding a condition; a Hanafi would require the condition to be present in the text itself.
This illustrates how the condition shapes the scope of qiyas in each school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع/ القياس للنص الموجب تعديه عن الإعتاق وفي اليمين عن أحد الأمور الثلاثة، ولا يخفى أن هذا مبطل للأصل (و) منها (أن لا توجب العلة (المستنبطة) زيادة على النص مطلقاً مقيداً كان أو مخالفاً (عندنا، لأنه (نسخ) وتغيير (مطلقاً) فلا يجوز بالقياس الذي هو دون النص (و) لا توجب زيادة منافية عند الشافعية) فإن مطلق الزيادة ليست معتبرة عندهم ومنعها) أي الزيادة على النص (مطلقاً تجويز التخصيص) للعام (والتقييد) للمطلق بها كابن الحاجب) أي كما يمنع مع ابن الحاجب مطلقاً (تناقض) ظاهر
The fourth principle: qiyas upon the text requires its extension from manumission and in the oath from one of three matters, and it is not hidden that this invalidates the principle. And among them: that the derived cause must not necessitate an addition to the text, whether restricted or contradictory, according to us, because it is abrogation and change absolutely, so it is not permissible by qiyas which is inferior to the text. And they must not necessitate an addition that is contradictory according to the Shafi'is, for absolute addition is not considered by them. And prohibiting addition absolutely permits specification of the general and restriction of the absolute by it, like Ibn al-Hajib – that is, just as he prohibits absolutely, there is apparent contradiction.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage states a key condition for the derived effective cause (al-illa al-mustanbata) in Hanafi usul: it must not add anything to the original text. The reason is that adding would constitute abrogation (naskh) or change, which is not permissible by qiyas since qiyas has lower authority than the text. The Hanafi position is contrasted with the Shafi'i view, which generally allows additions. The author notes that prohibiting addition absolutely would contradict allowing specification (takhsis) and restriction (taqyid) by qiyas, as pointed out by Ibn al-Hajib, implying a logical inconsistency in the Shafi'i stance.

Key Terms
العلة المستنبطة (al-illa al-mustanbata): The effective cause derived by the mujtahid through ijtihad from the original case
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later revelation; analogical reasoning cannot effect abrogation
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification of a general term to exclude some of its instances
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction of an absolute term by adding a condition

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the mustanbata 'illa must not necessitate any addition to the text, as that would constitute abrogation (naskh) which is beyond the authority of qiyas.

Condition for the Derived Cause: No Conflicting Descriptor
State a condition for the derived cause regarding conflicting descriptors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conflicting descriptor (mu'arid) requires combining into a composite illa and when it does not, per Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A derived illa must have no conflicting descriptor; if one exists, combine into a composite cause unless each is independently proven.

This lesson examines a key condition for the derived cause (al-illa al-mustanbata): it must not have a conflicting descriptor (mu'arid) suitable for causality in the original case. If such a descriptor exists, the Hanafi school permits combining both into a composite cause. However, an exception arises if each descriptor is independently supported by evidence, leading to a deeper ikhtilaf about multiple causes. Understanding this condition prevents premature rejection of valid qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage states that among the conditions for the derived cause is that it should not have a conflicting descriptor (wasf mu'arid) suitable for entering into causality (mudakhalah fi al-'illiyah) in the original case.
The term mu'arid refers to a descriptor that could also serve as the effective cause.
2
Determine the ruling when a mu'arid exists
If a conflicting descriptor exists, it is permissible (jaza) to use a composite cause (al-ta'lil bil-majmu') that combines both the original and the conflicting descriptor. The cause then does not remain independent.
Composite cause means the ruling is attributed to both descriptors together.
3
Identify the exception
The passage adds an exception: if each descriptor is independent by its own evidence (kull mustaqill bi-dalilihi), then combining is not required. The permissibility of combination depends on the disagreement about multiple causes.
This exception acknowledges that some scholars allow multiple independent causes to coexist.
4
Understanding the underlying ikhtilaf
The final part of the passage notes that the need for combination or independence relates to the broader ikhtilaf about whether multiple causes can be valid for a single ruling. Hanafi usul allows composite causes as a solution.
This shows how a condition on the cause leads into a major theoretical dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة (للمستنبطة) خاصة أن لا يكون لها) وصف (معارض صالح لمداخلته في العلية في الأصل وإلا أي إذا كان معارض (جاز التعليل بالمجموع المركب منها ومن المعارض فلم يبق مستقلاً (إلا أن يكون كل مستقلاً) بدلیله فحينئذ عدم الاشتراط والاشتراط موقوف على الخلاف في جواز تعدد العلل
And among them – meaning from the conditions of the cause, particularly the derived one – is that it should not have a conflicting descriptor suitable for entering into causality in the original. Otherwise, if there is a conflicting one, it is permissible to use the composite of it and the conflicting descriptor, so it does not remain independent. Unless each is independent by its own evidence, then there is no requirement of combination, and whether combination is required depends on the disagreement about the permissibility of multiple causes.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage outlines a condition for the derived cause: there must not be another suitable descriptor (wasf) that could also serve as the effective cause in the original case. If such a conflicting descriptor exists, the jurist should combine both into a composite cause. However, if each descriptor is independently supported by evidence, then combining is not necessary. The issue of whether multiple causes can exist separately is subject to debate among scholars.

Key Terms
معارض (mu'arid): A conflicting descriptor that could also be the effective cause in the original case

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Reject the qiyas entirely
B) Use a composite cause combining both descriptors
C) Choose the stronger descriptor without combining
D) Abandon the analogy

Key Takeaway
A derived illa must not have a conflicting descriptor; if one exists, it is permissible to combine them into a composite cause, unless each is independently supported.

Condition: Evidence for the Cause Should Not Cover the Branch
Discusses condition that evidence for illa should not already include branch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disputed condition regarding qiyas and explain the Hanafi position that multiple proofs are permissible.

Core Idea
The evidence for the cause may also cover the branch; multiple paths of proof are a benefit, not a redundancy.

In qiyas, some scholars require that the evidence for the 'illa must not already include the branch ruling. The Hanafi school rejects this condition, allowing multiple proofs. This lesson explains the reasoning and refutes the objection that this constitutes a retreat from qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the condition
Identify the condition being debated: that the dalil (evidence) for the 'illa should not encompass the hukm of the far' (branch), even by its generality.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some scholars argue that if the text already covers the branch, qiyas is redundant and the reasoning is wasted. Understand their reasoning.
3
Present the Hanafi argument
The chosen Hanafi view (al-mukhtar) is that this condition is not required. The path of qiyas is established regardless of other paths. Multiple ways of knowing are beneficial, so prolongation is not without benefit.
4
Refute the objection
Address the claim that resorting to the text covering the branch is a retreat from qiyas. The retreat is only to establish causality, not the ruling itself. The ruling is proven by both qiyas and the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها) أي من شروط العلة أن لا يكون دليلها متناولاً حكم الفرع ولو بعمومه) إذ حينئذ يكفي هذا الدليل لإثبات الفرع وضاع التعليل إلا عند النزاع في دخول الفرع (فيه) أو في كون عمومه حجة ونحوهما فإنه حينئذ يجوز إثبات الفرع بالعلة المثبتة بهذا الدليل لكون وجودها فيه أظهر (والمختار عدمه) أي عدم الاشتراط بهذا الشرط (لنا) المسلك ثابت ولا ينافي سلوكه وجود مسلك آخر، لأن تعيين الطريق ليس بواجب على المناظر، ولا يخيل المانع إلا انتفاء الفائدة مع التطويل وليس بمتحقق إذ تعدد طرق المعرفة من الفوائد فليس بتطويل بلا فائدة الشارطون (قالوا) في الرجوع إلى الدليل المتناول لحكم الفرع (رجوع عن القياس إليه فلا الرجوع (ممنوع، لأن (الثبوت أي ثبوت حكم الفرع ( بكل) من القياس، وهذا الدليل والرجوع في القياس إليه لإثبات العلية لا لإثبات الحكم غاية ما في الباب أن هذه المسافة أطول من مسافة الإثبات من الدليل ولا ضير فيه
And among them: that the evidence for the cause should not encompass the ruling of the branch, even by its generality. Because then this evidence would suffice to prove the branch and the reasoning is wasted, except when there is dispute about the branch's inclusion in it or about the generality being proof etc., then it is permissible to prove the branch by the cause established by this evidence because its presence in it is more apparent. The chosen view is non-necessity of this condition. We maintain: the path is established and the existence of another path does not negate following it, because specifying the path is not obligatory for the debater, and the only prevention is lack of benefit with prolongation, which is not realized, since multiplicity of ways of knowledge is beneficial, so it is not prolongation without benefit. They said: resorting to the evidence that encompasses the branch ruling is a retreat from qiyas to it. We say: the retreat is not admitted, because the establishment of the branch ruling is by both qiyas and this evidence, and the retreat in qiyas is to establish the causality, not to establish the ruling. The most is that this path is longer than the path of proof from the evidence, and there is no harm in that.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition that the evidence (dalil) for the 'illa should not already cover the branch ruling, even generally. Some argue that if the text already includes the branch, qiyas is unnecessary. However, the chosen (mukhtar) view in the Hanafi school is that this condition is not required. The proof is that having multiple paths to knowledge is beneficial, and the opponent's objection that it is a retreat from qiyas is refuted: the retreat is only to establish causality, not the ruling itself, and the longer path is not harmful.

Key Terms
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen or preferred view in the Hanafi school on this issue
تعليل (ta'lil): Reasoning by cause; the process of establishing a ruling through the 'illa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that qiyas is redundant if the text already covers the branch?
A) Qiyas becomes invalid because it is unnecessary
B) Multiple paths of knowledge are beneficial, so qiyas is valid
C) The objection is correct, so qiyas must be abandoned
D) The text must be reinterpreted

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require that the evidence for the cause be exclusive of the branch; multiple proofs are permissible and beneficial.

Can a Shar'i Ruling Be the 'Illa for Another Ruling?
Debates whether a hukm shar'i can serve as the illa for another hukm shar'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a hukm shar'i can be an illa, identify the opposing arguments regarding temporal sequence, and evaluate the Hanafi response of choosing simultaneity and suitability.

Core Idea
A shar'i ruling can itself be the effective cause for another ruling – the Hanafi view based on the debt analogy in the hadith.

This lesson explores a key usul dispute: can a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) function as the effective cause (illa) for another ruling? The Hanafi school answers yes, while others restrict illa to descriptive attributes. Understanding this dispute sharpens your awareness of how different schools conceptualize causality in law. We will examine the Hanafi proof from the Khath'amiyyah hadith, the opposing temporal sequence objection, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
State the central question: is a hukm shar'i (e.g., the ruling that a debt is owed) eligible to be the illa for another hukm shar'i (e.g., the permissibility of paying on behalf of another)?
The dispute is about the nature of the illa – must it be a physical attribute or can it be a ruling itself?
2
State the Hanafi position and proof
The Hanafi chosen view (al-mukhtar) is permissibility. They cite the hadith where the Prophet analogized the obligation of Hajj to a debt: the ruling 'debt' is the illa for the validity of paying it for another.
The hadith: 'Do you not think that if your father owed a debt and you paid it, it would be accepted from him? The debt of Allah is more worthy.'
3
Trace the opposing argument
Opponents argue that if a ruling is a cause, temporal sequence forces problems: if the cause precedes the effect, the effect would be non-existent (naqd); if it follows, the effect precedes its cause; if simultaneous, there is no reason to prefer one as cause over the other (tahakkum).
This is a logical objection based on the temporal relationship between cause and effect.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response chooses the third option (simultaneity) and denies tahakkum by appealing to munasaba (suitability). A ruling is suitable to be the cause because it points to a meaning (ma'na) that the Lawgiver considers as the rationale. They also use ijma (consensus) to argue that the second ruling is discovered later but its obligation was already established from the time of the first.
Example: the ruling of a mudabbir (slave with deferred manumission) cannot be sold, and this ruling is the illa for the same prohibition for umm al-walad.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis can derive new rulings by analogizing from existing shar'i rulings (not just from attributes), expanding the scope of qiyas.
This affects how a mujtahid identifies the illa in many cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أيضاً حكم شرعي معلل بالأول عندنا (وقيل) إنما يجوز كونها حكماً إن كان التعليل لجلب مصلحة وإن كان لدفع مفسدة فلا تكون حكماً شرعياً (وقيل : لا يجوز مطلقاً) سواء كان لجلب مصلحة أو دفع مفسدة (لنا ما عن (الخثعمية أنها ،قالت يا رسول الله أن أبي أدركه الحج وهو شيخ كبير لا يستمسك على راحلته، أفيجزئ أن أحج عنه ؟ فقال صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «أرأيت لو كان على أبيك دين دین فقضيته أما كان يقبل منك؟ العكس (أقول: على أن الحكم (الثاني) المتأخر (يجوز أن يكون (إجماعياً ثابتاً بالإجماع على علية الأول) فحينئذ نختار الشق الأول ونقول : إنما تثبت عليته حين تحقق الإجماع عليها وعنده ثبت الثاني (فلا نقض وأنت ثم بعد الحين بوحي آخر، فحينئذ دخل في الشق الثالث ولزم التحكم، أو لم يتعلق بل إنما يتعلق بنزول الوحي الآخر، فحينئذ لا يصح استنباطه الأول ولزم النقض فافهم. ولك أن تجيب أيضاً باختبار الأوّل وتمنع استحالة التخلف لجواز المانع فتدبر.
Issue: They differed on whether a shar'i ruling can be an effective cause for another shar'i ruling. The chosen view is permissibility of it (the cause) being a shar'i ruling, like the Hanafi statement regarding the mudabbir: a slave whose manumission is attached to absolute death – this is a shar'i ruling established by his obligation, so he cannot be sold, like umm al-walad, and this is also a shar'i ruling made conditional upon the first according to us. And it was said: it is permissible only if the reasoning is to bring benefit, but if to repel harm, then it cannot be a shar'i ruling. And it was said: not permissible absolutely, whether for benefit or harm. Our proof is the hadith of al-Khath'amiyyah... and after all, he (peace be upon him) indicated that the cause for the validity of payment is the thing becoming a debt upon the liability, which is a shar'i ruling, so understand. Those who elaborate said: if it were not to bring benefit, it would be to repel harm arising from it, and this is false because the shar'i ruling does not give rise to harm... (continues with arguments and responses).

Commentary
This extensive discussion addresses whether a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) itself can serve as the effective cause (illa) for another ruling. The Hanafi chosen view is that it can, as exemplified by the mudabbir (a slave whose freedom is tied to the master's death) being treated like umm al-walad (mother of a child) in that neither can be sold. The Hanafi proof includes the hadith where the Prophet analogized the obligation of Hajj to a debt, showing that the shar'i ruling of being a debt is the cause. Opponents raise issues of temporal sequence, arguing that the cause must precede or accompany the effect, leading to paradoxes. The Hanafi response includes choosing the third temporal option (simultaneity), arguing suitability (munasaba) determines causality. Additional responses involve ijma and the concept of the second ruling being discovered later. The passage ends with critical analysis and counterarguments.

Key Terms
الحكم الشرعي (al-hukm al-shar'i): A legal ruling from the Lawgiver, which in this context is considered as a potential effective cause
المناسبة (al-munāsaba): Suitability or appropriateness of a meaning to be the cause of a ruling; used to determine the illa in Hanafi usul.

The Permissibility of a Composite 'Illa in Qiyas
Affirms the Hanafi view that the illa can be composite, with rational proof and an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on composite 'illa and apply the rational proof to explain why a composite cause is permissible in qiyas.

Core Idea
A composite 'illa is permissible; intentional aggressive killing proves it.

This passage tackles a key methodological question in Hanafi usul: can the effective cause (illa) be composed of multiple attributes? While some scholars insist on a single simple attribute, the Hanafi school defends the permissibility of a composite 'illa. The argument is both rational and empirical, as demonstrated by the example of intentional aggressive killing. Understanding this concept is crucial for correctly applying qiyas in complex cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Some scholars reject a composite 'illa; the Hanafi chosen view permits it. Recognize that the passage presents an ikhtilaf.
The opposing school is not named here, but it is a known position.
2
Extract the rational proof
The text argues that there is no rational impossibility in a composite being conjectured as a cause, just like a simple cause is conjectured. Focus on the phrase 'لا يمتنع عقلاً'.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) relying on the absence of impossibility.
3
Analyze the example
Intentional aggressive killing (qatl al-'amd al-'udwan) is given as a real instance of a composite cause. It combines intent and aggression, both necessary for qisas.
The example proves existence, which shows that composition is not merely possible but actual.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
The opposing view requires simplicity. Understand that the thamarah al-khilaf is whether a composite set of attributes can serve as an illa in qiyas.
The Hanafi view allows more flexibility in deriving rulings.
5
Apply to a new hypothetical
Consider a case like 'selling alcohol in the marketplace to Muslims' – can the illa be composite? Use the rational proof to argue yes, if the combination is suitable for hukm.
This tests understanding of the rational possibility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلف في جواز تركب العلة فذهب البعض إلى أنه لا يجوز تركبها و(المختار جواز كونها مركبة لنا لا يمتنع عقلاً كون المجموع المركب من عدة مفاهيم (مما يظن عليته بمسلكها (كالبسيطة فإنها تظن بمسالكها والحاصل دعوى البديهية في الإمكان ثم بين الوجود، وقال (كيف) لا يصح وقد وقع تركب العلة كالقتل العمد العدوان) فإنه علة
Issue: They differed on the permissibility of the composition of the cause. Some held that it is not permissible, and the chosen view is the permissibility of it being composite. For us, it is not rationally impossible for the total composed of several concepts to be conjectured as a cause by its ways, like the simple cause, for it is conjectured by its ways. The gist is the claim of obviousness in possibility, then existence. And he said: how could it be invalid when the composite cause has occurred, like intentional aggressive killing, for it is a cause...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This short passage addresses whether the effective cause (illa) can be composed of multiple attributes (tarkib). Some scholars reject composition, but the Hanafi chosen view accepts it. The rational proof is that there is no impossibility in a composite being conjectured as a cause, just as a simple cause is conjectured. The example given is intentional aggressive killing (qatl al-'amd al-'udwan), which is a composite of 'amd (intent) and 'udwan (aggression) and serves as the cause for the ruling of qisas or diyah. The text cuts off but implies further discussion.

Key Terms

مسالك العلة (masalik al-illa): The recognized methods for deducing the effective cause in qiyas
القتل العمد العدوان (qatl al-'amd al-'udwan): Intentional aggressive killing, the example of a composite cause

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Can a Legal Ruling Serve as a Cause for Another Ruling?
Debate on whether a hukm shari can be an illa for another hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that a hukm shari can serve as an 'illa for another ruling, and identify the logical objections and responses.

Core Idea
A hukm shari can be an illa for another hukm when it is suitable (munasib) or supported by ijma.

This passage delves into a core usul dispute: can a legal ruling itself function as the effective cause for another ruling? The Hanafi school affirms this with conditions, while some absolute deniers reject it based on temporal paradoxes. The discussion is anchored in a hadith about performing Hajj on behalf of a deceased parent, where the debt obligation (a ruling) becomes the 'illa for substitution.

Guided Path
1
State the disputed issue
The question is whether one hukm shari can be the 'illa for another hukm in qiyas.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafis permit it with suitability (munasaba) or consensus, citing the Hajj hadith and the example of hadd penalties.
The hadith from Nasa'i is used as a primary proof.
3
Understand the absolute deniers' objection
They argue that temporal precedence, delay, or coincidence lead to inconsistency (naqd) or arbitrariness (tahakkum).
Their objection is based on logical necessity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi responses
Three responses: choose coincidence with munasaba, invoke consensus to validate causality, or distinguish delay in revelation from delay in ruling.
The third response is critiqued by the author.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect is that qiyas with a ruling as 'illa is valid in Hanafi usul, expanding the scope of analogical reasoning.
e.g., qiyas of selling impure items on selling wine.

لعلية أحدهما للآخر من العكس (والجواب) اختيار الشق الثالث، ومنع التحكم للمناسبة في أحدهما) يعني بكون أحدهما وصفاً مناسباً باعثاً على شرع الآخر، فتعين للعلية كبطلان البيع للنجاسة) فإن النجاسة مناسبة لبطلان إحرازها بالبيع دون العكس (أقول: على أن الحكم (الثاني) المتأخر (يجوز أن يكون (إجماعياً ثابتاً بالإجماع على علية الأول) فحينئذ نختار الشق الأول ونقول : إنما تثبت عليته حين تحقق الإجماع عليها وعنده ثبت الثاني (فلا نقض وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن الإجماع كاشف عن حكم ثابت من قبل، فللمستدل أن يقول : إن الحكم الأوّل إن كان علة فعليته من قبل، وإن ظهر بالإجماع بعده فالحكم الثاني أيضاً قبل مقارن، وإن كان انكشافه بالإجماع الآن، فالتحكم وإلا فالنقض فافهم (مع أن اللازم) ههنا (التخلف) أي تخلف الثاني عن الأوّل في النزول لا في الحكم فإنه كان يجوز استنباط هذا الحكم الثاني من الأوّل من وقت نزوله، لكن نزوله بعد متخلفاً عنه لتقرير ما كان يستنبط بالتعليل، وهذا ليس من التخلف في شيء، وهذا أيضاً جواب باختيار الأول (فافهم) وأنت تعلم فساده فإن هذا الحكم الذي كان جائز الاستنباط هل تعلق بالمكلف من حين تعلق الأوّل، وإن كان الكشف بالاستنباط ثم بعد الحين بوحي آخر، فحينئذ دخل في الشق الثالث ولزم التحكم، أو لم يتعلق بل إنما يتعلق بنزول الوحي الآخر، فحينئذ لا يصح استنباطه الأول ولزم النقض فافهم. ولك أن تجيب أيضاً باختبار الأوّل وتمنع استحالة التخلف لجواز المانع فتدبر .
He asked: Should I perform Hajj on his behalf? He said: What do you think if your father had a debt... etc. Narrated by Nasa'i. After this and that, the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated that the cause for the validity of substitution is that the thing becomes a debt on the conscience, which is a legal ruling. So understand. The detailers (mufassilun) said: Even if it is not for bringing benefit, it would be for repelling harm arising from it, and that is false because a legal ruling cannot be a source of harm. And they prevented by denying the implication, based on the permissibility of it including a lesser harm and a predominant benefit for which it was legislated. Then it is necessary to repel that lesser harm to complete the wisdom, so it is repelled by another ruling like the punishment for adultery. The preservation of lineage was legislated, which is the benefit of its legislation, but it leads to the destruction of souls because of stoning, which is an inherent harm. So it is repelled by exaggeration in its proof by requiring four witnesses and not accepting confession except four times in four sessions, and by dropping due to doubts. Thus the wisdom was completed. The absolute deniers (nafun mutlqan) said: If a ruling were a cause for another ruling, then it must either precede, follow, or coincide with its effect, and all are invalid. If the cause-ruling precedes its effect, that necessitates inconsistency (naqd); if it follows its effect, that necessitates delay which was already disproven; if it coincides with its effect, that necessitates arbitrariness because each is a legal ruling and there is no priority for one being the cause of the other over the opposite. The answer chooses the third option and denies arbitrariness because of suitability in one of them, meaning one is an appropriate attribute that prompts the legislation of the other, so it is designated as the cause, like the invalidity of sale due to impurity. Impurity is suitable for invalidating acquisition by sale, not the opposite. I say: As for the second later ruling, it may be established by consensus on the causality of the first, so then we choose the first option and say that its causality is established when consensus is realized on it, and at that point the second is established, so no inconsistency. And you are not unaware that consensus reveals a pre-existing ruling. So the proponent can say: If the first ruling is a cause, its causality existed earlier, even if revealed by consensus later, then the second ruling also existed earlier in comparison; but if its revelation by consensus is now, then arbitrariness, otherwise inconsistency, so understand. Similarly, the necessary consequence here is delay, meaning the delay of the second relative to the first in revelation, not in ruling. Because it was possible to derive this second ruling from the first from the time of its revelation, but its revelation after it being delayed from it for the sake of confirming what was derived by reasoning, and this is not delay at all. This is also an answer by choosing the first. Understand, and you know its flaw: this ruling that was possible to derive — did it become incumbent on the mukallaf from the time the first became incumbent? If the discovery was by derivation and then later by another revelation, then it falls into the third category and arbitrariness is necessitated; or it did not become incumbent but only becomes incumbent by the revelation of the other revelation, then the first derivation is not valid and inconsistency is necessitated. Understand. And you can also answer by choosing the first and denying the impossibility of delay because of the possibility of an impediment, so ponder.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether a legal ruling (hukm shari) can serve as the effective cause ('illa) for another legal ruling in qiyas. The text begins by citing a hadith from al-Nasa'i about performing Hajj on behalf of another, which the author uses to show that the cause of the validity of substitution is the conversion of the obligation into a debt on the conscience—a hukm shari. This establishes that a ruling can indeed function as an 'illa. The 'mufassilun' (detailers, likely the proponents) then argue that even if a ruling does not directly bring benefit, it may repel harm, and they counter the objection that a ruling cannot produce harm by distinguishing between a lesser harm and a predominant benefit. They give the example of the hadd penalty for adultery: the ruling preserves lineage (benefit) but leads to execution (harm), so the harm is mitigated by strict evidentiary requirements and dropping penalties due to doubts. The absolute deniers (nafun mutlqan) object that if a ruling were a cause, it must either precede, follow, or coincide with its effect—all impossible. The author presents three answers: first, choosing the third option (coincidence) and denying arbitrariness by pointing to suitability (munasaba) in one attribute, as in the case of impurity invalidating sale. Second, a rejoinder that the later ruling may be established by consensus (ijma') on the causality of the first, so that the causality is affirmed at the time of consensus, thus no inconsistency. Third, distinguishing between delay in revelation versus delay in ruling: the second ruling could be derived from the first from the start, and its later revelation only confirms that derivation, so no real delay. The author then critiques these responses and leaves room for further reflection, ultimately suggesting that an external impediment (mani') can justify delay. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes the Hanafi position that suitability and consensus can validate a hukm as an 'illa, while carefully addressing logical objections.

Key Terms
العلة (al-‘illa): الوصف المناسب الباعث على تشريع الحكم
المناسبة (al-munasaba): كون الوصف مناسباً للحكم بحيث يصلح باعثاً عليه
النقض (al-naqd): تخلف الحكم عن العلة أو العلة عن الحكم

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a hukm shari can serve as an 'illa for another hukm, provided there is suitability (munasaba) or consensus (ijma), which overcomes logical objections of temporal paradox.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
6 min
Permissibility of Composite Cause (Illa Murakkaba) in Qiyas
Arguments for and against composite illa in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on composite illa and respond to the two main objections against it.

Core Idea
Composite cause is valid: multiple attributes form a single notional cause; absence of part does not invalidate if an impediment exists.

This passage tackles a foundational question in usul al-fiqh: can the effective cause (illa) in qiyas be composed of multiple attributes? The Hanafi school affirms this, while some other schools deny it. Through a careful analysis of objections and responses, the text defends the composite illa using concepts of notional unity (ittihad i'tibari) and impediment (mani'). By studying this debate, you will strengthen your ability to evaluate arguments about causality in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Recognise that the chosen (mukhtar) view is that composite illa is permissible. Note the examples given: intentional murder with transgression for qisas, and intentional violation of the fast for kaffarah.
2
State the first objection and its rationale
The objectors argue that if the composite is the cause, causality must subsist either in each part, one part, or the whole. Each option fails: the first makes each part a separate cause; the second makes the composite redundant; the third requires a unifying aspect leading to regress.
3
Explain the Hanafi response using notional unity
The Hanafis respond that causality subsists in the composite as a whole, which has a notional unity (ittihad i'tibari) due to a notional form (hay'a i'tibariyya) incidental to the combination. This does not lead to regress because causality itself is notional and can attach to multiplicity directly.
The text states: 'العلية اعتبارية فيجوز أن يتصف بها الكثرة من حيث هي كثرة'.
4
State the second objection about naqd
The objectors argue that if the cause is composite, the absence of any part should negate the cause, leading to inconsistencies (naqd). For example, if urine (first impurity) and madhy (second impurity) occur, the second should not cause impurity because the effect already exists—a contradiction.
5
Explain the Hanafi response using impediment and possibility condition
The Hanafis respond that the lack of effect is due to an impediment (mani'), namely the prior existence of the effect. Once impurity exists, the possibility (imkan) for a new cause to act is lost. Alternatively, the true cause is the absence of a specific part, not any part. Thus, no genuine naqd occurs.
The text emphasises that 'التخلف لمانع لا يقدح في اقتضاء المقتضى'.

Source Text

والحاصل دعوى البديهية في الإمكان ثم بين الوجود، وقال (كيف) لا يصح وقد وقع تركب العلة كالقتل العمد العدوان) فإنه علة لوجوب القصاص، والهتك على صوم الشهر المبارك عمداً لوجوب الكفارة وهكذا. المنكرون (قالوا : أولاً لو كان المجموع علة فقيام العلية إما بكل جزء منه أو بواحد أو بالمجموع، والأقسام باطلة، لأنه إن قامت العلية بكل جزء منه (فكل) منها (علة) لا المجموع (أو) إن قامت بواحد فهو (العلة لا المجموع (أو) قامت (بالجميع من حيث هو جميع) فإذاً لا يصح قيام الواحد بالكثير فلا بد من جهة وحدة) بحسبها يصير المحل واحداً (فالكلام) عائد (فيها) كالكلام في العلية وتسلسل والحمل) أنا نختار الثالث ونقول يعني يجوز أن يكون وصف العلية التامة مركباً من العليات النواقص، ويكون الكل قائماً بالمجموع وأجزاؤه بأجزائه ومعنى قيام الجميع بالجميع قيام الأجزاء بالأجزاء فتدبر . وأما الجواب عن استدلالهم (بأنها ليست وإن كان هذا الوصف بالجعل من الشارع فافهم (و) قالوا : (ثانياً لو تركب الوصف لكان عدم كل جزء علة لانتفائها) ضرورة أن عدم سبب لعدم المركب ويلزم النقض بعدم ثان بعد عدم (أول) مستلزم للعدم المركب، وعدم استلزام هذا العدم الثاني له لاستحالة انعدام المعدوم) فقد وجد عدم جزء، ولم يوجد بحذائه عدم المجموع فلزم النقض، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن البول إذا خرج فقد وجد الحدث ، ثم إذا خرج بعد ذلك المذي فلم يوجد حدث لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود والمثلين فلزم النقض والحل أن التخلف ههنا المانع وهو الحصول بعلة أخرى والتخلف لمانع لا يقدح في اقتضاء المقتضى (والسر) في مانعيه الحصول بعلة أخرى أن الإمكان (شرط) في تأثير العلة، يعني أن تساوي نسبة الطرفين مع قطع النظر عن تأثير المؤثر شرط ( والضرورة) قبل تأثيره (ولو بالعلة تنافيه أي الإمكان بالتأويل المذكور (أقول : ولك أن تقول في الجواب (العلة عدم كل جزء من الأجزاء بدلاً (أولاً) وبالذات يعني علة العدم للمركب عدم جزء من أجزائه، بل عدم علة ما من علله، وإنما يطلق على كل واحد واحد من أعدام الأجزاء، لأنه متحقق في ضمنها، فلم يلزم النقض والتخلف عما هو علة حقيقة (فافهم) وإذا تأملت حق التأمل أيقنت أن الجواب حقيقة هو هذا، وأجيب في «المختصر بأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الجزء عدم شرط العلية بأن يكون وجود الجزء شرطاً لها، وأورد عليه أن الكلام في تركب العلة من الأوصاف فلا يكون أنه الجزء
Issue: There is disagreement about the permissibility of a composite cause. Some hold that it is not permissible, and the chosen view is that it is permissible for us (Hanafis) because it is not rationally impossible for a composite of several concepts to be considered a cause through its appropriate paths, like a simple cause which is considered through its paths. The conclusion is a claim of self-evidence in possibility, then he showed existence, and said: How could it be invalid when composite causes have occurred, such as intentional murder with transgression as the cause for the obligation of qisas, and intentional violation of the sacred month's fast as the cause for the obligation of kaffarah, and so on. The deniers said: First, if the composite is the cause, then the establishment of causality is either by each of its parts, or by one part, or by the composite. All these divisions are invalid because if causality is established by each part, then each part is a cause, not the composite. If by one part, then that part is the cause, not the composite. If by the composite as a whole, then it is not possible for one thing to subsist in many without a unifying aspect. So speech returns to that unifying aspect, leading to a regress. The burden (of proof) is on us: We choose the third option and say that causality subsists in the composite that has become one notionally, by virtue of a notional form incidental to the combination, which does not lead to a regress because there is no need for another form, so reflect on it. I say: Since causality is notional, it is permissible for multiplicity as such to be described by it without needing one aspect. And you should know that the first answer is not complete without this addition, because one could say: The occurrence of the form requires the locus to be one, so some kind of unity is necessary, leading to regress. So one must accept the occurrence of the form to pure multiplicity, which is the second answer, so understand. Moreover, the composite cause is a sum of partial causes, each part being necessary for the effect in some way, so it is permissible for each part to have partial causality. That is, the attribute of complete causality can be composed of partial causalities, and the whole subsists in the composite, and its parts in its parts. The meaning of the whole subsisting in the whole is the parts subsisting in the parts, so ponder. As for the answer to their argument that causality is not an attribute of the composite description itself, but rather an attribute of the Lawgiver related to it, meaning He decreed the ruling accordingly, as in al-Mukhtasar, its weakness and feebleness are clear because causing and being made by the Lawgiver is indeed an attribute of the description, although this description is by the Lawgiver's act, so understand. And they said: Secondly, if the description were composite, then the absence of each part would be a cause for the absence of the composite, necessarily because non-existence of the cause leads to non-existence of the effect. This leads to inconsistency (naqd) when a second absence occurs after a first absence that necessitates the composite absence, but the second absence does not necessitate it because non-existence cannot be annihilated. So the absence of a part occurred, but correspondingly the absence of the composite did not occur, thus inconsistency. And you should know that when urine exits, impurity (hadath) occurs; then when madhy exits afterward, no impurity occurs because it is impossible to bring about what already exists, leading to inconsistency. The solution is that the lack of effect here is due to an impediment (mani‘), which is the occurrence by another cause. A lack of effect due to an impediment does not negate the efficacy of the effective cause. The secret behind the impediment being occurrence by another cause is that possibility is a condition for the influence of the cause, meaning the equal relation of the two states regardless of the influence of the effect is a condition, and necessity before its influence (even by a cause) contradicts this possibility as interpreted. I say: You can also say in response: The cause of the absence of each part alternatively and essentially — meaning the cause of the absence of the composite is the absence of one of its parts, but rather the absence of any of its causes. The term 'absence of each part' is used because it is realized within it, so inconsistency does not arise, and delay from what is truly the cause does not occur. If you truly reflect, you will be certain that this is the true answer. And in al-Mukhtasar, it is answered that it is permissible for the absence of the part to be the absence of a condition of causality, i.e., the existence of the part is a condition for it. But it is objected that the discussion is about the composition of the cause from attributes, so the part cannot be that.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether the 'illa (effective cause) in qiyas can be composite (murakkab), i.e., consisting of multiple attributes together, or must be simple (basit). The text is a commentary (sharh) on a matn, likely al-Qur'an or al-Mukhtasar. The author states the chosen Hanafi view: composite 'illa is permissible and rationally possible. He provides examples: intentional murder with transgression ('amad wa 'udwan) as the composite cause for qisas (retaliation), and breaking the fast of Ramadan intentionally as the cause for kaffarah (expiation). These examples show that multiple attributes combine to form a single 'illa. The deniers raise two main arguments: first, if the composite is the cause, causality must either be in each part, one part, or the whole; each leads to problems. The response is that causality is in the composite as a whole, which has a notional unity (ittihad i'tibari) due to a notional form (hay'a i'tibariyya) arising from the combination, avoiding regress. This is possible because causality itself is a notional attribute that can attach to multiplicity. Second, the deniers argue that if the attribute is composite, the absence of any part would cause the absence of the composite, leading to inconsistencies (naqd). The response here uses the example of impurity: after urine causes impurity, later madhy does not cause impurity again because the effect already exists—this is due to an impediment (the existence of the effect by a prior cause). The secret is that possibility (imkan) is a condition for the cause's efficacy; once the effect is realized, possibility is lost, so the second cause cannot act. Alternatively, the true answer is that the absence of the entire composite is caused by the absence of any one part in a specific sense, not each part individually. The text also references al-Mukhtasar's answer that the absence of a part may be the absence of a condition, not the cause itself, but an objection is raised that the discussion concerns composition of attributes, not parts of a condition. The commentary follows the author's order, presenting each objection and response, and concludes that the final answer is sound.

Key Terms
العلة المركبة (al-‘illa al-murakkaba): مجموعة من الأوصاف المتعددة التي تكون معاً علة لحكم شرعي
الهيئة الاعتبارية (al-hay’a al-i‘tibariyya): صورة عقلية تعرض لمجموع الأوصاف بسبب اجتماعها، تجعلها كشيء واحد
النقض (al-naqd): تخلف الحكم عن العلة مع وجودها، أو تخلف العلة عن الحكم

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits composite illa in qiyas, relying on notional unity and the concept of impediment to preserve consistency.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced

The Hanafi Response to the Infinite Regress Objection on Compound 'Illa
Analyzing opponents' objection that compound 'illa requires a unifying aspect leading to infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi concept of considerational (i'tibari) unity resolves the objection that a compound 'illa leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
A compound 'illa is a considerational unity; no separate unifying aspect is needed, avoiding regress.

In this passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, the commentator defends the Hanafi position that a cause ('illa) can be composed of multiple descriptions without requiring a single physical unifying aspect. Opponents argued that without a unifying aspect, the causal relationship would lead to infinite regress. The Hanafi response introduces the concept of considerational form (hay'a i'tibariyya) to block the regress. This lesson walks through the objection, the Hanafi answer, and the key methodological concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponents argue that if a composite whole is the cause, then causality must stand either on each part (making each a separate cause), on one part (making that one the cause), or on the whole (but the whole cannot stand by many parts without a unifying aspect). This leads to a need for a unifying aspect, which itself requires another unifying aspect, resulting in infinite regress.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the impossibility of one attribute inhering in multiple loci.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi scholar chooses the third option: causality stands by the composite whole, but the whole is unified with a considerational unification (i'tibaran), not a real unity. The whole is a considerational form (hay'a i'tibariyya) that arises from the combination of parts.
This considerational form does not require a separate unifying aspect, thus stopping the regress.
3
Explain the concept of considerational unity
Considerational (i'tibari) means mentally constructed, not physically existent. Therefore, multiplicity can be described by a single considerational attribute without needing a real unifying aspect. The causality is itself considerational, so it can apply to the composite as a whole.
The commentator emphasizes that causality is i'tibariyya, so the multiplicity of parts does not pose a problem.
4
Evaluate the response and note the weaker answer
The commentator also mentions a second weaker answer: that 'illa is not a description of the composite but a description of the Lawgiver. He dismisses this as flimsy. The robust answer is the considerational unity.
Recognize which answer is relied upon (mufta bihi) in Hanafi usul.

Source Text

المنكرون قالوا: أولاً لو كان المجموع علة فقيام العلية إما بكل جزء منه أو بواحد أو بالمجموع، والأقسام باطلة، لأنه إن قامت العلية بكل جزء منه فكل منها علة لا المجموع أو إن قامت بواحد فهو العلة لا المجموع أو قامت بالجميع من حيث هو جميع فإذاً لا يصح قيام الواحد بالكثير فلا بد من جهة وحدة بحسبها يصير المحل واحداً فالكلام عائد فيها كالكلام في العلية وتسلسل والحمل أنا نختار الثالث ونقول إنها قائمة بالمجموع الذي توحد توحداً اعتبارياً باعتبار عروض هيئة اعتبارية لازمة للاجتماع لا تتسلسل لعدم الحاجة إلى هيئة أخرى فتأمل فيه أقول على أنها أي العلية اعتبارية، فيجوز أن يتصف بها الكثرة من حيث هي كثرة ولا يحتاج إلى جهة واحدة ولا يذهب عليك أن الجواب الأول لا يتم إلا بانضمام هذا، فإن له أن يقول: عروض الهيئة لا بد له من أن يكون المحل واحداً، فلا من نوع وحدة فيلزم التسلسل، فلا بد من التزام عروض الهيئة للكثير المحض، وهو الجواب الثاني فافهم مع أن العلة المركبة مجموع العلل الناقصة فإن كل جزء منها يتوقف عليه المعلول في الجملة فيجوز أن يقوم بكل جزء عليه ناقصة يعني يجوز أن يكون وصف العلية التامة مركباً من العليات النواقص، ويكون الكل قائماً بالمجموع وأجزاؤه بأجزائه ومعنى قيام الجميع بالجميع قيام الأجزاء بالأجزاء فتدبر. وأما الجواب عن استدلالهم بأنها ليست صفة للوصف المركب حتى يتوجه السؤال في قيامه به بل صفة للشارع متعلقة به: بمعنى حكم بثبوت الحكم عنده كما في المختصر، فلا يخفى وهنه وسخافته فإن الباعثية وكونه مجعولاً من الشارع كذلك صفة للوصف البتة، وإن كان هذا الوصف بالجعل من الشارع فافهم
The deniers said: First, if the composite is the cause, then the standing of causality is either by every part of it, or by one, or by the composite. The divisions are invalid, because if causality stands by every part, then each part is a cause, not the composite. Or if it stands by one, then that one is the cause, not the composite. Or if it stands by the whole insofar as it is a whole, then the standing of one by many is invalid, so there must be a single aspect by which the locus becomes one; then the discussion returns to it like the discussion on causality and infinite regress. And the burden: we choose the third and say it stands by the composite which is unified with a considerational unification, by virtue of the occurrence of a considerational form necessary for the combination, which does not lead to infinite regress due to no need for another form. So reflect on it. I say: the point is that causality is considerational, so multiplicity can be described by it as multiplicity and does not need a single aspect. And it should not escape you that the first answer is not complete unless this is added, for he may say: the occurrence of the form must have the locus as one, so there must be some kind of unity, leading to infinite regress. So he must commit to the occurrence of the form to pure multiplicity, which is the second answer. So understand. And with that, the composite cause is the sum of incomplete causes; each part of it is depended upon for the effect in general, so it is permissible that each part is an incomplete cause. That is, it is permissible that the description of complete causality is composed of incomplete causalities, and the whole stands by the composite and its parts by its parts. So understand. As for the answer to their argument that it is not a description of the composite description such that the question arises about its standing with it, but rather it is a description of the Lawgiver related to it, meaning the ruling of establishing the ruling with Him as in al-Mukhtasar, its weakness and flimsiness is not hidden, for motivativeness and being made by the Lawgiver is also a description of the description entirely, even if this description is by the Lawgiver's making. So understand.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul dispute on the nature of a compound 'illa (cause) in qiyas. The opponents (likely Shafi'is) argue that if the 'illa is composed of multiple descriptions, then either each part individually is the 'illa (leading to multiplicity), or one part is (defeating composition), or the whole is (but then the whole cannot stand by many parts without a unifying aspect). They claim this leads to infinite regress in the unifying aspect. The Hanafi response, from the commentator (Sharh Muslim al-Thubut), is that the 'illa is considerational (i'tibari): the whole is a considerational unity (al-majmu' al-mutawahhid i'tibaran) that does not produce regress. The 'illa as a whole is a construct of incomplete causes (al-'ilal al-naqisa), and the complete causal description is a sum of partial ones. The final answer is that 'illa is not a description of the composite but a description of the Lawgiver, though the commentator dismisses this as weak. The lesson type is a usul dispute (Hanafi vs. opponent) with rational proofs.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): The cause or effective legal reason in qiyas that leads to the ruling.
اعتبارية (i'tibariyya): Considerational: not real but mentally constructed, allowing multiplicity without a real unifying aspect.
هيئة اعتبارية (hay'a i'tibariyya): Considerational form: an abstract structure that arises from the combination of parts, preventing infinite regress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what prevents infinite regress in a compound 'illa?
A) The 'illa is reduced to a single part.
B) The whole is considered a real physical unity.
C) The whole is considered a considerational (i'tibari) unity.
D) The need for a cause is denied entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi concept of considerational unity allows a compound 'illa to be effective without a separate unifying aspect, thus blocking the infinite regress objection.

The Second Objection Against Compound ‘Illa: Non-Existence of Parts
Addressing the objection that non-existence of each part would cause non-existence of composite, leading to contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the naqd objection against compound ‘illa and distinguish between the two proposed answers.

Core Idea
The non-existence of a compound ‘illa is caused by the first missing part, not by each part individually.

This passage addresses a sophisticated objection against the permissibility of compound ‘illa in qiyas: if the ‘illa is composed of multiple parts, then the absence of each part would independently cause the non-existence of the ‘illa, leading to a logical contradiction (naqd) when multiple parts are absent. The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing the true cause of non-existence from mere non-existence of conditions. This lesson unpacks the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the opponent’s claim: if the ‘illa is composite, then the non-existence of each part is a cause for the non-existence of the whole, leading to naqd when a second part is absent after the first has already caused non-existence.
The key phrase is ‘لزم النقض’.
2
Understand the urine-madhi analogy
The opponent gives an example: when urine exits, impurity occurs; when madhi exits after, impurity does not occur again (impossible to create twice). This illustrates the contradiction if every part were a cause.
This is a qiyas thalbi (negative analogy).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (first answer)
The text’s preferred answer: the true cause of the composite’s non-existence is the non-existence of any part (the first one that is absent). Subsequent non-existences are not causes because the composite is already non-existent.
‘علة عدم المركب عدم جزء من أجزائه’
4
Evaluate the alternative answer (shart al-‘illiyya)
A second answer suggests that the non-existence of a part is the non-existence of a condition for the ‘illa. The text criticizes this: if it is a condition, then its non-existence still causes non-existence of the effect, so the same naqd recurs.
The criticism is that the problem is merely pushed back.
5
Apply the distinction
Understand the practical consequence: compound ‘illa is valid and does not lead to logical contradiction. This allows Hanafi usul to accept composite causes in qiyas.
Thamarah: no need to break ‘illa into single parts to avoid naqd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: ثانياً لو تركب الوصف لكان عدم كل جزء علة لانتفائها ضرورة أن عدم سبب لعدم المركب ويلزم النقض بعدم ثان بعد عدم أول مستلزم للعدم المركب، وعدم استلزام هذا العدم الثاني له لاستحالة انعدام المعدوم فقد وجد عدم جزء، ولم يوجد بحذائه عدم المجموع فلزم النقض، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن البول إذا خرج فقد وجد الحدث، ثم إذا خرج بعد ذلك المذي فلم يوجد حدث لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود والمثلين فلزم النقض والحل أن التخلف ههنا المانع وهو الحصول بعلة أخرى والتخلف لمانع لا يقدح في اقتضاء المقتضى والسر في مانعيه الحصول بعلة أخرى أن الإمكان شرط في تأثير العلة، يعني أن تساوي نسبة الطرفين مع قطع النظر عن تأثير المؤثر شرط والضرورة قبله ولو بالعلة تنافيه أي الإمكان بالتأويل المذكور أقول: ولك أن تقول في الجواب العلة عدم كل جزء من الأجزاء بدلاً أولاً وبالذات يعني علة العدم للمركب عدم جزء من أجزائه، بل عدم علة ما من علله، وإنما يطلق على كل واحد واحد من أعدام الأجزاء، لأنه متحقق في ضمنها، فلم يلزم النقض والتخلف عما هو علة حقيقة فافهم وإذا تأملت حق التأمل أيقنت أن الجواب حقيقة هو هذا، وأجيب في المختصر بأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الجزء عدم شرط العلية بأن يكون وجود الجزء شرطاً لها، وأورد عليه أن الكلام في تركب العلة من الأوصاف فلا يكون وجود الجزء شرطاً، وأجيب بأنه لا استحالة في أن يكون الشيء جزء لشيء شرطاً للصفة العلية العارضة له فتأمل فيه وتعقب المصنف بأنه لو سلم الشرطية فلا يندفع الإيراد، فإن عدم الشرط يوجب عدم المشروط، كما أن عدم العلة يوجب عدم المعلول، ففي صورة تعدد الأجزاء بتعدد الأعدام فإذا عدم لواحد فلا ينعدم بأخرى فيلزم المحذور قهقرى فافهم
And they said: Secondly, if the description is composite, then the non-existence of every part would be a cause for its non-existence, necessarily because the non-existence of a cause is for the non-existence of the composite, and it entails contradiction due to a second non-existence after a first non-existence that necessitates the non-existence of the composite, and this second non-existence does not entail it due to the impossibility of non-existing something already non-existent. So the non-existence of a part occurred, and corresponding to it, the non-existence of the whole did not occur, thus contradiction occurs. And it should not escape you that when urine exits, the ritual impurity occurs; then when madhi exits after that, no ritual impurity occurs due to the impossibility of creating what already exists and two likes. So contradiction occurs. The solution is that the delay here is due to a preventer, which is occurrence by another cause, and delay due to a preventer does not invalidate the efficacy of the requirer. And the secret in its prevention by occurrence by another cause is that possibility is a condition for the effect of the cause, meaning that equal relation to both sides disregarding the effect of the effective agent is a condition, and necessity before it even by the cause contradicts it, i.e., possibility according to the aforementioned interpretation. I say: You may say in answer: the cause of the non-existence of the composite is the non-existence of a part of its parts, conversely primarily and essentially, meaning the cause of the non-existence of the composite is the non-existence of a part of its parts, rather the non-existence of a cause among its causes, and it is applied to each one of the non-existences of the parts because it is realized within them. So contradiction and delay from what is truly the cause do not occur. So understand. And if you reflect thoroughly, you will be certain that the true answer is this. And it was answered in al-Mukhtasar that it is permissible that the non-existence of the part is the non-existence of a condition of causality, such that the existence of the part is a condition for it. And it was objected that the discussion is about the composition of the cause from descriptions, so the existence of the part cannot be a condition. And it was answered that there is no impossibility in something being a part of something and a condition for the causal attribute that occurs to it. So reflect on it. And the author criticized that if the conditionality is granted, the objection is not repelled, for the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned, just as the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect. So in the case of multiple parts with multiple non-existences, when one is non-existent, it does not become non-existent by another, so the problem recurs backward. So understand.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the opponents against compound 'illa: if the 'illa is composite, then the non-existence of each part would cause the non-existence of the whole. This leads to a problem: after the first non-existence of a part causes the whole to be non-existent, a second non-existence of another part cannot cause it again (since the whole is already non-existent). The example of urine and madhi is given: after urine causes impurity, madhi cannot cause it again because impurity already exists (impossibility of two likes). The Hanafi response distinguishes: the true cause of the composite's non-existence is not each part's non-existence individually, but the non-existence of any part (the first one). The non-existence of any part is sufficient, so there is no naqd when a second part is absent. Another answer from al-Mukhtasar (that the non-existence of a part is the non-existence of a condition) is criticized because if it is a condition, then its non-existence still causes non-existence of the effect, leading to the same problem. The text sides with the first answer: the 'illa for non-existence is the non-existence of any part, not each.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Logical contradiction where a ruling is expected but fails to apply, used here to test the validity of the 'illa.
شرط العلية (shart al-'illiyya): Condition for causality: something whose existence is required for the 'illa to be effective.
علة مركبة (‘illa murakkaba): A cause composed of multiple attributes that together produce the ruling.
تخلف (takhalluf): Delay or failure of a ruling to follow its cause, here due to a preventer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core Hanafi answer to the objection that non-existence of each part causes non-existence of the composite, leading to naqd?
A) The composite does not exist at all
B) The non-existence of the composite is caused by the non-existence of any part, not each
C) The non-existence of the composite is caused by the non-existence of all parts simultaneously
D) The objection is invalid because qiyas is not used for non-existence

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends compound ‘illa by showing that the non-existence of any part causes the whole, and subsequent non-existences are irrelevant, avoiding logical contradiction.

Condition of Muqtada for Ta‘lil of Non-Existence
Examining whether existence of muqtada is required for ta'lil al-'adam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two contexts (indication vs causation) in ta'lil al-'adam and apply the Hanafi rule regarding the existence of muqtada.

Core Idea
Muqtada existence is needed only for real causation, not for mere logical indication of non-existence.

In Hanafi usul, when we say that non-existence of a ruling is due to a preventer or absence of a condition, must we assume the requisite (muqtada) exists? This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut resolves a seeming disagreement by distinguishing between actual causation and logical indication. Understanding this distinction sharpens our ability to correctly assign causes in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the two views: one holds that muqtada existence is not required for ta'lil al-'adam; the other holds it is required. Note that both are mentioned in the text.
The first position is introduced with 'لا يشترط', the second with 'وقيل: نعم يشترط'.
2
Analyze the rationale behind each
Understand why the first view says muqtada is unnecessary: both the absence of muqtada and the presence of a preventer independently indicate non-existence. The second view argues that without muqtada, non-existence is simply due to its own absence, not the preventer.
The text uses 'مستبد في الدلالة' to describe independence in indication.
3
Locate the key distinction
Find where the commentator (al-Hashiya) clarifies the dispute: if the discussion is about logical indication (dalala) for knowledge, muqtada is not needed; if about actual causation (fi nafs al-amr), muqtada is needed.
The phrase 'إن كان النزاع في الدلالة المفيدة للعلم فالحق عدم الاشتراط وإن كان في العلة حقيقة فالحق الاشتراط' is the pivot.
4
Apply to the question of presupposition
Understand that when making an argument (istidlal) that a preventer is the cause, we sometimes presuppose muqtada to show the preventer's effect. But for pure indication, no presupposition is needed.
The passage says 'لا حاجة إلى تقدير المقتضى' in the context of indication.
5
Summarize the ruling
Conclude: muqtada existence is a condition for real causation but not for logical indication. In practical usul analysis, identify which context applies.
This resolution preserves both views as valid in their respective domains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا يشترط في تعليل العدم بالمانع وكذا في تعليله بانتفاء الشرط وجود المقتضى، وقيل: نعم يشترط وإلا أي وإن لم يكن وجود المقتضى مشروطاً، بل جاز عدمه فالعدم للمعلول العدمه لا لوجود المانع فيلغو التعليل به الناكل من عدم المقتضى ووجود المانع مستبد في الدلالة والإعلام على عدم المعلول وإن كان في الواقع العدم معلولاً لأحدهما ومن البين أنه لا دخل في الاستدلال بدليل لعدم قيام آخر، قال في الحاشية: فيه إشارة إلى أنه إن كان النزاع في الدلالة المفيدة للعلم فالحق عدم الاشتراط وإن كان في العلة حقيقة فالحق الاشتراط انتهى والسرّ فيه أن علة العدم بالذات عدم العلة التامة، وذلك إنما يكون بعدم واحد من الأجزاء لا بعينه إذا كانت مركبة والخصوصية ملغاة، فإذا وجد المانع فقد بطل تمامية العلة فعدم المعلول، وإذا عدم المقتضى فعدمه بعدم العلة ظاهر، فالتحقيق، أنه إن كان النزاع في العلة حقيقة فالاستناد إلى الأول منهما أياً كان فافهم وحينئذ أي حين ما بينا لا حاجة إلى تقدير المقتضى بأن يكون بحيث لو فرض المقتضى لمنعه كما ظن في التحرير لأن الكلام إن كان في نفس الدلالة فالكل مستقل فيها فلا حاجة إلى تقدير المقتضى وإلا فلا تأثير للمانع في نفس الأمر عند انعدام المقتضى نعم: العدم بعدمه أظهر من العدم بالمانع، ولا يذهب عليك أنه إنما احتاج إلى تقدير المقتضى ليظهر مانعية المانع حتى يصح الاستدلال به فافهم
Issue: The existence of the requisite (muqtada) is not required when attributing non-existence to a preventer, nor when attributing it to the absence of a condition. And it was said: Yes, it is required. Otherwise, i.e., if the existence of the requisite is not conditioned, but its non-existence is permissible, then the non-existence of the effect is its own non-existence, not due to the existence of the preventer, so the attribution becomes invalid. The two [cases] of non-existence of the requisite and existence of the preventer are independent in indicating and informing of the non-existence of the effect, even if in reality the non-existence is caused by one of them. And it is clear that there is no role for proof based on the absence of another. He said in al-Hashiya: There is an indication that if the dispute is about indication that provides knowledge, then the truth is that there is no requirement; if it is about actual causation, then the truth is that requirement is needed. End. The secret is that the real cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the complete cause, and that occurs only by the non-existence of one of its parts, not a specific one, if it is composite and the particularity is disregarded. So if the preventer exists, then the completeness of the cause is nullified, so the effect is absent. And if the requisite is absent, then its absence is due to the absence of the cause, obviously. So the investigation is that if the dispute is about actual causation, then the attribution is to the first of the two, whichever it may be. So understand. And then, i.e., when we have clarified, there is no need to presuppose the requisite such that it would be as if the requisite were presumed to prevent it, as thought in al-Tahrir, because if the discussion is about the very indication, then each is independent in it, so no need to presuppose the requisite; otherwise, the preventer has no effect in reality when the requisite is absent. Indeed, non-existence due to its absence is more apparent than non-existence due to the preventer. And it should not escape you that one needed to presuppose the requisite only to show the preventative nature of the preventer so that proof by it becomes sound. So understand.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage resolves a usul dispute about whether, when we say 'the non-existence of the effect is due to a preventer' or 'due to the absence of a condition', the existence of the muqtada (the requisite that would have caused the effect) must be assumed. One view says no: the preventer alone indicates non-existence even without the muqtada. Another says yes: without the muqtada, the non-existence is simply due to its own non-existence, not the preventer. The commentator clarifies: if the discussion is about logical indication (dalala) for knowledge, the muqtada is not needed; if about actual causation (fi nafs al-amr), the muqtada is needed. The real cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the complete cause (which includes the muqtada or absence of preventer). If a preventer exists, it nullifies the cause; if muqtada is absent, the cause is absent. For the purpose of proof (istidlal), sometimes we presuppose the muqtada to show that the preventer is indeed preventing, but strictly for causation the muqtada must exist.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): The requisite factor whose existence is assumed for the preventer to be considered the cause of non-existence.
تعليل العدم (ta'lil al-'adam): Attributing non-existence of an effect to a cause (preventer or missing condition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the investigation in this passage, what is the ruling on requiring the existence of muqtada when attributing non-existence to a preventer?
A) It is always required
B) It is never required
C) It is required for actual causation but not for mere indication
D) It is required for indication but not for causation

Key Takeaway
The requirement of muqtada existence for ta'lil al-'adam depends on whether we are discussing real causation (then needed) or logical indication (then not needed).
