Al-Shabah (Semblance): An Invalid Path for Determining 'Illa
Definition and critique of al-shabah as a path for 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-shabah, explain why it is not a valid maslak for 'illa according to the Hanafis, and identify the points of dispute with other schools.

Core Idea
Al-shabah is an illusion of suitability—not a valid cause, according to the Hanafis.

In this lesson, we examine a controversial method for establishing the effective cause ('illa) in analogical reasoning (qiyas): al-shabah (semblance). The Hanafi school categorically rejects it, while some other schools consider it a weak path. Understanding why shabah fails to produce legal certainty will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments for causality.

Guided Path
1
Define al-shabah and its illusory nature
Read the definition: al-shabah is an attribute that is not genuinely suitable (munasib) but creates an illusion of suitability because the Lawgiver has taken it into account in some rulings. Identify the key phrase: 'يوهم المناسبة' (creates an illusion of suitability).
This illusion is the core problem—it is not real suitability.
2
Analyze the example: khabath vs. hadath
The text presents an argument: 'Purification from filth (khabath) is intended for prayer, just like purification from ritual impurity (hadath), so water is required for both.' Trace why this is an example of shabah: the attribute 'intended for prayer' seems suitable for requiring water, but in hadath the requirement is devotional (ta'abbud), not based on that attribute. Thus, the suitability is only apparent.
The illusion is exposed by deeper analysis of the rulings.
3
Identify the Hanafi position and the dispute
The commentator states that according to the Hanafis (and some Shafi'is like al-Qadi al-Baqillani), al-shabah is neither a cause nor a path (maslak). It is 'absolutely invalid' (باطل قطعاً). Note the opposing views: some consider it a weak path, others an absolute path. Understand the Hanafi argument: lack of genuine suitability means it cannot produce even strong conjecture (zann) of causality.
Weak conjecture (zann da'if) is insufficient for legal causation.
4
Distinguish the second meaning of shabah
The passage mentions another usage of shabah: when a branch (e.g., a murdered slave) has two attributes (humanity and property) that make it similar to two origins (free person and horse). The jurist determines which similarity is stronger. However, the commentator explicitly says this meaning is 'not from what we are in'—it is not part of the current discussion about masālik al-‘illa.
Do not conflate this tasbih (comparison) with the invalid maslak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه الشبه وهو ما ليس بمناسبة لذاته، بل يوهم المناسبة وذلك التوهم إنما هو بالتفات الشارع إليه في بعض الأحكام فيتوهم منه المناسبة (كقولك: إزالة الخبث طهارة تراد للصلاة فتعين فيها الماء) ولا يجوز مائع آخر كإزالة) (الحدث يتعين فيه الماء، فكونها طهارة مرادة للصلاة، ليس هذا مناسباً لوجوب الماء، بل إنما يناسبه إزالة ما هو نجس، لكن في الحدث لا يمكن إزالتها إلا بالتعبد، وذلك بالماء، وفي الخبث بإزالة عينه (ليس بعلة ولا مسلك خبر لمبتدأ ، وهو قوله : الشبه (عندنا، وعليه القاضي الباقلاني والصيرفي وأبو إسحق الشيرازي كلهم من الشافعية (وأما سائر الشافعية فبعضهم قالوا إنه علة، وليس بمسلك) بل إن ثبت بمسلك المسالك ونحوه يقبل وإلا لا وعليه ابن الحاجب) من المالكية (وأكثرهم من على أنه من المسالك وهو باطل قطعاً. إذ ليس فيه مناسبة تفيد ظن العلية وإن أفاد ظناً ضعيفاً : فهو لا يغني من الحق شيئاً، ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من اعتبره مسلكاً مطلقاً) مثل سائر المسالك ( وكثير على أنه مسلك ضعيف لا يصار إليه مع إمكان مسلك آخر وقد يقال) الشبه (لا شبه وصفين كائنين في فرع تردد بهما بين أصلين كالآدمية والمالية ثابتين (في العبد المقتول تردد بهما بين الحر) لأن الآدمية تقتضي كون دمه أشرف مثل شرافة الحر (فتؤخذ ديته) كما تؤخذ في الحر (و) بين (الفرس) فإن المالية تحكم أنه مثل فتؤخذ قيمته بالغة ما بلغت) كما تؤخذ في الفرس المتلف (وهو) أي العبد المقتول بالحر أشبه لأن المشاركة أكثر ولأنه مبقي في حق الدم شرعاً على آدميته مثل الحر (وهذا المعنى ليس مما نحن فيه).
A note: al-Shabah is that which is not suitable in itself, but creates an illusion of suitability, and that illusion is only by the Shari'ah's attention to it in some rulings, so suitability is imagined from it. (As in your saying: Removal of filth (khabath) is a purification intended for prayer, so water is specified for it, and another liquid is not permissible, like removal of (hadath) where water is specified. So the fact that it is purification intended for prayer is not suitable for the necessity of water; rather what suits it is the removal of something impure, but in hadath it cannot be removed except by devotion (ta'abbud), and that is with water, and in khabath by removing the substance itself.) It is neither a cause nor a path (maslak). (Predicate of a subject, which is his saying: al-shabah) (According to us, and al-Qadi al-Baqillani, al-Sayrafi, and Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, all from the Shafi'is) (As for the rest of the Shafi'is, some of them said it is a cause, and not a path) Rather, if it is established by a path of paths and the like, it is accepted, otherwise not. And upon this is Ibn al-Hajib from the Malikis. (And most of them hold that it is among the paths, and it is absolutely invalid. For there is no suitability in it that benefits conjecture of causality, and if it benefits weak conjecture, it does not avail against truth at all.) Then they differed: some considered it a path absolutely like other paths, and many consider it a weak path that is not turned to when another path is possible. (And it may be said) al-shabah (is not about two descriptions existing in a branch by which it is hesitated between two origins, like humanity (adamīyya) and property (māliyya) established in the murdered slave, by which he is hesitated between the free person (because humanity requires that his blood is nobler like the nobility of the free, so his diya is taken as taken from the free) and between the horse (because property rules that he is like it so his value is taken however much it reaches, as taken from the destroyed horse). And he (the murdered slave) is more similar to the free because participation is more and because he is legally preserved in terms of blood in his humanity like the free. (And this meaning is not from what we are in.)
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines الشبه (semblance) as an attribute that is not genuinely suitable for the ruling but creates an illusion of suitability because the Shari'ah has looked at it in some rulings. The example given is comparing purification from impurity (khabath) to purification from ritual impurity (hadath): both require water, but the suitability for water in hadath is by devotional command, not by the attribute 'intended for prayer'. The commentator states that according to the Hanafis, الشبه is neither a valid 'illa nor a valid path (maslak) for establishing 'illa. Some Shafi'is (al-Baqillani, etc.) agree, while others consider it a weak path or absolute path. The second meaning of الشبه is then mentioned: when a branch (slave) has two attributes (humanity and property) making it similar to two origins (free person and horse), and it is determined which origin it is more like. However, this usage is not part of the current discussion.

Key Terms
الشبه (al-shabah): A description that is not genuinely suitable but creates an illusion of suitability because of the Shari'ah's occasional attention to it.
المسلك (al-maslak): A method or path for establishing the 'illa (effective cause) in qiyas.
المناسبة (al-munasaba): Genuine suitability between an attribute and the ruling, such that the attribute can be considered the 'illa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of al-shabah as a method for determining the 'illa?
A) It is a valid maslak (path), though weak.
B) It is neither a valid cause nor a path; it is absolutely invalid.
C) It is a valid cause but not a path.
D) It is valid only if supported by a text.

Key Takeaway
Al-shabah is an illusion of suitability, not a valid basis for establishing the 'illa; the Hanafi school decisively rejects it.

Al-Dawaran: Is Co-extensiveness a Valid Method for Determining the 'Illa?
Usul dispute on the validity of dawaran as a maslak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on al-dawaran, identify the arguments for and against it, and apply the critique to avoid circular reasoning in istinbat.

Core Idea
Al-dawaran alone does not prove causality; circular reasoning and counterexamples invalidate it.

One of the most debated methods for identifying the 'illa in qiyas is al-dawaran—the observation that a ruling and a description co-occur and co-absent. This passage presents the Hanafi rejection (alongside many Ash'aris) and the Shafi'i acceptance, with a detailed critique using the wudu verse and the example of correlatives. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate causal claims in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define al-dawaran
Understand the two components: al-tard (positive co-extensiveness) and al-‘aks (negative co-extensiveness).
Dawaran is also called 'co-rotation' or 'concomitance'.
2
Identify the schools and their positions
Hanafis and many Ash'aris (like al-Ghazali and al-Amidi) reject dawaran. Shafi'is of Iraq accept it as a conjectural proof; some accept it definitively, often conditioned on a text.
The passage states: 'The Hanafis and many Ash'aris said no; others said yes as a conjecture or certainty.'
3
Analyze the wudu verse example
Opponents claim that the verse shows dawaran: wudu is obligatory with hadath (even without standing) and not obligatory without hadath (even with standing), isolating hadath as the cause.
Quran 5:6: 'When you stand up for prayer...'
4
Examine the Hanafi critique
If the text is present in both states, using dawaran leads to circular reasoning (العود على الأصل بالإبطال). The correct interpretation (from Ibn Abbas) is that 'standing' is qualified by hadath, so the text itself points to hadath. Additionally, dawaran fails in correlatives (mutadā'ifayn) like fatherhood and sonship, which co-exist without causality.
The Hanafis argue that correlation alone is not sufficient; there must be no strong preventer.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
In a case where a description co-rotates with a ruling but there is no text, Hanafis would not consider it a valid cause, while Shafi'is might accept it conjecturally.
The practical difference lies in the acceptance of causes derived purely from observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الخامس الدوران وهو (الطرد أي كلما وجد الوصف وجد الحكم والعكس) أي كلما انتفى الوصف. انتفى الحكم نفاه الحنفية وكثير من الأشعرية كالغزالي الإمام حجة الإسلام والآمدي والأكثر) سواهم قالوا (نعم) حجة (فقيل) حجة (ظناً وعليه شافعية العراق . وقيل) حجة (قطعاً، وشرط بعضهم في حجية الدوران قيام النص في حال وجود الوصف) فيثبت الحكم (و) في حال عدمه، ولا حكم له فيقطع حينئذ بأن العلة هو الوصف لدوران الحكم معه دون النص (كآية الوضوء) وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَتأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصلوة ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] الآية (فإن الوضوء) يجب بالحدث وإن لم يكن القيام ولا يجب بعدمه) أي الحدث (وإن كان القيام فالنص لا دخل له في الحكم (وليس هذا الرأي بشيء : لأن التعليل حينئذ أي حين انتفاء الحكم لانتفائه ووجود الحكم بوجوده مع قيام النص في الحالين ( يعود على أصله بالإبطال فلا يصح، لأنه قد تقدم أن من شرط التعليل عدم العود إلى أصله بالإبطال والمراد في (الآية إذا قمتم وأنتم محدثون كما هو مأثور عن ابن عباس وقد قرأ) إذا قمتم من مضاجعكم فالنص إنما يفيد وجوب الوضوء بالحدث دون مطلق القيام، فعند عدم الحدث ليس النص قائماً . قال النافون (أولاً) لو كان الدوران مسلكاً للعلة لثبتت أينما ثبت، لكنه (تخلف في المتضايقين فإن أحد المتضايقين دائر مع آخر وجوداً وعدماً ولا علية (وأجيب) التخلف (بمانع قطعاً) وهو لا يقدح فالملازمة ممنوعة فإن كونه مسلكاً إنما هو إذا لم يكن هناك مانع قوي وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن المقصود أن الدوران أمر أعم من التضايف، ولما كان مانعاً عن العلية فالقدر المشترك بينه وبين غيره من أين يفيد العلية
The fifth path: al-Dawaran, which is (al-tard, i.e., whenever the description is found, the ruling is found, and the converse) i.e., whenever the description is absent, the ruling is absent. The Hanafis and many Ash'aris like al-Ghazali, the Imam Hujjat al-Islam, and al-Amidi, and most others) said (yes) it is a proof. (It was said) a proof (conjecturally, and upon this are the Shafi'is of Iraq. And it was said) a proof (definitively. And some conditioned for the probity of dawaran the existence of a text in the case of the description's existence) so the ruling is established (and) in its absence, there is no ruling, so then it is conclusive that the cause is the description due to the co-rotation of the ruling with it without the text. (Like the verse of wudu) which is His statement: 'O you who believe, when you stand up for prayer' [al-Ma'ida:6] the verse. (For wudu) is obligatory due to hadath even if there is no standing, and not obligatory by its absence) i.e., hadath (even if standing exists, so the text has no role in the ruling. (And this opinion is nothing: because the ta'lil then, i.e., when the ruling is absent due to its absence and exists due to its existence while the text is present in both cases, (goes back and nullifies its own basis, so it is invalid, for it has preceded that from the conditions of ta'lil is not going back to nullify its basis. And the intended meaning in the (verse is 'when you stand up while you are in a state of hadath' as transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas, and he recited) 'when you stand up from your sleeping places.' So the text only indicates the obligation of wudu due to hadath, not due to absolute standing. So when hadath is absent, the text is not present.) The rejecters said (firstly): If dawaran were a path for the cause, it would be established wherever it is established, but it (is absent in correlatives (mutadā'ifayn), for one correlative co-rotates with the other in existence and non-existence, yet there is no causality. (And it was answered) the absence (is due to a preventer definitely) and that does not harm, so the implication is denied, for it being a path is only when there is no strong preventer. And you must know that the intended meaning is that dawaran is more general than correlation (tadā'uf), and since correlation is a preventer for causality, from where does the commonality between it and others benefit causality?
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth method (al-maslak al-khāmis) for determining the 'illa: al-dawaran (co-extensiveness), defined as the coincidence of the description and the ruling in existence (tard) and non-existence ('aks). The Hanafis and many Ash'aris (al-Ghazali, al-Amidi) reject it, while others accept it either as a conjectural proof (Shafi'is of Iraq) or as a definitive proof, sometimes conditioned on the presence of a text in the case of existence. The example of the wudu verse is given: wudu is obligatory due to hadath, not due to standing. The refutation is that relying on dawaran alone leads to invalid ta'lil (reasoning) because it goes back and nullifies the original basis (the text) if the text is present in both cases. The correct interpretation of the verse, as reported from Ibn 'Abbas, is that standing is qualified by hadath. The second argument against dawaran is that it fails in the case of correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship) which co-exist and co-absent without causality. The response is that there is a preventer (correlation) which is a strong preventer, so dawaran is not a valid method when such preventers exist.

Key Terms
الدوران (al-dawaran): The method of establishing causality by observing that whenever the description exists, the ruling exists, and whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent (tard wa 'aks).
الطرد (al-tard): The positive side of dawaran: the presence of the description coincides with the presence of the ruling.
العكس (al-'aks): The negative side of dawaran: the absence of the description coincides with the absence of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on al-dawaran (co-extensiveness) as a method for determining the 'illa?
A) It is a valid proof definitively.
B) It is a valid proof conjecturally.
C) It is rejected.
D) It is accepted only when supported by a text.

Key Takeaway
Al-dawaran alone is not sufficient to prove causality; a valid ‘illa must be supported by textual indication or a reliable method that avoids circular reasoning and counterexamples.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Al-Dawaran: Correlation as a Mask for 'Illah — Hanafi Critique
Analyzing the fifth maslak (dawaran) for identifying 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-dawaran, state the Hanafi position rejecting it, and apply at least two Hanafi arguments (mutual correlatives and mere concomitance) to refute a claim that correlation proves causality.

Core Idea
Correlation does not equal causation — dawaran alone cannot establish legal causality without a separate indicator.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth maslak for identifying the 'illah (effective cause) in qiyas: al-dawaran (coextensiveness). The Hanafi school strongly rejects dawaran as a valid pathway, arguing that mere correlation (tard and 'aks) does not prove causality. We will explore the definition, the opposing view held by some Shafi'is, and the key Hanafi objections—including the example of mutual correlatives and the argument that dawaran only indicates concomitance, not causal efficacy.

Guided Path
1
Define dawaran and its components
Al-dawaran is defined as the combination of tard (whenever the description is present, the ruling is present) and 'aks (whenever the description is absent, the ruling is absent). Understand this as a claim of perfect correlation.
The term 'dawaran' literally means 'rotation' or 'coextensiveness'.
2
Identify the two main positions
The Hanafis (and many Ash'aris) reject dawaran as a maslak. Some Shafi'is (especially of Iraq) accept it, either probabilistically or definitively. Note that some condition it on the absence of a separate text in both states.
The example of the wudu verse (5:6) is used to illustrate the conditioned view.
3
Analyze the first Hanafi argument: mutual correlatives
Mutual correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship) exhibit perfect tard and 'aks without any causal relation. This shows that dawaran can exist without causality, proving it is not a sufficient indicator.
The response from proponents is that a strong preventer (mana') is present, but Hanafis argue that the correlation is more general than causality.
4
Analyze the second Hanafi argument: mere concomitance
Dawaran could be mere concomitant (e.g., the bad smell of wine coexists with intoxication but is not the cause). Hence, dawaran alone does not distinguish between causality and mere accompaniment.
The text emphasizes that dawaran is 'a'amm min al-'illiyya' (more general than causality).

غير واف فإن مقصود الإمام أن الدوران اجتماع أمرين وإن كان لأحدهما دخل في دفع بعض ما هو منا في العلية لكن الأمر الآخر ليس له دخل أصلاً فالمجموع منهما كيف يكون دالاً على الاقتضاء والعلية وهل هذا إلا كجمع وليس فيه استدلال بعدم علية الأجزاء على عدم علية المجموع حتى يرد ما ذكر، وفيه أيضاً شائبة من الخفاء لأنه لا يجب أن يدل المسلك على تحقق الشروط، نعم يجب في نفس الأمر كما أن النص يدل على العلية لا على تحقق شروطها، فكذلك الدوران يجوز أن يكون مسلكاً وإن لم يدل على الشروط فتأمل. قال : (المثبتون للعلية إذا وجد الدوران ولا مانع من معية أو تأخر أو غيرهما حصل العلم بالعلية (أو) القائل بالظن يقول حصل الظن عادة كما في دوران غضب إنسان على اسم) فإنه يدل دلالة واضحة على أنه علة الغضب حتى يعلمه الأطفال وقد يتوهم أنه إذا كان كذلك صار العلم الحاصل به علماً ضرورياً كالتجربيات والحدسيات . قال : ولا يلزم أن يكون العلم الحاصل به ضرورياً كما وهم لأن حصول المبادىء قد لا يتفق دفعة بل بالتدريج والحركة فلا يكون ضرورياً (وأجيب بأن حصول العلم بمجرده ممنوع نعم يدل على الملازمة المطلقة و(يحصل) العلم أو الظن بالعلية عند ظهور انتفاء الغير من أنحاء الملازمة فليس الدوران نفسه دليلاً
The fifth maslak is al-dawaran, which is (al-tard, i.e. whenever the description is found the ruling is found, and al-'aks) i.e. whenever the description is absent the ruling is absent. The Hanafis and many Ash'aris such as al-Ghazali, al-Amidi, and the majority of others rejected it. They said (yes, a proof; some said a proof by probability, and upon this are the Shafi'is of Iraq; and some said a proof definitively). And some conditioned the validity of dawaran on the existence of a text in the case of the presence of the description, so the ruling is established, and in the case of its absence, no ruling, so it is determined that the 'illah is the description due to the correlation of the ruling with it apart from the text, like the verse of wudu, which is His saying: 'O you who believe, when you rise to prayer' [5:6], for wudu is obligatory upon hadath even if there is no rising, and it is not obligatory in its absence meaning hadath even if there is rising, so the text has no role in the ruling. And this opinion is nothing, because the ta'lil (causal attribution) then, i.e. when the ruling is absent due to its absence and the ruling is present with its presence while the text is operative in both states, reverts to its origin with nullification, so it is invalid, for it has preceded that among the conditions of ta'lil is non-reversion to its origin with nullification, and the intended meaning of the verse is 'when you rise while you are in a state of hadath' as narrated from Ibn Abbas, and he read 'when you rise from your sleeping places', so the text only indicates the obligation of wudu due to hadath, not absolute rising, so in the absence of hadath the text is not operative. The rejecters said: (First) if dawaran were a maslak for 'illah, it would be established wherever it is established, but it is absent in mutual correlatives, for one of two mutual correlatives correlates with the other in existence and non-existence without causality. And it was answered: the absence is due to a definite preventing factor, which does not harm, so the implication is denied, for it being a maslak is only when there is no strong preventer, and you are not unaware that the intended meaning is that dawaran is more general than mutual correlation, and since it is a preventer for causality, from where does the commonality between it and other than it benefit causality? Understand. And after this there is nothing but dialectic. And they said (Second): The essence of dawaran is merely the non-existence of each apart from the other, and it is more general than causality and could be a concomitant, like the evil smell of wine, so causality is not established by it. It was answered: If you mean by possibility the equivalence of the two sides, it is denied; rather causality is preponderant. And if you mean non-impossibility rationally, it does not conflict with probability, for probability does not negate possibility. I say: You can choose the first side and argue for equivalence by the fact that the 'illah and the concomitant are equal in being described by tard and 'aks, because it is common to both, so there is no preponderance for one over the other except by an external preponderating factor, so dawaran alone is not a proof. And from here it was said: Suitability for causality requires the appearance of appropriateness; otherwise there is no priority for one over the opposite. Understand. And they argued (Third) with what is specific to Imam al-Ghazali: al-ittarad (consistency) is safety from naqd (counterexample) only, so its utmost is safety from one corrupting factor, and safety from one corrupting factor does not necessitate safety from corrupting factors absolutely, so it does not necessitate causality. Even if it necessitated safety absolutely, it does not necessitate causal efficacy, and there is no causality without it. And al-'aks is not a condition for causality; rather its existence is like its non-existence in this regard. And it was answered: The utmost that follows from your statement is that ittarad does not necessitate causality, and likewise 'aks; but as for their combination, it may necessitate, for sometimes combination entails causality even if the individual parts do not, like a composite property composed of two general accidents, for each one, though a general accident, but the composite is specific, so combination has an effect that individuation does not. And this is insufficient, for the Imam's purpose is that dawaran is a combination of two things; even if one of them plays a role in repelling some of what is problematic in causality, the other has no role at all, so how can the combination be indicative of causal efficacy and causality? Is this not like combining tard with it not being a legal ruling? As for the two general accidents, it is possible that each is an additional property, so each has a role in specialization, and upon combination specialization intensifies, unlike our case. The more concise is that a combination only affects something if each of its parts has an effect, even at the time of combination; and the parts of dawaran are tard and 'aks, and they have no role in causal efficacy at all, as is clear, so understand. And it may be said: The Imam's purpose is that causality has considered conditions, and dawaran only indicates one of them, so it is not suitable as a proof for causality. And there is no inference in it from the non-causality of the parts to the non-causality of the whole such that what was mentioned is refuted. And there is also a shade of ambiguity in it, because it is not necessary that the maslak indicates the fulfillment of the conditions; indeed it is necessary in reality, just as the text indicates causality but not the fulfillment of its conditions, so similarly dawaran may be a maslak even if it does not indicate the conditions. So ponder. He said: (The affirmers of causality say: when dawaran is found and there is no preventer of coexistence, precedence, or others, then knowledge of causality is attained. Or the proponent of probability says probability is obtained by custom, like the correlation of a person's anger with a name, for it clearly indicates that it is the cause of anger, such that even children know it. And it may be thought that if it is so, then the knowledge obtained by it becomes necessary knowledge like empirical and conjectural knowledge. He said: But it is not necessary that the knowledge obtained by it be necessary, as was thought, because the acquisition of preliminaries may not occur all at once but gradually and through motion, so it is not necessary. And it was answered: The attainment of knowledge by mere dawaran is denied. Yes, it indicates absolute concomitance, and knowledge or probability of causality is attained when the non-existence of other types of concomitance becomes apparent, so dawaran itself is not a proof.)
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fifth maslak (pathway) for identifying the 'illah (effective cause) in qiyas, known as al-dawaran (correlation or coextensiveness). The author defines dawaran as the combination of tard (consistency: whenever the description is present, the ruling is present) and 'aks (inverse: whenever the description is absent, the ruling is absent). The core question is whether such correlation is a valid tool for establishing causality. The Hanafi school, along with many Ash'ari scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Amidi, reject dawaran as a valid maslak. Others, particularly some Shafi'is, accept it as a proof, either probabilistically or definitively, especially when a textual ruling is present only in one of the two correlation states. The author explains the conditions and objections: some require that in the presence and absence of the description, the ruling is not already established by a separate text, otherwise the correlation would be circular. The example of the wudu verse (al-Ma'idah 5:6) is used: wudu is obligatory upon hadath, not merely upon rising; thus the ruling correlates with hadath, not rising. The opponents argue that dawaran fails in cases of mutual correlatives (mutadayiqayn) where correlation exists without causality, and that correlation could be mere concomitance rather than causation. The author also discusses whether dawaran can yield certainty or only probability, comparing it to the correlation of anger with a name. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that dawaran alone is insufficient to establish 'illah; it may indicate concomitance but not causality. The commentary follows the author's order: first defining dawaran, then presenting the dispute, then the counterarguments and responses, and closing with the conclusion that dawaran is not itself a proof but may be useful when combined with other indicators.

Key Terms
الدوران (al-dawaran): The coextensiveness of a description and a ruling such that whenever the description is present, the ruling is present (tard), and whenever the description is absent, the ruling is absent ('aks).
الطرد (al-tard): Consistency: the presence of the description coincides with the presence of the ruling.
العكس (al-'aks): Inverse: the absence of the description coincides with the absence of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on al-dawaran (correlation) as a maslak for identifying the effective cause ('illah) in qiyas?
A) It is accepted as a definitive proof.
B) It is accepted as a probabilistic proof under certain conditions.
C) It is rejected entirely as a valid maslak.
D) It is considered the strongest maslak.

Key Takeaway
Dawaran (correlation) is rejected by Hanafis as a valid maslak for 'illah because it only indicates concomitance, not causation.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Dawaran as Proof for Causality: Hanafi Critique
Examining the third argument against dawaran and the responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main arguments for and against dawaran as proof for causality and apply the Hanafi requirement of munasabah to evaluate the strength of dawaran.

Core Idea
Dawaran alone proves only concomitance; causality requires suitability or effectiveness.

This passage delves into a core usul al-fiqh debate: can the mere co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness of a description with a ruling (dawaran) prove that the description is the cause? The Hanafi school insists that dawaran is insufficient without suitability (munasabah) or effectiveness (ta'thir). The text presents arguments from al-Ghazali and others, followed by a Hanafi critique. This lesson unpacks the key arguments and shows why the Hanafi view prevails, sharpening your ability to evaluate causal reasoning in qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two central arguments against dawaran
Locate the two main objections to dawaran as proof: (1) dawaran is mere concomitance, like the repugnant smell of wine; (2) even the combination of consistency and co-exclusiveness does not prove causality because the components lack entailment.
The first argument is attributed to unspecified opponents; the second is specifically from al-Ghazali.
2
Analyze the affirmers' response and the Hanafi rebuttal
Al-Ghazali and others argue that dawaran without preventers yields certainty or presumption, using the example of anger co-extensive with a name. The Hanafi response distinguishes between concomitance and causation, noting that children know the judgment, not the cause.
The rebuttal emphasizes that dawaran only indicates concomitance, and that suitability is a condition.
3
Examine the 'combination' argument and its critique
Affirmers claim that the combination of consistency and co-exclusiveness has a unique effect. The Hanafi critic retorts that each part (consistency and co-exclusiveness) plays no role in entailment, so the combination cannot prove causality.
The passage illustrates this with the analogy of composite particulars.
4
Apply the Hanafi condition of munasabah to a new example
Take the modern example: the co-extensiveness of high price with desirability. According to the Hanafi view, dawaran alone is insufficient; one must show suitability (e.g., high price indicates scarcity, which relates to desirability).
This demonstrates how the Hanafi principle is applied outside the classical text.

الموانع انتفاء المناسبة أو انتفاء التأثير فلا بد من انتفائه فلا يلزم استقلال الدوران مسلكاً، بل راجع إلى المناسبة وغيرها وإن أريد معيناً أو مبهماً منعنا الملازمة ( ودفع) هذا الجواب بأنه قدح في Separation from the fourth principle / analogy, so understand. After this there is nothing but debate. Then they said, secondly: The essence of co-extensiveness is that each is inseparable from the other, which is more general than causality, and it is possible that it be concomitant like the repugnant smell of wine. Thus causality is not proven by it. It was answered: If you mean by possibility equal probability of both sides, we deny it; rather causality is preponderant. And if you mean impossibility of reason, it does not contradict presumption, for presumption does not preclude possibility. I say: You may choose the first option and argue for equal probability based on the equal description of cause and concomitant in having co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness, due to their generality from each side. Thus there is no preponderance of one over the other except by an external preponderator, so co-extensiveness by itself is not a proof. From this it was said: suitability of causality for apparent appropriateness is a condition; otherwise there is no priority for it over the reverse. So understand. And they argued, thirdly, with their argument being specific to al-Ghazali: al-ittirād (consistency) is safety from rebuttal, nothing more. Its utmost is safety from one corrupting factor, and safety from one corrupting factor does not require safety from all corrupting factors absolutely, so it does not require causality. And if it required safety absolutely, it does not require entailment, nor causality without it. And co-exclusiveness is not a condition for causality; rather its existence is like its absence in this regard. It was answered: The utmost of what follows from your explanation is that consistency does not require causality, and likewise co-exclusiveness. But their combination may be a cause, for sometimes combination has the entailment of causality even if the individuals do not, like a composite particular consisting of two general attributes: each alone is a general attribute but the composite is particular. Thus combination has an effect not present in isolation. This is inadequate, for al-Ghazali's intent is that co-extensiveness is a combination of two things. Even if one of them has a role in repelling some of what is problematic in causality, the other has no role at all. So how can the combination indicate entailment and causality? Is this not like combining consistency with it not being a legal ruling? As for the two general attributes, it is possible that each is a relative particularity, so each has a role in specification; when they combine, specification intensifies, unlike our case. The shorter view is that the combination only affects something if each of its parts affects it, even if at the time of combination, and the parts of co-extensiveness are consistency and co-exclusiveness, and they have no role in entailment at all as shown. So understand. It is said: al-Ghazali's intent is that causality has valid conditions, and co-extensiveness indicates only one of them, so it is not a valid proof of causality. There is no argument from the non-causality of the parts to the non-causality of the whole to refute what was mentioned. There is also some obscurity in it, for it is not necessary that the proof method indicates the fulfillment of the conditions. Indeed it is necessary in reality, just as the text indicates causality not the fulfillment of its conditions. So similarly, co-extensiveness may be a proof method even if it does not indicate the conditions. Reflect. He said: Those who affirm causality, when co-extensiveness is found and there is no preventer of concomitance or delay or others, they attain certainty of causality. Or one who says presumption says that presumption habitually occurs, as in the co-extensiveness of a person's anger with a name. For it indicates clearly that it is a cause of anger, even children know it. It may be mistakenly thought that if so, the knowledge attained is necessary knowledge like empirical and intuitive knowledge. He said: It is not necessary that the knowledge attained by it be necessary as was mistakenly thought, because the attainment of principles may not occur at once but gradually and by movement, so it is not necessary. It was answered: The attainment of knowledge by it alone is denied. Yes, it indicates absolute concomitance, and knowledge or presumption of causality occurs when the non-existence of other types of concomitance appears. So co-extensiveness itself is not a proof and method. In other words, if you mean by your saying co-extensiveness is useful when there is no preventer that it is useful when all preventers are absent, that is granted. But among the preventers is the absence of suitability or the absence of effect, so their absence must be established. Hence the independence of co-extensiveness as a proof method does not follow; rather it reduces to suitability and others. If a specific or vague preventer is intended, we deny the concomitance. This answer was rebutted as an attack on empirical knowledge, for it is known by experience that knowledge is attained by it alone, as children affirm it, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. This is astonishing, for the testimony of experience to the attainment of knowledge by it alone absolutely is denied. How can it testify, while co-extensiveness in reciprocal relations is more complete and stronger yet there is no causality. If it testifies to the attainment of knowledge when the preventer is absent, its testimony is granted. But you have known that among the preventers is lack of effect. So knowledge only occurs when this absence is absent, thus suitability is required. I say: There is reflection in this; reflect. If he means the testimony of experience to the existence of the ruling when the co-extensive factor is present, that is granted but it is not a preventer. If he means knowledge of the causality of the co-extensive factor, the testimony of experience is denied. Children only affirm the necessity of anger at this name; as for what its cause is, there is not even presumption, let alone certainty. The same applies to other empirical cases.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate on whether dawaran (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness) can prove causality (`illah) in qiyas. The text presents several arguments and counter-arguments. First, the opponent argues that dawaran is more general than causality—it can be mere concomitance (like the smell of wine being concomitant but not the cause). The response distinguishes possibility of equal probability vs. rational possibility, and notes that presumption (zann) can still favor causality. The commentator suggests that without a preponderator, dawaran is insufficient. Then the third argument from al-Ghazali is presented: consistency (ittirād) only indicates safety from one rebuttal, not all, so it cannot prove causality. The response tries to argue that the combination of consistency and co-exclusiveness might have a unique effect, but this is refuted because neither component contributes to entailment. The passage then discusses a claim by the affirmers of causality that dawaran, without preventers, can yield certainty or presumption, like the connection between a name and anger. The response counters that this only proves absolute concomitance, not causality, and that experience actually supports the requirement of suitability (munasabah). The children's knowledge is only of the ruling, not the cause. The Hanafi position is not explicitly stated here, but the overall tone supports the view that dawaran alone is insufficient—thus aligning with the Hanafi emphasis on effectiveness (ta'thir) and suitability.

Key Terms
دوران (dawaran): Co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness: the presence of a ruling when a description is present and its absence when the description is absent.
اطراد (ittirād): Consistency: the absence of counterexamples (naqd) against a description being a cause.
مناسبة (munasabah): Suitability: the description's relevant connection to the ruling, which Hanafis consider essential for causality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the child does not know the cause, only the ruling.
B) Because anger is not a legal ruling.
C) Because the example is not empirical.
D) Because dawaran requires effectiveness, which is absent.

Key Takeaway
Dawaran is insufficient for causality; Hanafis require munasabah (suitability) or ta'thir (effectiveness) to establish `illah.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Hanafi Classification of Legal Descriptors: Co-extensiveness, Effectiveness, and Signs
Summarizing dawaran and defining cause, reason, condition, and sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ahl al-tard and ahl al-fiqh in Hanafi usul and explain why dawaran alone is insufficient to prove causality without effectiveness (ta'thir).

Core Idea
Consistency alone does not prove causality; the description must show effectiveness.

The passage you are about to read explains a key distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the difference between a cause (illah) and a mere sign (alamah), and why a method called dawaran (co-extensiveness) is not sufficient on its own to establish a causal link. The Hanafi school insists that a description must be effective (mu'aththir) to qualify as a cause in qiyas, a requirement they associate with ahl al-fiqh, as opposed to ahl al-tard who rely on mere consistency. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method under discussion
Locate the term 'dawaran' in the text and note its definition: the simultaneous presence of ruling with the factor and absence when the factor is absent.
This is the surface-level test for causality.
2
Recognize the limitation of dawaran
The commentator explains that dawaran only shows concomitance in some cases, not causality in the unknown branch. It could be accidental.
Hanafis reject dawaran as sufficient proof.
3
Contrast the two groups: ahl al-tard vs. ahl al-fiqh
Ahl al-tard accept dawaran without requiring effectiveness; ahl al-fiqh (the Hanafis) require ta'thir (effectiveness) as a necessary condition for causality.
Effectiveness is analogous to suitability (munasabah) in Shafi'i terminology.
4
Distinguish between cause and sign
The passage uses the example of the shadow (duluk) for prayer obligation: it is a sign (alamah) indicating the time, not the cause of obligation.
Signs are agreed upon but not real causes.
5
Apply the principle
Consider how a Hanafi would evaluate a proposed cause in qiyas: they would check both consistency (ittirād) and effectiveness (ta'thir).
This becomes a tool for critiquing analogical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن حاصل الدوران وجود الحكم عند وجود المدار في غير الفرع وانتفاؤه عند انتفائه في غيره، وأما الفرع فحاله غير معلوم، ولذا يحتاج إلى إثبات علية المدار ليعلم في الفرع فالدوران إنما يوجب أن المدار ملازم للحكم في بعض المحال، والبعض مشكوك الحال، فحينئذ يجوز أن تكون الملازمة اتفاقية لأجل مقارنته بعلة في ذلك البعض، فيستلزم الحكم، ولا توجد تلك العلة في الفرع المقصود معرفة حكمه فلا يلزم كون المدار علة ولا كونه ملازماً لها فافهم ثم اعلم أن الحنفية ينسبون الدوران إلى أهل الطرد) دون أهل الفقه (إذ يريدون من لا يشترط ظهور التأثير الذي هو الملائمة عند الشافعية) وبأهل الفقه من اعتبر التأثير (وعلى هذا فالسبر والإخالة (كذلك لا بد أن ينسب إلى أهل الطرد (وأما من يضيف الحكم إلى ما لا مناسبة له أصلاً فلم يوجد حتى يعني بأهل الطرد (كما في التحرير) وهذا التوجيه توجيه حسن لو تحمل عباراتهم ذلك، فإن قلت كيف نفيتم وجود من يضيف الحكم إلى غير أنه ينسب الحكم إلى العلامة كما في الدلوك؟ أجاب بقوله : (والإضافة إلى الإمارة المناسب مع والعلامة كالدلوك للوجوب) يضاف إليه وجوب الصلاة اتفاق) لكنه ليس من العلة إجماعاً لا (مجازاً) والكلام في العلة الحقيقية .
Know that the essence of co-extensiveness is the existence of the ruling when the co-extensive factor exists in other than the branch, and its non-existence when the co-extensive factor is absent in other than the branch. As for the branch, its state is unknown; hence it is necessary to prove the causality of the co-extensive factor to know the branch. So co-extensiveness only necessitates that the co-extensive factor is concomitant with the ruling in some instances, and some instances are of unknown state. Then it is possible that the concomitance is accidental due to its association with a cause in that instance, thus it entails the ruling, and that cause is not present in the branch intended to know its ruling. Hence it does not necessitate that the co-extensive factor be a cause, nor even a concomitant of it. Understand. Then know that the Hanafis attribute co-extensiveness to the people of al-tard, not to the people of fiqh. They mean those who do not require the appearance of effectiveness, which is suitability according to the Shafi'is. And by people of fiqh they mean those who consider effectiveness. On this basis, al-sabr wa al-taqsim must also be attributed to the people of al-tard. As for one who ascribes the ruling to something with no suitability at all, such a person does not exist such that he is meant by 'people of al-tard,' as in al-Tahrir. This interpretation is good if their expressions allow it. If you say: How did you deny the existence of one who ascribes the ruling to something other than that? He ascribes the ruling to a sign, as in the case of a shadow (duluk)? He answered: The ascription to a sign that is suitable and the sign like the shadow for obligation—the obligation of prayer is ascribed to it by agreement, but it is not a cause by consensus, not even metaphorically. The discussion is about the real cause.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a summary and clarification. The commentator first explains the limitation of dawaran: it only shows that the co-extensive factor accompanies the ruling in some cases, but it could be accidental. Therefore it does not prove causality for the branch case. Then the Hanafi position is stated: Hanafis attribute the use of dawaran to ahl al-tard (those who do not require effectiveness), while ahl al-fiqh (who require effectiveness) do not rely on it. This means that for Hanafis, dawaran is not a sufficient proof for `illah; they require that the description show effectiveness (ta'thir) which is the same as suitability (munasabah) in Shafi'i terminology. The commentator also clarifies that ascribing a ruling to a sign (e.g., shadow as a time indicator for prayer) is not about the real cause; it is merely a sign (alamah) agreed upon, not a cause. The real cause (al-illah al-haqiqiyyah) must be effective and suitable. Thus, the Hanafi school insists on effectiveness and suitability in identifying the `illah.

Key Terms
الدوران (al-dawaran): The co-extensiveness of a description with a ruling: whenever the description exists, the ruling exists, and whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent.
أهل الطرد (ahl al-tard): Those who accept consistency (ittirād) as sufficient for causality, without requiring effectiveness.
أهل الفقه (ahl al-fiqh): Those who require effectiveness (ta'thir) as a condition for a description to be a cause.
التأثير (al-ta'thir): Effectiveness: the quality of a description that produces or influences the ruling in a suitable manner.
العلامة (al-alamah): A sign that indicates the ruling but is not the cause; e.g., the shadow indicates the time for prayer but does not cause the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in the passage, which group (ahl al-tard or ahl al-fiqh) requires a description to be effective (mu'aththir) for it to be considered a cause?
A) Ahl al-tard
B) Ahl al-fiqh
C) Both groups
D) Neither group

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a valid cause must be both consistent in its co-occurrence with the ruling and effective (mu'aththir) in a way suitable to the ruling; mere dawaran is insufficient.

Hanafi Critique of Correlation (Dawrān) as Proof of Causality
Critique of correlation as proof of causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi critique of correlation (dawrān) as a method for proving causality in qiyas and distinguish it from the requirement of suitability (munāsaba).

Core Idea
Correlation does not prove causation; only suitability and effect establish a cause in Hanafi qiyas.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut presents the Hanafi critical refinement of the claim that correlation (dawrān) can independently establish causality. The commentator defends the Hanafi position that correlation is insufficient unless combined with suitability (munāsaba) and effect (ta'thīr). By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Hanafis attribute the correlation method to 'ahl al-tard' and how they reject experiential arguments for its sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim about correlation
The opponent argues that correlation alone, as in experiential knowledge, produces certainty of causality. The commentator challenges this by clarifying what correlation actually shows.
The opponent is likely a Shafi'i or non-Hanafi usuli who accepts correlation as a standalone method.
2
Analyze the Hanafi conditional concession
The Hanafi position concedes that correlation may indicate causality if all preventers are absent, but 'preventers' include lack of suitability. Thus correlation reduces to suitability and is not independent.
This is a classic Hanafi move: break down the opponent's condition to show it contains hidden assumptions.
3
Examine the experiential counter-argument and its refutation
The opponent cites that children associate the name of a person with anger, claiming this shows correlation gives certainty. The Hanafi refutation distinguishes between knowing the ruling exists and knowing its cause.
The commentator uses the example of relativity (father-son) to show perfect correlation without causality.
4
Understand the attribution to ahl al-tard
Hanafis assign the correlation method to 'people of rejection' who do not require manifest effect (suitability). True fuqaha require effect, making correlation insufficient.
This attribution is a rhetorical move to marginalize the method within Hanafi usul.
5
Apply the distinction: sign vs. cause
The passage uses the example of dalook (sun declining) as a sign for prayer obligation. While it indicates the ruling, it is not the cause; the cause is something suitable (e.g., time entering). Correlation alone conflates sign with cause.
This clarifies that the discussion is about 'illa haqiqiyya' (true cause), not mere indicators.

فمسلم لكنه غير مانع، وإن أريد معرفة علية المدار فشهادة التجربة ممنوعة والأطفال إنما يقطعون بوجوب الغضب عند هذا الاسم. وأما أن علته ماذا فلا ظن به فضلاً عن القطع وكذا الحال في سائر التجربيات فتأمل . ملازم للحكم في بعض المحال، والبعض مشكوك الحال، فحينئذ يجوز أن تكون الملازمة اتفاقية لأجل مقارنته بعلة في ذلك البعض، فيستلزم الحكم، ولا توجد تلك العلة في الفرع المقصود معرفة حكمه فلا يلزم كون المدار [Page 356] ... and as a method. In other words, if by your statement 'correlation is useful in the absence of a preventer' you mean it is useful when all preventers are absent, it is conceded, but among the preventers is the absence of suitability or the absence of effect, so it must be absent; hence correlation is not independent as a method, but rather refers back to suitability and other things. And if a specific or vague [correlation] is intended, we deny the necessary connection. And [the opponent] rebuts this by saying it impugns experiential matters, for it is known by experience that knowledge is obtained by it alone, for children are certain of it, as stated in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. This is astonishing: the testimony of experience to the attainment of knowledge by it alone absolutely is denied. How can it testify while correlation in relative matters is more complete and stronger, yet it does not indicate causation? And if it testifies to the attainment of knowledge in the absence of a preventer, its testimony is conceded, but you know that among preventers is the lack of effect, so knowledge is only obtained when this lack is removed, so suitability is required. I say: there is reflection in this, so reflect. For if he means the testimony of experience to the existence of the ruling whenever the correlate exists, it is conceded but it does not prevent. If he means to know the causality of the correlate, the testimony of experience is denied; children only conclude the obligation of anger at this name. As to what its cause is, there is no suspicion, let alone certainty. The same applies to other experiential matters. So reflect. Know that the sum of correlation is the existence of the ruling when the correlate exists in other than the branch, and its absence when it is absent in other than it. As for the branch, its case is unknown; therefore it is necessary to prove the causality of the correlate to know it in the branch. Correlation only necessitates that the correlate is concomitant with the ruling in some cases, and that case is of uncertain state. Then it is possible that the concomitance is coincidental due to its association with a cause in that case, so it necessitates the ruling, but that cause does not exist in the branch whose ruling is sought to be known, so it does not necessitate that the correlate is a cause nor that it is concomitant with it. Understand. Then know that the Hanafis attribute correlation to the people of tard (rejection) not to the people of fiqh. By them they mean those who do not require the appearance of effect, which is suitability according to the Shafi'is. By the people of fiqh they mean those who consider effect. On this basis, sifting and consideration also must be attributed to the people of tard. As for one who adds the ruling to something with no suitability at all, he does not exist, so by 'people of tard' he means [something else], as in al-Tahrir. This clarification is good if their expressions can bear it. For if you say: How do you deny the existence of one who adds the ruling to a mere sign like al-dalook (the sun's declining)? He answers: Adding to an indicator that is suitable, and a sign like al-dalook for obligation, is added to the obligation of prayer by agreement, but it is not a cause by consensus, even metaphorically. The discussion is about the true cause.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut is a critical refinement of the matn's claim about correlation (dawrān) as a method for proving causality in qiyas. The commentator begins by analyzing the claim: if by 'correlation is useful in the absence of a preventer' you mean it is useful only when all preventers are absent, that is conceded, but the absence of suitability or effect is itself a preventer, so correlation is not an independent method—it reduces to suitability. The opponent (likely non-Hanafi) argues that experiential knowledge (tajribiyāt) proves correlation alone gives certainty, citing that children associate the name with anger. The commentator denies this: experience only shows the coexistence of ruling and correlate, not causality. Even in relative matters like 'father' and 'son', perfect correlation exists but no causality. Thus, correlation at best shows concomitance, which may be coincidental. The Hanafi position, the commentator explains, attributes the correlation method to 'ahl al-tard'—those who do not require manifest effect (which is suitability according to Shafi'is). True fiqh scholars require effect. Even sifting and consideration (sabr and takhrīj) are attributed to ahl al-tard as commonly understood, but the commentator clarifies that no one actually adds a ruling to something completely unsuitable; the example of adding obligation to the sun's decline (dalook) is actually adding to a suitable indicator, not a mere sign. The passage thus guards against overrating correlation and aligns with Hanafi usul: causation is established by suitability (munāsaba) and effect (ta'thīr), not mere correlation.

Key Terms
دوران (dawrān): Correlation method: observing a ruling's presence and absence with a attribute's presence and absence to infer causality.
مناسبة (munāsaba): Suitability: the attribute's inherent appropriateness for the ruling.
أهل الطرد (ahl al-ṭard): Those who do not require appearance of effect (suitability) in causal reasoning; contrasted with ahl al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Correlation cannot be observed consistently.
B) Correlation might be coincidental due to association with an actual cause, and the attribute may not be the real cause.
C) Hanafis never use correlation in any form.
D) The Quran prohibits using correlation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, correlation without suitability is insufficient to establish causation; the true cause must have an inherent appropriateness for the ruling.

Sabab fi Ma'na al-Illa: Indirect Causation in Hanafi Usul
Defining sabab fi ma'na al-illa with false witness liability example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa', distinguish it from direct causation (mubashara), and apply it to determine liability in cases of indirect causation like false witness testimony.

Core Idea
An indirect cause so strongly linked to harm is treated like direct causation for compensation but not for retaliation.

This passage introduces the key Hanafi usul concept of 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa' — a cause that, though indirect, carries such a strong connection to the resulting harm that it is treated legally as if it were the direct cause for certain liabilities. The author illustrates this with the example of driving an animal that tramples someone, and then applies it to the troubling case of false witnesses whose testimony leads to execution. Understanding this concept is crucial for distinguishing between categories of causation in Hanafi fiqh, especially where qisas (retaliation) applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Term and its Definition
The passage introduces the term 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa'. Identify the Arabic phrase and its literal meaning. Note that it is a cause that, though indirect, is treated like an effective cause (illa) for certain legal consequences.
'Sabab' means cause, 'illa' means effective cause in qiyas.
2
Analyze the Driving Beast Example
The author uses the example of driving a beast that tramples a person. The driving is permissible, but when done without safety, the driver is liable for diyah. Examine how the driver's action is considered a cause in the meaning of effective cause: the trampling is the direct cause, but the driving is attributed to the harm because it set the chain in motion.
The key is that the driver's act is not direct (mubashara) but still leads to liability for compensation.
3
Apply the Concept to False Witnesses
The passage extends the same reasoning to false witnesses. Their testimony leads to the judge's ruling and the guardian's execution. The judge and guardian are not liable because they acted on apparent evidence. Thus, the false witnesses are the cause in the meaning of effective cause, and they owe diyah.
The witnesses' intentional lie (kadhib) strengthens the causal connection.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if the witnesses admit intentional lying, they should be subject to qisas because the cause strengthened with complete intent is like direct causation. Understand the Shafi'i use of istihsan and the concept of hikmat al-zajr (deterrence).
The Hanafi school rejects this because qisas requires direct equivalence in action (mumathala), not just in result.
5
Derive the Usul Principle
From this analysis, derive the Hanafi usul principle: The classification of a cause as 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa' triggers liability for diyah but not for qisas or kaffarah. The distinction rests on the nature of the causal link and whether intervening agents (judge, guardian) have free will.
This principle is used in many fiqh chapters, especially in jinayat (homicide) and testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب ما هو الأصل الرابع/ القياس كسوق الدابة فوطئت آدمياً فهو ) أي السوق (لم يؤثر في التلف وإنما أثر فيه الوطء وهو مضاف إليه لكن لوطئها الذي هو العلة (إضافة إليه) فيكون سبباً في معنى العلة، فإن قلت: كيف يكون التلف الحرام سبباً منه وكيف يكون هو بمنزلته مع أنه مباح . قال : والسوق بشرط السلامة هو المشروع) لا المطلق بأي طريق كان فتجب الدية لكونه مباشراً لما هو في معنى العلة، فوجد التعدي منه في إتلاف نفس معصومة، فيجب ضمانه (لأجزاء المباشرة) أي لا جزاء المباشرة أي الفعل كالحرمان من الميراث إن كان المتلف قريباً (ونحوه) من القصاص والكفارة إلا استحباباً واحتياطاً ومنه الشهادة وهي على القتل العمد العدوان سبب للقصاص فإنها مؤدية إليه بواسطة إيجابه القضاء وتمكن الولي فتكون سبباً لتخلل العلة، لكنه ليس القضاء صالحاً لإضافة التلف الحرام إليه، فإنه مجبور شرعاً، ولا فعل الولي، لأنه اعتمد الحجة، فلم يبق إلا الشهود إذا كانوا كاذبين، فهي سبب في معنى العلة فعليهم الدية إذا (رجعوا لأنهم أتلفوا نفساً معصومة فيجب جزاء محل التلف (لا) القصاص) أي لا يجب القصاص (لأنه جزاء المباشرة والفعل ولم يوجد منهم وعند الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى (يقتص إذا قالوا : تعمدنا الكذب لقتله فأما إذا قالوا : أخطأنا وولي المقتول يدعي التعمد يحلف، فإن حلف يقضي بالدية المغلظة في ماله والشافعي رضي الله عنه إنما حكم بهذا مع أنه لا ينازعنا في هذا الأصل ( لأن السبب المؤكد بالقصد الكامل كالمباشرة حكماً، وكيف لا يكون كالمباشرة، وإنما شرع القصاص فيها لحكمة الزجر لينزجر الناس عن القتل وإذا لم يعد هذا النحو من التسبيب مباشرة حتى لا يحكم بالقصاص فأتت حكمة الزجر ويفتح باب شيوع القتل بهذا الوجه بخلاف وضع الحجر في الطريق) لأنه لم يتأكد بالقصد الكامل (ودفع) قول الشافعي رضي الله عنه بأن القصاص بالمماثلة كما قال تعالى: ﴿فَاعْتَدُوا عَلَيْهِ بِمِثْلِ مَا اعْتَدَى عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٩٤] ولا مماثلة بين المباشر والتسبيب وإن تأكد بالقصد الكامل، وله أن يقول : المعتبر المماثلة في المقصود وهو يحصل من الفعلين على السواء بأكمل الوجوه، ولعل ما قال الشافعي رضي الله عنه استحسان فافهم
It is necessary what is the fourth principle / analogy (qiyas) like driving a beast that tramples a human. So it (i.e., the driving) did not affect the destruction, rather the trampling affected it and it is attributed to him, but due to the trampling which is the cause (attributed to him), it becomes a cause in the meaning of the effective cause. If you say: How can the forbidden destruction be a cause from him, and how can it be like it when it is permissible? He said: Driving with the condition of safety is what is legislated, not absolutely in any way. So the blood money is obligatory because he is a direct causer of what is in the meaning of the effective cause, so transgression from him in destroying a protected life is found, so his guarantee is obligatory due to parts of direct causation, i.e., not the penalty of direct causation, i.e., the act, like deprivation of inheritance if the destroyer is a relative (and similar) from qisas and kaffarah, except as recommended and precautionary. From that is testimony, which in intentional aggressive murder is a cause for qisas, because it leads to it through the intermediary of the judge's ruling and the ability of the guardian, so it becomes a cause allowing the effective cause. But the judge's ruling is not suitable for attributing the forbidden destruction to it, for it is compelled by law, nor the guardian's act, because he relied on evidence. So nothing remains except the witnesses if they are false, for they are a cause in the meaning of the effective cause. So upon them is the blood money if they retract because they destroyed a protected life, so the penalty for the place of destruction is obligatory (not qisas), i.e., qisas is not obligatory because it is the penalty of direct causation and action, and it is not found from them. And according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), he imposes qisas if they say: We intentionally lied to kill him. As for if they say: We erred, and the guardian of the slain claims intentionality, he swears; if he swears, he rules for aggravated blood money from his wealth. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) only judged thus, even though he does not dispute us in this principle, because the cause strengthened with complete intent is like direct causation in legal ruling. How can it not be like direct causation? Qisas was only legislated for it for the wisdom of deterrence to deter people from killing. If this type of causation is not considered direct causation so that qisas is not ruled, then the wisdom of deterrence is nullified and the door of spreading killing through this means is opened, unlike placing a stone on the road because it is not strengthened with complete intent. (And refuting) al-Shafi'i's statement that qisas is based on equivalence as Allah said: "So transgress against him in the like of what he transgressed against you" [al-Baqarah 194] and there is no equivalence between direct causation and causation even if strengthened with complete intent. And he could say: What matters is equivalence in the intended result, and it is achieved from both actions equally in the most complete ways. Perhaps what al-Shafi'i said is istihsan. Understand.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa' (cause in the meaning of effective cause) within the discussion of qiyas. The author uses the example of driving a beast that tramples a person. The act of driving is permissible, but when done without the condition of safety, it leads to harm. The trampling is the direct cause of death, but the driving is considered a cause in the meaning of effective cause because the harm is attributed to the driver due to his action. Thus, the driver is liable for blood money (diyah) because his act is like direct causation (mubashara) in terms of liability. The passage then applies this principle to false witnesses (shuhood kazibeen). When false witnesses testify in a murder case, the judge rules based on their testimony, and the guardian executes the qisas. The witnesses are the cause of the death through the intermediary of the judge's ruling. The judge's ruling is compelled (majbur) and the guardian acts on evidence, so only the witnesses remain as the cause. Therefore, they are considered 'sabab fi ma'na al-illa' and are liable for blood money if they retract. Qisas is not required because it is a penalty for direct action, which they did not perform. The passage then presents the Shafi'i view: if the witnesses admit intentional lying, qisas is obligatory because the cause strengthened with complete intent is like direct causation. The Hanafi response argues that there is no equivalence (mumathala) between direct causation and causation for qisas. The verse on equivalent retaliation (al-Baqarah 194) requires direct equivalence in the act, and causing through testimony is not identical to committing murder. The author suggests that al-Shafi'i's ruling may be based on istihsan (juristic preference), which is a recognized source in the Hanafi school but used differently.

Key Terms
السبب في معنى العلة (al-sabab fi ma'na al-illa): An indirect cause that is so strongly connected to the effect that it is treated legally like the direct effective cause (illa) for liability
المباشرة (al-mubashara): Direct causation of an act without intermediary
التسبيب (al-tasbib): Indirect causation where the cause sets a chain leading to the effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the liability of false witnesses who retract after their testimony led to execution?
A) They are liable for qisas because their intentional lie caused death
B) They are liable for diyah (blood money) only, not qisas
C) They are not liable at all because the judge and guardian are the direct agents
D) They are liable for both diyah and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
False witnesses who intentionally cause execution are liable for diyah, not qisas, because qisas requires direct participation in the killing.

Real Cause and Indication: When Indication Does Not Create Liability
Defining real cause and indication with exceptions for specific undertakings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real cause (sabab haqiqi) and a direct cause (mubashir) in Hanafi law, and apply the rule that an indicator is not liable unless he has a specific undertaking.

Core Idea
A pure cause (sabab mahd) without direct intervention does not incur liability; only a specific undertaking or direct act does.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains the Hanafi usul principle that a 'real cause' (sabab haqiqi) is one that does not directly produce the effect; an independent free agent intervenes. The key example: indicating property to a thief. The indicator is not liable because the thief's choice breaks causation. However, there are exceptions: the depositary (mudi') and the pilgrim in ihram (muhrim) are liable because they had a specific duty. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying causation in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction
The passage distinguishes between a cause attributed to the actor (in the meaning of effective cause) and a real cause not attributed. Identify the criterion: attribution of the effect to the cause.
2
Analyze the example of indication
Work through the example of indicating property to a thief. Why is the indicator not liable? Because the thief's free choice is the direct cause.
3
Examine the exceptions
The depositary (mudi') and muhrim (pilgrim) are liable. Why? They had a specific undertaking (iltizam) to safeguard or not to harm. Their indication is a breach of that duty, making them direct causers.
4
Understand the objection and response
An objection suggests that every Muslim undertakes by Islam not to harm. The response: Islam's undertaking is general, not specific; it creates sin but not financial liability.
5
Apply the rule
Now apply: In a scenario, determine whether the indicator is liable based on whether he has a specific undertaking.

Source Text
